You can say little more to Dyson than “ Wel-
come I’’ His merits are obvious ; his defects, in
comparison, are nob. Though he flies neither
▼ery high nor on a very strong pinion, his work is
generally pleasant and sometimes impressive.
And Dyson takes pains to do his best. Selecting
from memory some incident, some scene, pathetic
or humorous, he tells its story or paints its picture
in well-pondered measure, with the aid of vivid
epithets or graphic vernacular. The field he
treads has hardly been skirted by earlier bards,
who seem merely interested spectators of the
miner at his work. Dyson, once miner himself,
knows his subject to the foot of the letter. His
area of experience, indeed, is limited to the
methods of a rather primitive sluicing and reefing
in Victoria ; but mining operations are generically
the same everywhere, and the manager of the most
modern winding-plant can thrill to the pioture of
“The Old Whim Horse” whom some strange
memory impels to plod round the whim-path of
the long-deserted mine—
He hears in the sluices the water rushing
As the buckets drain and the doors fall baok:
When the early dawn in the east is blushing,
He is limping still round the old, old traok.
Now he pricks his ears, with a neigh replying
To a call unspoken, with eyes aglow,
And he sways and sinks in the cirole, dying ;
Prom the ring no more will the grey horse go.
And even a modern cyanider can enjoy the de-
scription of Finn’s far-back mill—
All the heads are different sires, and the horses
Are so crazy that the whole caboodle rocks,
And eaoh time a stamper thunders down it forces
Little spirtings through the crannies in the box.
then the feed-pipe's mostly plugged and aggravating,
And the pump it suffers badly from a cough ;
Every hour or so they burst a blooming grating,
And the shoes are nearly always coming off.
She goes biff, puff, bang, bump, olitter-clatter, smash,
And she rattles on for halt a shift, and lets up with a crash ;
Then silence reigns a little while, and all the land is still
While they’re tinkering awkward patches on the tin-pot
mill.
, * » *
Dyson’s youthful lot was cast amid a fine com-
munity of Austral Cornishmen, and some of his
best pictures in prose and verse are made of the
lights and shadows of the Cornish character. The
devout Brother Peetree made his bow in The
Bulletin not long ago—
Brother Peetree always Started with a murmured sup-
plication,
Knelt beside a form, serenely, with a meek, submissive
face;
But he rose by certain stages to a rolling exhortation,
And a wild, ecstatic bellowing for sanotity and grace;
And he threw his arms to Heaven, and the seats went
down before him
As he fought his way along the aisle, and prayed with
might and main,
With hysterical beseeohings. Then a sudden peace fell
o’er him,
And he finished, sobbing softly, at his starting-point
again.
And the elders, to their ears pale with reverential fears,
And the sisters and the ohoir indulged in hot, repentant
tears ;
And the sinners for salvation did with eagerness declare,
When beloved Brother Peetree wrestled mightily in
prayer.
“ The Trucker,” one of Dyson’s early contribu-
tions to The Bulletin, remains among the
strongest pieces in the new book. There are
some good lines in “ The Prospectors,” now first
printed :
As the soft breeze stirs all ths tender green of the bush
that is newly born,
And the wattles blaze on the flats and gladden the hills
with the glow of morn,
We are trenching high in the stony slopes, or turning the
creeks below,
Or the gorge re-echoes the thud of picks and the songs
that the miners know.
When the lode strips clean with a yellow sheen our for-
tunes are fairly won ;
When the dish pans bare, up with tent and ware, and
hurrah ! for the outward run.
• • * •
The pleasant task of tasting may end with a
stanza or two from “The Worked-Out Mine”—
The engine-house is dark and still,
The life that raged within has fled ;
Like open graves the boilers chill
That once with glowing fires were red;
Above the shaft in measured pace
A rotted rope swings to and fro,
Whilst o’er the plat and on the braoe
The silent shadows come and go.
Ah 1 woeful mine, where wives have wept,
And mothers prayed in anxious pain,
And long, distracting vigil kept,
You yawn for victims now in vain !
Still to that god, whose shrine you were.
Is homage done in wild device;
Men hate you as the sepulchre
That stores their bloody sacrifice.
+.
Really, as Jeffrey said (we hdpe it was Jeffrey),
“ this will never do.” Mr. Gay grows too
irritating. Mr. Gay is a Victorian writer of
verses. About a year ago he published some
of his verses—sonnets. Simultaneously a flock
of paragraphs rose in the air and settled on
the newspapers, reciting how Mr. Gay was a poor
and hopeless invalid on a sick-bed, who, while
rapidly proceeding from Gay to grave, solaced his
dying hours by dictating poems to his nurse.
Therefore, buy Gay’s poems, price two bob.
• * * *
A particularly harrowing paragraph, as Mr.
Gay admits, settled on The Bulletin, and was
duly printed in black ink and sympathy. But
objection was made at the time to the fashion of
ad misericordiam pleas in literature. Mr. Gay, it
was declared, should keep his deathbed out of his
poetry : the Muse must walk on her own legs, not
on charitable crutches. And Mr. Gay protested
that he had been misunderstood.
♦ * * *
Well, nearly a year has passed, and The Bul-
letin is glad to hear that Mr. Gay’s funeral
seems as far off as ever. If he is dying, he is
only dying as fast as the rest of us. We are all
shorn by the same scythe, and there really seems
no reason why any particular little blossom
should discount its obituary praises in advance.
* * * •
Anyhow, nearly a year has passed, and Mr.
Gay bobs up again with another little book of
verses—price half-a-orown this time. And, sure
enough, here is the old flock of deathbed advts.
back again. One of them struck The Bulletin,
but this time The Bulletin, in the charming
sporting phr«», “wwn jjJJ?’',* iSStoliii in
BS. S ; d^"" ,JW
hid in the point 'r»ro
under the circumstances w , P who m i g ht
can forget the death of poor ' ’ , i Before
still have been alive had ™?*,?’
instead of immediately after hi* unhappy en •
Now, Air. Gay. will doubtless repudiate respon-
sibility for this kind °f thmg , ! t
not silence his claque? And why does he set
literary teeth on edge by offering his new volume
to a host of bet hanked patrons, as thus—
During twelve years in Austrujasi 1 1 av^, r ,f c^l^:f U de I
kindness from many friends. To lhe *‘-^! h fhtir , ames .
dedicate this little boo-, and uive Ar ten Mr
Hon. J. H. Abbott, Miss K. C. Arden, Mras M.Ar.len, Mr.
and Mrs. A. Bayne, Miss Bayne, Mr. D. W. Bayne
and so on with a list of about 100 people who
have presumably sent flattering letters or bananas.
“ Gratitude by all means ; but why don t men
do these things in private? What is there m
literature more hateful than the advt. ot
cheap literary Maecenas?
• * * *
And so it goes on, with a beautiful unan-
imity of back - scratclu is. Drunton Stephens
says Gay’s sonnets are “ worthy to be pre-
served among the treasures of the language :
Gay retorts by another sonnet which calls
B.S. “eldest and first of Austral singers,
and claims kinship (1) because they are both
poets—more or less ; (2) they are both Scotch
—entirely ; (3) they are both going to die sooner
or later. The Misses M. E. and H. A. Sampson
are thanked for “much kindness,” and there is a
sonnet to Miss A. M. Sampson (deceased). Again
—by all means; but why advertise the names ?
Mr. Gay has plenty of literary instinct: why will
he not avoid even the appearance of bartering
the Muse’s favors for woollen comforters ?
* * • *
Some people say it is in bad taste to write like
this; but in how much worse taste is it to give
reason for writing like this ? One’s pen moves
unwillingly ; but more duty is owed to Literature
than to the litterateur. And Mr. Gay is quite
good enough poet to rely on public appreciation,
without permitting his friends to clamor for
public charity. “ Christ on Olympus,” indeed,
is a mere mouthing of sonorities—a nothing ; but
one or two of the accompanying sonnets are
fair (most have the expression of a man who
is walking with his wife); and two or three of the
miscellaneous verses are rather better in a level,
monotonous manner, easily pardoned to a weak-
pulsed mger. “ The War of the Ghosts ” suggests
Stevenson, but the thought is well handled ; and
—here is another rendering of the Love and Death
idea. [The versions run now : G. F. Watts —
Love friend; Death enemy. Syd. Jepheott:
Love and Death, both friends. Wm. Gay : Love
enemy, Death friend. Room for one more bard
or artist with : Love and Death, both enemies.]
There is a pretty set of figures called “ A Sea
Maiden,” whereof one verse—
Her breath in sweets doth more abound
Than fragrant equatorial gales,
Her voice is pleasant as the sound
Of rising winds in idle sails—
may be quoted for the pleasure of disagreeing
with Melb. Abgus dictum that “gales” ruins the
simile. “Only a very boisterous breath indeed
should be brought into comparison with a gale.’’
But there is no violence about the poetic gale •
when Burns wrote of lovers breathing their tender
tale “beneath the milk-white thorn that scents
the evening gale,” he wasn’t referring to a hurri-
cane; and in Mr. Gay’s verse the idea of frag-
rance is paramount.
4.
Norman Gale is only a minor poet, but there is
a fresh, open-air fragrance about his milkmaids
and buttercups often more satisfying to the soul
than the psychological balancings of better brains.
He is fine and physical, a Pan-worshipper who
woos naiads in brooks and nymphs on haycocks—
sweats in the sunshine and sings when the
harvest-moon glows red over the trees. His
high spirits are catching and tonicky. Lately he
has issued a pretty book of children’s verses, full
of dainty line illustrations which admirably match
the print. Author is much indebted to artist, yet
artist s name is not mentioned for praising. Some
examples of the skill of both will surely be wel-
Australian books post-free from The BULLITO
office for stamps or postal notes of any provinoe:—
“Banjo’s” poems, ss. 4d. ; Lawson’s poems, 6s. 6d.;
Lawson’s prose stories and sketches, 6s. 6d. ; Beoke’l
“By Reef and Palm,” Is. 9d. and 2s. 3d.; Beoke's
“Ebbing of the Tide,’’ 3s. and 4s. ; Louise Mack's “The
World is Round, 9d. and Is. 3d. ; Jephcott’s Poem 3, 3s.
9d. ; Essex Evans's, 3s. 9d. ; Francis Keuna’s, 2s. 9d.;
Ethel Turner’s “ Seven Little Australians,” “ Family at
Misrule,” “ Story of a Baby,” “Little Larrikin," 3s. eaoh.
Edward Dyson’s “ Rhymes from the Mines and
Other Lines,” mostly reprinted from The Bulle-
tin, are published by Angus and Robertson in a
form similar to that of their editions of Paterson
and Lawson. The result, however, is less satis-
factory. Dyson’s book is so dirtily and clumsily
printed that it pains the eye. It is time, too, to
protest against the fashion of padding books with
advertising sheets, no matter on what bad British
precedent founded. One wants one’s poetry, at all
events, “unmixed with baser matter.”

0, Love for long did fly from me,
And I did flee from Death—
“ O Love, I burn, I die for thee! **
I cried with anguished breath.
“ O Death, I fear thy dark pursuit I
O Love, I burn, I die !
O Death, withhold thy evil foot!
O Love, no longer fly ! ”
Then Love no longer fled, but turned
And stabbed me through and through ;
And Death, whom I with fear had spurned,
My pains did all Bubdue*

TIM’S GRACE.
When Baby Tim, who's very small,
Says grace for me, and Nurse, and Paul,
He asks the Lord to make us all
* Ter-looly fankful.’
And if we laugh till we are red,
Nurse strokes his sandy-colored head,
And loves him more because he said
1 Ter-looly fankful.’
For when he’s older, Nursie says,
And grown from all his pretty ways,
She’ll often miss his funny phrase,
‘ Ter-looly fankful.’
CRADLE SONG.
Bees are resting sugary thighs, i
Stars awake in the evening skies,
Timothy, Timothy, close your eyes,
King of the cradle, sleep.
Sleep, my honey ; O sleep, my star,
Dream where the rainbow ribbons are,
Ride with the Queen in the Fairies’ oat,
King of the cradle, sleep.
Father is tossing upon the sea,
Timothy rocks at home with me ;
Weary of trumpet, cannon, and knee.
King of the cradle, sleep.
God, whose babes are many and far,
Keep him from craft, and save from war ;
Give to my rose from a golden star
Honey and innocent sleep.

SPECIAL DISEASES.
He has, by diligent research, investigated their

man y years, obtained the highest position in his
profession. The various diseases embraced in his
speciality are those which have hitherto caused
more misery and suffering than any other kind of
trouble that affects the human frame.
Young Men
who suffer from any of the following symptoms,
namely : —Frequent headaches, giddiness, drowsi-
ness in the daytime, irregularity of the bowels,
feeling of fullness after eating, specks before the
eyes, halos or circles round a candle, cold hands
and feet, pains in the back or under the shoulder-
blade, tenderness of the breast-bone on pressure,
palpitation of the heart, clammy hands, an
aversion to society, a fear of something dreadful
about to happen, impure blood, spots or
pimples on the back, chest, legs, or face, failing
memory, hacking cough, falling of the hair, ftc.,
&c., should consult him, for he is now enabled
quickly and effectually, to remove the cause, and
so prevent life long misery.

St. John’s Works, W.C.. LONDON.
The terrors of overlanding ! A mob of
550 cattle from Warenda, trying to reach the Ade-
laide market by way of Herrgott and Birds ville,
lost 470 of the total number from thirst. No won-
der Western pastoralists are prepared to bore
‘‘half-way to hell ” to strike water.
One W.A. publican has taken down, re-
moved, and rebuilt his wood-and-iron hotel eight
times, as he persistently followed up the rush that
keeps moving further and further out. A fortune
awaits the man who can invent a portable stump-
jumping pub. for use on the goldfields.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
Apply Sulpholine Lotion. It drives away

figurements. Sulpholine developes a lovely skin.
Is. bottles. Made In London.*
A Mackay quandary. The local quod is
both prison and lock-up. If you get into the
local limbo in its capacity as prison you can only
be hauled therefrom on a Judge’s order. If you
get into the same building as lock-up the police
can bring you into court at any time. The other
day a Kanaka was sentenced to a month in prison
and remanded on another charge. Once in prison
it required a Judge’s order to get him out to be
tried on the second charge. Eventually the
magistrates had to reconsider the sentence and
amend it to a fortnight’s lock-up so that they
could lay hands on him on the second charge.
“ While the Billy Boils,” by Henry Law-
son, with eight plates by F. P. Mahony, is already
in its fourth thousand, and may be had from Angus
and Robertson, Sydney. Post free 5/6. *
Victoria’s one really humorous publication
is the Govt. Gazette. A recent issue contained
an announcement that, in future, the sweetbriar
would be a thistle ; and a police description of a
“ wanted ” man : “ About 25 years of age, medium
height and build, common, ordinary features, fair
moustache, dark clothes.” “Common, ordinary
features ” is a stroke of genius.

THE GABO LIGHT.
Have you been round the Gabo Light
On a night
. That’s bright,
With a sky full of stars ?
On a coasting-boat with a boiler so thin
a 23 could let ? ut tbe steam with the prick of a pin ;
And a screw that goes with a whirr
With a stir,
A gurr,
And a series of jars
lhat jolt out the bolts that are holding her frame
-from the bellowing waves and Newspaper Fame.
Have you been round the Gabo light
When the white
_ Seas fight
On the circlet of rocks ?
wt en e ?? aLs roll round in * h e hold of the boat;
When the Demon of Fear has his clutch on your
throat; J
When the engine that’s put dead slow
’Gainst the flow
And -blow
w? lrSt -j ambs > struggles, then locks ;
When women turn pale to their tightly-latched
bps
At perils which compass colonial ships.
Have you been round the Gabo Light
When its sight
Ts quite
A horror to your eyes ?
When the thud of the waves and the vessel’s kick
Combmed, make you most indisputably sick ’
W hen the screw turns with a wheezy,
Dumpetty
Dee-dee,
-From moon-set to moon-rise ;
When you think of the horrors which you have
endured,
Of the wife and—the money for which you’re
insured ?
Hawthorn.
HUGH W.R. MICRAE.
?
Dead lips (cold clings the clay—
Heavy the mould),
Doth sting of kisses stay—
Kisses twice-told ?
Gold hair (the grave will turn
Gold into dross),
Did stain of tears that burn
Pass with thy gloss ?
Closed eyes (each white lid bars
Blackness supreme),
In those once-shining stars
Lingers a gleam ?
Canst sleep, sweet heart that thrilled
(Still is the tomb),
Swift as love’s light touch willed—
Sleep through the gloom ?
Sydney.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion — i,e., any picture which has to be re-draum—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 2, 1897.
A Small Side Issue.
Despite the fact that the Federation pro-
blem has been discussed more or less con-
tinuously all over these provinces for the last
six years there is one strictly practical aspect
of it which, so far as The Bulletin is aware,
has not yet been even mentioned. There
has been much poetry shed on the question,
and much straggling eloquence, and much
high-flown imagery about Australia’s “ des-
tiny ’’—whatever a “destiny” may be—and
many other things ; but the purely business-
like side of the case has been considerably
neglected. Therefore the Federal delegates
are likely to go to the Convention with no
definite instructions on certain points about
which their instructions should be very
definite indeed. Most of these points relate
to finance, which is the weak side of all
Australasian Governments. Federation im-
plies, among other things, the establishment
of a central authority which will take over
and consolidate the public debts of the
several provinces, and convert them, as
opportunity offers, into one stock at a uni-
form low rate of interest, with one central
authority in London to manage the business.
The provincial Legislatures will be relieved,
in one act, of their great financial incubus’
which will be shifted on to the broader
shoulders and, it is to be hoped, the more
comprehensive brain, of the Commonwealth
Government. And the question which no
one seems to have asked as yet is whether
the provincial authorities will then be allowed
to commence piling up a new debt apiece to
replace the one they have lost, or whether
the power of borrowing money shall be
absolutely and permanently taken from
them. The Bulletin is of opinion,
for many good reasons, that it should be
taken from them. The general idea, judging
from the fact that no one has yet raised the
question, seems to be that the blessed privi-
lege of running in debt and accumulating
future insolvency remains as a matter of
course.
This paper’s contention is, and always has
been, that borrowing, if logically permissible
at all, is permissible only for the construction
of works which yield a direct revenue to pay,
or partly pay, the interest on loans. Bor-
rowing for roads, bridges, lock-ups, guns,
ammunition, gaols, lunatic asylums, statues,
public offices, art-galleries, museums, dredg-
ing-works and the like is pure, unmitigated
fraud ; it is a device to avoid the necessary
taxation or the necessary retrenchment in
order to make ends meet —a scheme by which
unscrupulous and incompetent Treasurers
get credit for bogus surpluses and keep up
an appearance of sham prosperity, and
thereby disguise their own incapacity.
None of these things produce revenue, or
are really assets in any fair sense of the
term. The roads require to be kept in re-
pair ; the bridges not only need to be kept
in repair but need periodical replacement ;
so do the lock-ups, gaols, and miscellaneous
buildings ; the dredging that is done to-day
needs shortly to be done over again to keep
pace with the continuous process of silting-
up. None of these things are assets, because
they are not saleable ; in fact, they are
liabilities because they involve a continuous
outlay for maintenance and supervision.
And as every year there are old struc-
tures wearing out which have to be re-
placed by new ones, the bridge and
lock-up and gaol and public office and other
items are as distinctly part of the regular
annual expenditure as the salaries of the
Civil Service. The theory that a bridge is a
permanent work which has only to be paid
for once and is done with, and which may,
therefore, be fairly charged to posterity, is
the thinnest fraud imaginable. It will be
necessary to go on building bridges at the
present rate for a century or so before the
country is sufficiently opened up, and long
before the work is finished the old structures
will want rebuilding, and before that is
finished, in another century or so, another
general rebuilding will be due, and so on ad
infinitum,.
* * * *
Almost the only works of any size which
can fairly be charged to loans are railways
and telegraphs. These will be taken over,
along with the existing debts, by the Federal
Government, and it will look after future
extensions, therefore it will also look after
any future borrowing for extension purposes.
If war or invasion should come this way, and
it be absolutely necessary to raise money for
purposes of bloodshed, that matter will also
belong to the Federal Government. There-
fore, almost the only purpose for which the
Provincial Governments can want to borrow
money is the old swindle of making a bogus
surplus by charging current expenses to
loans. They will have practically no repro-
ductive works to invest borrowed money in,
therefore they have no honest reason or ex-
cuse for borrowing at all, and there is no
visible reason why they should have any
borrowing powers whatever. The division of
power between the federal and provincial
authorities offers the bestpossible opportunity
to draw a sharp line between legitimate and
illegitimate loan expenditure. The federal
authority won’t have charge of roads and
bridges, therefore it can’t fool away loan
moneys on these prolific sources of swindle,
even if it would. The provincial authorities
will have charge only of practically unpro-
ductive works, which should not be charged
to loans, therefore it has no honest necessity
for any powers of loan-mongering whatso-
ever.
* * * *
If the Provincial Governments retain their
present borrowing powers they will almost
inevitably start to accumulate new debts as
soon as they are relieved of the old ones.
Therefore, the first result of Federation will
be that there will be seven debts instead of
six, or six debts instead of five, as at pre-
sent, which is hardly the result that Austra-
lian unity was intended to produce. The
new provincial debts, not being backed by
the Federal credit, will not participate in the
general reduction of interest, consequently
this advantage will be partly nullified. The
Federal legislature will attract the best in-
tellects in Australian politics, and the men
who muddle the provincial debts will pro-
bably be of an even lower grade than
heretofore and considering the results
which past financiers have produced, this is
a truly awful consideration ; therefore, there
is all the more reason why their powers of
cooking the public accounts should be limited.
If Australia is ever going to straighten up
and try to live honestly, and be reasonably
independent and self-reliant, and if its Trea-
surers are ever going to rise above the level
of fraudulent bankers and perpetrators of
bogus balance-sheets, this is evidently the
time and the opportunity. Otherwise the
future history of Australia will probably be
one long record of borrowing by the Com-
monwealth Government, more borrowing by
the Provincial Governments, provincial in-
trigues to get the new local debts taken over
by the Commonwealth when they grow too
burdensome, and similar troubles. The
shortest way out of the difficulty is by the
insertion of a clause in the Constitution to
the eftect that the Provincial Governments
shall have no borrowing powers whatever.
And if this arrangement cannot be anived
at, the next best thing is to enact that no
future provincial debts contracted by any of
its members shall be taken over by the Com-
monwealth under any circumstances what-
Then there will be no surreptitious
shifting on to the nation at, large of local
road and bridge expenditure incurred with-
out any national authority; and any province
which overruns the constable can default
peacefully on its own without serious damage
to the Federal credit; and any province
which proposes to swear off - and live cleanly
in the future can abolish local borrowing by
enactment of its own Legislature, and secure
for itself the advantages of its own economy.
The question at issue is a small matter just
now. and one easily dealt with, but it wi
probably be a very large one by-and-bye it
decisive measures are not taken.
The Horrors of Protection.
The Sydney Telegraph man who writes the
weekly article on the Horrors of Protection in
Victoria has of late broken out very badly
indeed, and is making an awesome show of him-
self with great masses of his own manufactured
statistics. The said statistics are so wildly
absurd that it is impossible to suppose they
deceive anybody, even the writer, but they are
interesting as samples of importers’ logic. I he
following is a fine instance :
DIRECT COST OF CUSTOMS DUTIES
(Except Duties on Intoxicants and Narcotics).
Government revenue ... ... ••• £ 999 > 999
Gross profits of trades thereon ... ... 540,000
Increased price charged by protected manu-
facturers ... ... **• ••• 2,600,000
Importers’ gross profit on one-half thereof ... 260,000
Retailers’ gross profit on two-thirds thereof ... 664,000
Total cost ... ... ••• £4,964,000
The direct cost of protection to Victoria, therefore, is
£4,964,000 a year, and of this enormous sum only £900,000
is reaped by the Government, while over £4,000,000 goes
into the pockets of private people, or is as much wasted
as if it were used to pay men for digging holes and filling
them up again. On an average, therefore, every Vic-
torian man, woman, and child pays £4 4s. a year in purely
injurious Customs duties, or every father of a family—
—counting a family as six persons—pays on an average
£25 a year to uphold the protective system.
* * * *
It thus appears that the traders pay £900,000
in Customs duties, and that they charge this to
the people along with £540,000 direct profit
thereon, to say nothing of £924,000 more profit
alluded to further down the list. As the trader
charges his profit simply on the cost of the
goods without counting which part of it is
original cost or freight or duty, it would thus
seem that the Victorian importer is in the habit
of making about 60 per shent profit all round.
This will be news indeed to a good many of the
fraternity in these hard times. Further, the
levying by the Government of £900,000 duty on
imports enables the local manufacturer to add
three times as much to his prices, and yet he
can sell as cheaply as the importer—-he must
do so, else he wouldn’t exist. In other
words, if a foreign article costs 2d. per lb. and
Id. duty makes it 3d., the manufacturer adds
more than 3d. to his prices as the natural con-
sequence, and still can sell his goods at 3d. to
keep pace with the importer; consequently, if
there was no Protection, he would be able to
sell his goods at less than nothing per lb. ; in
other words, he would be able to supply them
gratis, and pay people to take them away.
And then, having alleged that the people of
Victoria pay to themselves this stupendous
four millions odd, so that they still have it, and
the country still has it, the “ Horrors ” man
goes on to point out that it is all “ lost.” Also,
as a side issue, he charges £260,000 for
“ importers’ profit ” on goods that are not
imported, and so can’t pay any importers’
profit whatever.
* * * •
Then the “ Horrors ” person proceeds to deal
with industries in general. In every case he
brings out the same result—that there is so
much dead loss through Protection that it
would pay the province better to shut up the
industry altogether, and pension the workers
for life at their present wages, and import the
goods bodily. And when he has proved this in
every case the final result is that it would be
cheaper if all Vic. industries were shut up, and
all goods were imported, and nobody was doing
anything to earn money to pay for them, and
everybody was pensioned off out of a revenue
which wouldn’t exist because nobody was
earning any cash to pay taxes or duties with.
The following shriek about the wax-match
industry is a fair sample :
The clearest and most convincing proof of the loss
which protection inflicts upon the people is furnished by
the novel industry of making wax vestas in Victoria. A
duty of Is. per gross boxes was imposed on wax vestas
Taking advantage of this duty, the London firm of R
Bell and Co. established a factory for their production in
Melbourne in the year 1895. And this is the triumphant
result The revenue collected from wax vestas during
the four years preceding the establishment of the factory
—i.e., 1891 to 1894—averaged £14,089 per year. The
factory started operations during the first half of 1895
and the revenue collected during the succeeding half-years
was as follows J
Tn no qo Bi *' nonths ending Revenue.
December 31, 1895 ... %(ua
June 30, 1896
Moreover, there is every reason to expect that the duty
on matches will yield no revenue next year. Disregarding
and merely taking the present position
to be that the revenue of 1896 will be twice that for the
first six months of the year -viz., £3B74—there is here a
loss of revenue of £10,215. This sum, paid by the people
is now transferred to Messrs. Bell and Co p ’
->-Jonn° UfcpUt ’ acc °rding to Inspector Martin’s return, is
115,200 gross ; and as the factory charges 3d less per
gross boxes than imported matches plus duty are sold
for, the public gams the difference— viz., £1440 a year
The account, therefore, stands thus— ‘ V a '

It thus appears that because the people don’t
tax themselves £10,215 per annum therefore
they lose that amount; in fact, they lose it
because they don’t have to pay it. And when
the amount they receive is deducted from the
amount they don’t pay away, the difference is
the total loss. And the fact that Victoria now
exports considerable quantities of matches to
N.S.W., where they are sold as cheaply as
similar matches from England, proves no
doubt that the Victorian article is much de’arer
and the alleged existence of the alleged dear-
and S sn r< th eS th l?at Protection is the cause of it,
and so the thing goes round in a circle.
* * * *
On, the soap and candle question the “ Hor-
rors individual is equally mixed. He says •
wit^tutS 8 ’ h V pl ° ying ’ in 1893 > ‘he year
cost of the industry to the people of Victoria is approxi-
mately as follows :
DUTY COLLECTED.
On Candles ... ... •••
On soda crystals ... •" £7

on, 33J per cent. •••

62,780 cwt. candles, at lid. per lb. ...

17,632 cwt. soda crystals, at Is. per cwt.
Dealers’ gross profits thereon, 25 per cent ... 36,071
Gross total .. •• £1 89 ) 8 U
The loss to the people, after deducting the revenue re-
ceived by the Government, is, however, £182,737. Each
operative therefore costs the people over £414 a year,
whereas his wages are onlv £65 a year. If they all were
pensioned off for life at full wages, the annual saving
would still amount to £155,827.
This would be every interesting but for seven
or eight different troubles. Victorian soap and
candles are not dearer as alleged ; the fact that
Victoria now supplies N.S. W. with about as
much soap as England does shows that it some-
how contrives to manufacture as cheaply under
Protection as Britain does under Freetrade.
And even if the figures were correct they only
show that the Victorians pay themselves
,£lBO,OOO a year extra to keep their soap and
candle industry going (in addition to, say,
£400,000 for the goods themselves), and having
paid the money to themselves they naturally
still have it. "Wherefore the Horrors of Pro-
tection gentleman is of opinion that, instead of
paying themselves £580,000, it would be
cheaper to pay £400,000 to somebody else.
Also he points out in the first place that the
importer habitually charges 60 per cent, profit,
but in the soap and candle business, being
severely pressed by local competition created by
Protection, he only charges 33 per cent. Then
he urges that the whole business should be
abandoned to the importer, so that he may
again charge 60 per cent, profit, which would
amount to about £240,000 a year, in order to
save the alleged £lBO,OOO spent in encouraging
local manufactures. Curiously enough, the
Telegraph’s special lunatic, though he repre-
sents the importer as charging 60 per cent,
profit, makes no allowance anywhere for the
saving effected through the abolition of him and
his huge percentage.
* * * *
The narrative is a confused affair all through.
A protected country pays to itself duty on a
small quantity of goods imported in one parti-
cular line, and the duty (which it pays to itself
and which would otherwise have to be made up
by some other form of taxation) is one of the
losses arising from Protection. It doesn’t pay
duty on similar goods locally manufactured, and
the amount the revenue doesn’t receive under
this head is another loss. Consequently, both
the money it pays and the money it doesn’t pay
are dead loss. Then the money which goes out
of the country as the importers’ profit is loss,
and the amount which remains in the country
as the manufacturers’ profit is also loss. Next,
the amount which would otherwise go out of
the country as importers’ loot, if the local manu-
facturer hadn’t partly suppressed that 50 or 60
per cent, cormorant, isn’t profit. And when
the amount that the people pay to themselves
is added to the amount they don’t pay to them-
selves, but which they might pay if someth i n g was
which isn’t, and the amount that goes out of the
country is thrown in, and also the amount that
doesn’t go out of the country, and the thing that
is has been added to the thing that might have
been, the total makes up a huge alleged loss,
for which Protection is responsible. The moral
is that a pint is losing a quart annually, and
has been doing so for a long time, and isn’t yet
quite exhausted, which is a rather larger moral
than any ordinary person can hold.
Booth versus Confucius.
Dear Bulletin, —I lately remarked in your
columns upon the absurdity of the Salvation
Army s expectation of extricating—as regards
the Malay Archipelago—any considerable num-
ber of souls from the remarkably enduring grip
of Mahommed ; and, since it now appears that
the Corybantic Cult proposes, by extending its
operations to Cathay, to encroach upon what
has hitherto been regarded as the special
preserve of the hysterically-evangelical Young
Person, I should like to ask a few
straight-out questions. First, has the Salva-
tion Army the remotest idea that in the life of
the average “Chinaman at Home” the so-
called Christian virtues are very much more in
evidence than in any ostensibly Christian com-
munity ? Also, has the S.A. the faintest con-
ception of the fact that the average Celestial
intellect is vastly more comprehensive and acute
than the average S.A. ditto? Again, what
answer is the S.A. prepared to give to the
intelligent Chinaman who will, to a dead cer-
tainty, put to the would-be proselytiser the
following pertinent questions ?
* * • *
“What shall I gain by accepting from you a
theory of things speculative, which, while in
no way less fanciful and visionary than my own
enters so very much less deeply into your daily
life translates itself so much less practically
into your everyday conduct than the precepts
of my theological teachers do into mine ? Your
Christ, you say, has commanded you
when smitten on one cheek, to turn the
other to the smiter. Do you do it? Have
tivelwW^v 116 , ifc —individually or collec-

nation that you are—waxed insolently fat upon

the whole—heathen as you call me—a life which
accords much better than yours with the
moral standards by which you profess to
be guided. What, therefore, shall it profit me
to subscribe to your assertions that Three and
One—God and Man—are identical ? These
things are to me vastly more absurd supersti-
tions than any of the beliefs which you are so
ready thus to characterise in my case. The
question is simply one of intellectual superiority,
and you proceed throughout upon the assump-
tion that this of necessity rests with you. But
you have, so far, done nothing whatever to
prove this ; nor, until you have done so, can
you expect me to receive or respect your doc-
trines about things which cannot possibly be
known.
* * * «
“Meantime my beliefs—upon antecedent evi-
dence —are at least as likely to be correct as
your own. While you say that I am neces-
sarily wrong I, in return, say merely that you
are probably so ; and to that extent I am wider
thipi you are—and deeper. When you have
learnt the humility which you are always
preaching, and have got rid of your intellectual
disease of gratuitous assumption, you may pos-
sibly not be confined for your record of ‘ con-
verts ’ to that class of my countrymen which
finds its best parallel in the ignorant enthu-
siasts of your Salvation Army.”
* * * *
Now, to contend intellectually with the man
who will say this—and I have heard him say it
better —an itching evangelism sends forth its
green-witted girls—religious mountebankery, its
bellowing nincompoops. “ The humor of it ! ”
—Macassar.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Queensland and the Press.
The corrupt ring of Q. politicians—feeding
their bank with public money which they annex
in private overdrafts—is, of course, not repre-
sentative of the manhood and intelligence of
the province. On any fair electoral system
Queensland would place honest men in charge
of her government. But, whatever the methods
by which the existing Q. administrators reached
office, they legally speak and act for their pro-
vince ; and their acts and speeches must be
accepted as Queensland’s acts and speeches. It
is apparently their wish that the kanaka
country should be reckoned, not as a part of
white Australia, not as the friend of the
southern provinces, but as a lukewarm ally
or a secret enemy. Ignoble jealousies and
pocket-motives have held Queensland aloof
from the approaching Federal Convention to
try and found an Australian nation. Almost
as unworthy seem the measures more or less
aimed to exclude from Q. circulation the
Southern newspapers unafraid of Govt,
threats, unsubsidised by the Govt. bank. The
Bulletin is not here referring to itself. The
Australasian, the Leader and the Sydney
Mail, for example, though generally favorable
to constituted authorities and capitalistic
views, cannot avoid occasional expressions of
disgust at the robbery of the people by the
Northern financial rings. Q. Govt, proposes to
punish objectionable newspapers through their
profits. It prohibits the publication of in-
decent advertisements and gambling advertise-
ments ; and from Ist January, 1897, news-
papers published outside Queensland are to be
charged per 2oz. of weight for postal trans-
mission—which brings the postage on The
Bulletin to 2d., and on the Australasian to
3d. or more. As regards indecent advts. there
is only one opinion : they should not be pub-
lished. But decision as to what is or is
not indecent is often very hard. Obviously
the new law gives any Q. Postmaster-General
power to cause an unfriendly paper a great deal
of annoyance by deciding that its advts. are
indecent, while a friendly paper with precisely
the same advts. may be winked at. The pro-
hibition of sweep advts. may or may not be
judicious, but, on the part or Q. Govt., it is im-
pudently hypocritical. All Q. horse-races are
specially licensed by Govt. ; bookmakers and
totalisators are allowed to gamble openly with
the public for whose morals Govt, professes to
be so solicitous ; and all totalisator-receipts are
taxed 24 per cent, for the benefit of the revenue
—last year’s tax realising £3984. Thus the
Govt, which, with its white face, prosecutes a
newspaper for publishing sweep advts., with its
black hands takes nearly £4OOO per annum out
of sweep-profits ! The matter of increased
postage is the most serious. Its deliberate
object is to shut out from Q. circulation the
large southern weeklies—mostly antagonistic to
the Govt.-—while the large local weeklies
(which exist partly by ingratiating themselves
with Govt., and buying Govt, advts. with
editorial support) are carried at each,
as before, irrespective of 'weight. Again,
if Q. Parliament licenses this, The Bulletin,
though lamenting the narrow spirit shown and
wishing the province a more enlightened Parlia-
ment, has nothing to say. But Q- Parliament
does not license it. The new charge is made
by the Postmaster-General in apparent exercise
of his delegated authority ; and such authority,
if he have it, is plainly far too wide. For, if
theP.M.G. can thus discriminate between the
newspapers of his own province and those of
the rest of Australia, there is practically no
limit to his power. Instead of charging a hos-
tile newspaper per 20z., he may charge it
Is. per Joz., and destroy its circulation com-
pletely. The Russian censor blacks out pas-
sages offensive to Govt., but the Q. press censor
can black out the whole newspaper. Q. Post-
master-General’s attitude will make little dif-
ference to The Bulletin. But, in its anti-
federal spirit and its opportunities for petty
tyranny, it further disgraces Queensland and is
to be deplored by all Australia.

mercy gave an extra 48s. for stumps and bad ground,
some men working the same time stated that they only
made Is. per day. The land is, it is rumored, to be used
as a cemetery. It is one already—’tis filled with dead
Hopes and broken hearts, and if curses wrung by bitter
can damn any man’s soul, that of the bowelless
omcial whose interior contains only salvation and tea-leaves
will some day bump heavily against the very firebars of
nu”' bs ' P e l’ week for hard toiling from 8 a.m.
11 ‘ 0 p.m., and a 5-mile walk each way to and from work
thrown in to swell the hours from 6.50 to 6.30. Truly, the
relief of the Foreign-raid Govt, is a grisly thing. Riverina
squatters pay their Chinamen more than this. Once a
reliefer always a reliefer. Let the unemployed work till
every smew feels like a red-hot wire and all things turn
blue, let him sleep on the ground and start with the sun ;
a careful management will see that he makes no more
than will pay for food and perhaps rent. His boots he
must get from the Benevolent Asylum, or steal ; his
clothes he must cadge or pick up in Paddy’s Market, and
b6ar upon him the unmistakeable stamp of one of
“Creers Unemployed,” which bars him from almost
every hope of respectable employment. The present sys-
tem of relief-work benefits only those who superintend it
and grocer Kidman, the Govt, contractor. When the
reliefer runs out of food he gets a certificate from the
works inspector that he has worked so many hours, which
he presents by proxy to Boss Creer, who for 12
hours’ work will advance him an order on grocer
Kidman for two rations, 21b. of rice and one tin of con-
densed milk ; value, 3s. 4£d. The two rations amount to
four loaves of bread, 4lb. sugar (white), of tea, and
61b. beef or mutton, whichever it may suit contractor
Kidman to supply. The 3s. 4b>d. is carefully deducted
from the reliefer’s earnings on pay-day. The reliefer may
have no wood with which to cook his meat and boil water
to make his tea, but grocer Kidman does not sell wood,
and although the reliefer pays the Bureau hard cash for
its order he can only buy with it just what grocer Kidman
and an imbecile Govt, think he ought to require. It he
is very hungry he can eat the meat raw and chew the
tea ; or, if he is a bold, bad, ungrateful man he may steal
the back of a seat from the nearest park. Minister
Garrard seldom inspects relief-works—only once. The
men working at Randwick are sad fathers of families and
mostly men past middle age, and are anything hut
dangerous.
Morality put into Figures.
Ed. Bulletin, —Some little time ago Dr.
came in for a good deal of ridicule "for certain
assertions in re the chastity of the women of
N.S.W. No harm, I take it, can come from a
knowledge of the truth, for which we have not
far to seek. The figures lam about to quote
are taken from Coghlan’s “ Wealth and Pro-
gress,” and are susceptible of being readily
checked. Taking the years ’93 and ’94 to-
gether, it would seem that there were 15,415
marriages, and amongst the ladies who were
parties to these marriages there were 11,385
who had apparently not previously made a
slight trial of matrimony, while 4030 of them
had a quasi-matrimonial experience, as their
first children were born*’before they were nine
months married. Of course, there were some
amongst the 4030 whose delivery was prema-
ture, but against these may be set the women
who, though unchaste before marriage, yet
were not delivered of a child during their first
year. During ’93 and ’94 the illegitimate births
numbered 4947; so that there were 8977 women
whose children were either illegitimate or nar-
rowly escaped being so. Assume that all the
women with illegitimate children ought to
have been married during the years quoted, and
there were or would have been 20,362 marriages,
and of these 8977, or over 45 in every 100,
would have been of women who neglected to
keep their favors until after marriage. There is
another way of looking at the question. The
civil condition of women and their age are
ascertained at the census, and the number of
unmarried women at each age can be compared
with the number of women of the same age who
had illegitimate children during any year. It
is unfortunate that the comparison cannot he
made for the census year. In the figures lam
quoting, the number of unmarried women is for
1891 and the births for 1894. The comparison
errs on one side by reason of the slight increase
in single women in three years, and on the other
side because women in hospitals, gaols, and
lunatic asylums are included. For children
whose fathers and mothers were, as the daily
papers put it, familiar before marriage, I shall
use the euphemism, quasi-legitimate :
The last column represents the chances out of
a hundred of a single woman being with child at
each age. This, however, does not represent
the full case. If you take the number of mar-
ried women at their ages and divide by the
number of children born to them during a year
you obtain the probabilities of conception.
This for (say) age 17 is 2—that is for every two
married women of 17 one child will be born in
a year ; at ages from 35 to 40 the probability is
4to 1 against conception. So if you wish to
arrive at the presumed number of unmarried
women who have been familiar with men dur-
ing a year, multiply the percentage of births by
the chances against conception, which I shall
now proceed to do.
ihus you see that it is perfectly certain at (say)
age 20, out of 1000 single women 53 will have
,their little familiarities with men, while the
chances are that 133 will be so minded, the
worst period being from 21 to 24, where the
actual ascertained frailties are 65 in 1000,
whilst the probables number 169.— Herman
Beckner.
“ There was never a time when any Legislative
Council in Victoria exhibited itself in an atti-
tude more hostile to the broad aspirations of
an intelligent populace than the present. Mr.
Isaacs’s Companies Bill has shared the fate of
Mr. Wynne s, and, thanks to a House composed
of men personally interested in maintaining
loose conditions, the Vic. people have to see
their law continue in the old state of dis-
reputable insecurity to the investing public.
There is a party in the Council, voiced by
people like Mr. Reid, who continually cry out
We want to be let alone. There is no need
for all this radical legislation.’ But what is
the meaning of this fatuous form of words—
this cry to be let alone ? It can only mean
that the dishonesty which has been rampant in
company-promoting is to be continued, or to
have at least another chance to exert itself
against the defenceless part of the community.
It must mean that the hawks of society are to be
permitted another swoop upon the unguarded
pigeons.” Thus spake Melb. Age on Dec. 23.
The situation was fairly depicted. The Council’s
amendments of the Companies Bill had practi-
cally secured a further lease of life for “ the
old state of disreputable insecurity to the in-
vesting public,” and the Turner Government
was in duty bound to at least make an attempt
to obtain something better. But a little bird
came whispering in the editorial ear that Premier
Turner wanted peace at any price rather
than a righteous rebellion against Sargood’s
despotism. Next day, therefore, the Age an-
nounced that “ the Government took a wise
course in recommending the Assembly to accept
the Council s amendments in the Companies
Bill. . . . It would be a mistake to suppose
that the Bill as accepted by the Assembly is not
a valuable reform of the companies law.”
The N.S. Wales Ministry gets on steadily with
preaching Freetrade and practising Protection.
Its latest idea is to call for tenders for 40,000
blankets “to be manufactured locally.” This
isn’t a case of giving the work to the cheapest
tenderer, which is Freetrade ; nor of giving the
local man a certain specified advantage openly
announced and fixed by Parliament, which is
ordinary Trotection ; but of shutting out every-
body except the local maker, which is the most
aggravated form of Protection imaginable.
Even M’Kinley doesn’t go so far as this. And
this Protection isn’t given by Parliamentary
sanction ; it is fixed by the Reid Government
on its own responsibility, with no authority
whatever. And yet the Reid Government is
never tired of preaching that no industry (not
even the blanket industry) should be artificially
supported at the expense of the taxpayers (by
the State taking its goods, for instance, at a
higher price than similar articles could be pro-
cured at elsewhere). Also, it is never tired of
denouncing the late Dibbs-Lyne Government
for inviting local tenders only when it wanted
blankets (we mean iron pipes), and point-
ing out the reckless wast'e and wicked-
ness of the whole business. The Bulletin
believes in Protection, but it regards it as a
piece of sickening hypocrisy when a Government
lives and moves and gets its salary under false
pretences by preaching Freetrade, yet prac-
tises Protection on every possible occasion,
and at the same time brazenly denies that it
does practise it. If Premier Reid dare not live
up to the Foreign-trade creed, then it is time
the Foreign-trade party in the Assembly re-
placed him by a leader who isn’t ashamed of
his alleged faith. The spectacle of Reid, the
Freetrader, practising exactly the same kind of
Protection that he denounced as incurably sin-
ful when followed by Lyne, the Protectionist,
is a tolerably sickening spectacle for any honest
community.
Dr. O’Sullivan, speaking the other day as
president of the Vic. branch of the British
Medical Association, had something to say re
women on bicycles In spite of himself, he
had become convinced that cycling, as gener-
ally practised, must lead to unpleasant health
results. No man with a knowledge of special
female ailments could deny that the use of the
pedal sewing-machine, even when worked under
the most favorable conditions, was a frequent
cause of ill-health, but in cycling the dangers
to the individual were increased tenfold. Nerve
strain when using the machine was certainly at
its highest,” &c., See. The O’Sullivan opinion
is none the more valuable for having been deli-
vered by the president of a medical meeting, but
it is entitled to quite as much respect as the
oracular predictions of a Dr. Springthorpe, or
any other out-and-out barracker for the new
craze. It will not dismay the women who
already ride bikes, and recommend their fair
friends to join them in enjoyment of “ a most
delightful sensation,” but, on the other hand,
Dr. O’Sullivan’s warning may persuade some
of the hesitating ones to pause and look around
before they leap into the little leather saddle.
The question as to whether cycling is healthy
or otherwise for women seems to be answered
in the faces of those who have been longest on
the road. How do the pioneer cyclodonahs of
this warm country look after a couple of years
indulgence in their “ splendid exercise ” ?
How would Ponshopalia (phonetic) do as a
name for Queensland? The whole province,
east, west, north and south, is up the spout!
otab the fatman of other puovinces, and he,
- s °* n e him, bleeds sovereigns ; puncture
the Q. illusion, and as he collapses he exudes
promises to pay. r l he Government party indi-
vidually is in pawn to the Q.N.8., and the Bank
to the Government in its collective capacity.
The squatter is in pawn, and Lazarus at his
gate in the shape of the 160-acre cocky is under
the thumb of his storekeeper, he again under
the wholesale man, and so round to the bank.
Spider, the blackboy, has to crawl into the
bush past the Chinaman’s garden for the want
of a shilling to square “ his open account.” The
Chow s wife and family are mortgaged over in
the Flowery Land for his passage-money— the
Jap. up on the shell-beds owes for his diving-
suit, and little Billy Brown, at the Pro-
visional School, goes in terror of his life because
he can t pay a big boy for the dozen of marbles
he got on tick. In fact, the whole province
spends what time it has over from dodging its
creditor in hunting up the other fellow that
owes it something.
The charnel-men of the law have been threaten-
ingly shaking some of their bags of bones lately
in Australia. The operation makes stenches,
but is useful as a danger-signal, and should
ready people for a code containing as far as
possible all the common law, and so much of
the English statute law as is in force in Aus-
tralia, A clause should be inserted forbidding
future reference behind the code—making it,
in fact, a final statement of the law ub to the
date of its passing. A glance would then show
what was obsolete or unsuited to the circum-
stances of the country, and a cleansing revision
Act could and would be speedily passed. The
work is worth federal action (but federation
need not be waited for), especially as prac-
tically the same English common and statute
law is in force in all the provinces. The danger
of letting sleeping dogs lie, and dead dogs
pollute the legal air, is surely sufficiently shown
to even the indifferent Australian public, when
we have, in a few short weeks, a trial for
libelling a magistrate by sending a complaint
to the Minister of Justice in Victoria, two
instances in the same province of a pedantic
P.M. ordering a suicide’s body to be buried at
midnight, a trial for a “ conspiracy to im-
poverish ” in bydney, and a trial for sorcery
at lamworth. Codification has limitations,
perhaps dangers, but certainly not as manv as
the present no-system.
Writes “ E.M.H.” to The Bulletin :
About larrikins. Whatever vices pertain to this genus,
how does it happen that I, a girl reared in the push-
infested locality, upon going into the street after dark,
should look for probable insult in seeing l well-dressed
men loitering about? Of the larrikins at the corner I
take no heed. Upon one occasion I was protected by a
larrikin from the forced company of one who, by his guise,
would be called a gentleman. This well-dressed indivi-
dual followed me from the city to Newtown after ten one
night, and when I got into the quiet streets, compelled
me by his conduct to appeal to the first man I met, who
was a larrikin, and who snarled at his “superior,” some-
thing in the manner of a bull-dog, and accompanied me
to my friends. When I thanked him he mumbled some-
thing unintelligible, and lounged off with a short
“ S’long !” We certainly do hear of larrikins being expert
m kicking, the use of blue metal, and jumping on people,
but while the brutalities of the “gentleman” are con-
cealed or palliated by society the vices of the larrikin rise
broad-voiced in the ears of the people. If circumstances
compelled me, I would far rather pass through a crowd of
larrikins, trusting to my respectable appearance, than
through the same number of (say) University students.—
E.M.H.
[Which fits in with the contention of The
Bulletin that the respectable woman who be-
haves respectably and doesn’t dawdle is seldom
insulted by the “ larrikin,” who, to do him
justice, is always far more ready to help or pro-
tect a woman than to molest her. Yet, when
the law flogs anyone in Australia it is never a
black-coated “gentleman,” convicted on clear
evidence of having presumed on the privileges
of his social position, but always some unhappy
wretch in workman’s (or, if you like, larrikin’s)
garb.— Ed. B.]
Dear Bulletin,— Often during the last few
years have 1 heard the assertion that “ the
butter-trade saved Victoria.” Did it ? What
permanent good—beyond the sending to Hing-
land of decent cow-fat—has it done? The Vic.
butter-industry to-day is one of the worst-
sweated businesses on the face of the earth—
from beginning to end—from cow to consumer.
I know—l am in it. The farmers round here
(N. Gippsland) pay their milkers from 4s. to 10s.
per week and keep, and they work the usual day-
light till dark, which means in summer 15 or 16
hours a day, Sundays as well. 110 hours a
week ! Consider that, ye 48 hours’ men. The
milk goes to the factory or the creamery. Most
of these country creameries are now practically
the property of three or four Melb. companies.
As a general thing they put one man—in some
cases oply a youth—in complete charge of a
creamery. He has to receive, tally and test
the milk, take charge of an engine and boiler
and two or more separators, pump, &c., keep
the place .clean, and generally run the
whole concern, and he considers him-
self well paid at 30s. per week. At a
butter-factory there are generally three men
employed. The manager gets £2, the other
man 305., and the boy perhaps 10s. They work
from 5 a.m. to about 5 p.m., Sundays as well.
The butter goes to Melbourne, there to be ex-
ported or sold to grocers, &e. The butter
auctioneers work their half-dozen storemen and
clerks from 8 a.m. to 6 p.m., and twice a week
—on sale-nights—to 11 p.m. ; and they pay
them—well, in God’s name, what do they pay
them now ? Probably £1 a week. I used to
get 30s. in the better times of ’9O, and I was
not the worst-paid. The grocers’ assistants,
who handle the butter last of all, are probably
better-off than any of their fellow-workers,
thanks chiefly to the Factories Act; but farm-
laborers were apparently not worth considera-
tion in that measure, while butter-factories and
creameries were carefully exempted from its
operations, chiefly through the agency of an
interested party in the Leg. Council. It’s a
great trade entirely, but when next you eat
your butter don’t forget the human sweat th it
goes into it, or the many lives worn out to pro-
vide grease for other men’s food.—F. Cooson.

SUNDRY SHOWS.

Her Majesty’s (late Alexandra) “The Serpent’s Coil.”
“ Tommy Atkins ” was duly presented by
the returned Geo. Rignold at Sydney Royal, and
proved to be a vast, gorgeous, and beautifully im-
probable drama of the first magnitude. “ At-
kins ” is far above the level of the common or
garden melo-shriek. The plot is decent in a
literary sense; the dialogue is clever ; the mount-
ing is as good as G. Rignold always makes it ;
also, the church-scene in particular is a quite un-
usual piece of Gothic realism and elaborate finish.
“T. Atkins,” in fact, earns The Bullletin’s
warm approval as a vast, costly, well-devised
spectacle which is full of paint and varnish and
green landscape and valuable moral. The heroine,
it is true, goes wrong, and humps the illicit in-
fant about in very much the old way, but her
ejection by her hard old parent is done under novel
circumstances. Also, the father doesn’t relent,
but remains a hard-kicking old churchwarden to
the last, and almost his final wish as he dies in the
sure and certain hope of a glorious resurrection is
that his daughter will remain cold, and wet, and
homeless, and bedraggled about the hair all the rest
of her days. This is in itself a new departure.
Private Rignold is killed in the last act with his
sins, which include robbery with violence and the
throwing of the lost girl over a cliff, on her head,
which is also novel; and the Rev. Plimmer jumps
on his black coat and solemnly swears off the
snuffle of his fathers, and enlists as a private,
which is a startling departure from the usual
dramatic curate. The wicked captain has two
wives, and, though he is only a small man, it
takes four men to hold him back from marrying a
third one ; and when he perishes in the desert, a
disgraced, shot and singed man, he doesn’t repent
or say beautiful things in his last moment, but
dies with a howl and a curse, as he had lived.
After that, the rest is battle, murder, gunshot,
fire and red revolution, and the brown sands of
the desert are covered with glory and dead Atkins.
* * * *
The new co. is better than any which
Rignold has shown tor a good many years. The
Rev. Plimmer is a very fine hero who makes every
point in the character of the fighting curate. The
wicked captain of H.W. Diver is also above par ;
he is a good elocutionary sinner whose energy and
strict attention to crime deserve a long continu-
ance of public support. Diver is an actor of much
promise, and he is a bundle of nerves whose
activity is interesting after the slow impassiveness
of wicked captains in general. Hans Phillips
lends much picturesqueness to the slow, drinky
decadence of Ebenezer Skindle, and two new
ladies, Misses Gresham and Guildford, as the
second and third wives, are pretty and promise to
do well. Also G. Rignold is a satisfactory sub-
ordinate villain, and Miss Watts - Phillips
is as good as that clever little woman usu-
ally is. The merciful removal of the first wife
by premeditated gunshot is a decided relief.
That gaunt waif, by the way, after being fatally
wounded and knocked flat, gets to her feet again
and makes a column of remarks at Diver in a per-
fectly healthy voice, which might be good enough
in a consumptive or a case of death by influenza.
Also, the heroine with the groundless infant isn’t
all she might be, though she might easily be
worse. Taken all-round, however, “T. Atkins”
is a most deserving melodrama ; and the gradual
bloating of the nose of Ebenezer Skindle as that
clerical person sinks into the sere and beery leaf
and becomes a weak follower after skittles is a
moral lesson that is alone worth the entrance-
money.
** * *
Charles M‘Carthy, the new Irish-Amur-
fikin comedian, opened at Sydney Lyceum on
Saturday in “Lady Blarney.” M‘Carthy, as a
hroguey man in woman’s clothes, is certainly a
success, and ranks with the widow Sheridan-
O’Brien and with Frank Thornton as the lady
from Brazil where the nuts come from. This
paper doesn’t enthuse much over the man in
woman’s clothes as a general thing, but, so far
as the part is worth dong, M‘Carthy does it
very well. The play is a Widow O’Brienesque
frivolity to which the author has lent a tinge of
seriousness by inserting a wicked brother who
does enormous embezzlements and leads Harrie
Ireland astray, a disreputable father with a wart
who forges cheques, a good brother who worships
pretty Ida Hamilton, a large, stern captain
who comes home from the briny, sharky deep
to find that his eldest girl is morally
“lost,” and can’t honestly account for the
possession of the jewellery, and various other
things. It is a perverse kind of comedrama, for
Scot Inglis, looming up darkly in the long frock-
coat of sin, and the inscrutable smile of iniquity,
and the glossy bell-topper of black treachery,
wants to marry Ida Hamilton because she is rich,
and Robert Inman won’t marry her because he
is poor; and Harrie Ireland is more or less
willing- to marry either of them, but neither will
have her. W herefore, Scot Inglis sits gloomily
behind a screen embezzling the trust money, and
the captain, in the next room, bellows at his
jewelled girl who is “lost,” and Miss Hamilton
weeps because the good brother is accused of the
forged cheque, and the sinful, warty parent con-
ceals himself behind the furniture.
When these things fail, as they mostly do,
to keep the show going the he-woman cornea in
and rages in brogue, and falls in astonishing calis-
thenics over her train, and generally is an Irish
bull all over the premises. There is a lot of bright
dialogue in the part of the Irish lady, and
M ‘Carthy rushes through it gaily, and keeps the
character alive all the time. Otherwise the piece
is best supported by Scot Inglis, who is as good as
usual, though the long frock-coat shows a slight ten-
dency to be monotonous, by Inman, I rank Glark,
and Misses Harrie Ireland and Ida Hamilton. Ihe
former lady is, so far, a success in everything she
attempts, and, in addition, her stately figure makes
her about the best clothes-advt. in Australia.
Miss Hamilton has the first good part she has
played in this country, and the public, which
hitherto judged her ability by the small characters
which she did under the shadow of the awful
Potter, was surprised to find her make a real
success all through. A few other people help to
make up a generally strong company, ana under
these favorable circumstances “ Lady B. has
rather better prospects than its literary merits call
for.
* * *
Westmacott's pantomime at Her Majesty’s
promises to be a moderate success for various
reasons, about some of which The Bulletin is
not very clear. It is the only panto, in Sydney,
for one thing, and it is beautifully mounted, and
the mechanical arrangements run without a hitch,
and there is plenty of glitter and nice frock in it,
and a reasonable quantity of cave, and as much
hairy demon as any person has a right to expect,
and a lot of variety show—good, mixed, and in-
different. The Lucifers (one male and one female)
are the brightest and most original feature in it,
and are distinctly above par. Brother Lucifer
goes through an astonishing musketry drill with
part of himself for the musket, and shoulders
and presents arms with his own leg in a
surprising fashion. Also, he runs with both
legs round his neck, and kicks two baskets
simultaneously at a height of nine feet. The
average athlete is a dreary person who does
tricks in a dull, stodgy fashion and then goes
away, but Lucifer fills in the proceedings with a
stream of amusing patter, and doesn’t even profess
to take himself seriously. Lucifer is a very big
circumstance and ought to boom greatly. Provo,
the clever juggler, suffers from the fact that he
isn’t at all new, and so do the clever Banvards
and Joe Walhalla. As for “ Sinbad ”in general,
it is just the same as any other “ Sinbad,” and the
other “Sinbad” is so like “Cinderella” that
neither of them can be told from “Jack the
Giant-Killer,” and the whole lot could pass for the
“ House that Jack Built ” without anybody notic-
ing the difference. Crackles’ great joke, “ I’ve
got another hat,” grows wearisome when it
has been repeated 47 times, and it is a
mechanical steam joke at the best. When
Crackles says “ I’ve got another hat,” D’Arcy
Stanfield, as the awful man in woman’s clothes,
lifts up his voice and wails “ I’m such a young
thing, too,” and then Lawton remarks “Oh, I
am a popular man. ” These three statements con-
stitute almost all the real fun of the pantomime.
George Leopold, in the character of Premier
Reid, falls very flat indeed, and in general the
local allusions are old and musty, or else new and
weak. The pantomimist who tries to get a laugh
out of the Soudan goat at this late hour, especi-
ally as the animal is long since dead, is decidedly
out of date. Nevertheless, Lawton’s whistle and
the wonderfully limber Lucifer, and the scenery
and the transformation and the nice girls and the
frocks count for a great deal, and The Bulletin’s
opinion is that if the painful Crackles doesn’t get
another hat too often the management will make
a very much undeserved profit out of “Sinbad,”
despite the fact that three-fourths of the panto,
ought to be killed on the spot. Regarding the
girls, they are all nice in a moderate way, but no
one of them is conspicuously nicer than the rest.
Anyhow, this paper hasn’t got them sorted out
yet.
** * *
The Brough co. made its re-appearance at
Sydney Cri. in “Nancy and C 0.,” a play in four
bursts of comic insanity which rages exceedingly
for 2 2 hours or so, and seems likely to do well.
There is a blessed absence of any moral in it, and
the sex problem ceases from troubling, and the
weary villain is at rest. Nancy is a lady suffering
from suppressed literature which has struck in-
wards, and the worst thing she does is to help a
perfectly respectable young man to write a play.
But, this being a suspicious world, the two are sup-
posed to be bent on surreptitiously hugging each
other, and an ancient and meddlesome person fol-
lows them up and drives them to distraction.
The play consists largely in the agitated flight of
the pair, and their concealment in many places,
and a fatuous pursuit by the meddlesome
patriarch and others, and the falling of everybody
over everybody else’s feet under circumstances
which are too complicated to admit of explana-
tion. The solemn truth of the matter is generally
such a ghastly improbability that it isn’t worth
while making anyone attempt to believe it, there-
fore the people concerned tell awful whoppers to
each other when they are found concealed in the
hat-rack under dim and shady conditions, or
trying to escape up a chimney, and thereby make
the confusion even more aggravated than before.
• * * .
There is a perfect blizzard of suspicious
circumstances in the four acts. Brough, who is
merely trying, in a respectable wig and a weak
voice, to bring the wicked to justice, is himself
snowed up under many base calumnies before the
trouble is over. Mrs. Brough, who helps Roberts
to write the drama, is a bright, fresh, loveable
character, and Roberts himself is a distinct suc-
cess, but circumstances are too much for them
also. A number of other people are either
brilliantly fallen upon in the dark, or else they
rush into dimly-lighted rooms in search of the
alleged person who committed the thing ia
didn’t happen, and can be heard coming down
with the ability of the true artist and being
severely bumped against the furniture. miss
Hope Mayne, who makes her first Sydney appear-
ance in “ Nancy,” promises remarkably well, ana
as the piece has evidently caught on, and Fercy
Ward, another new personage, seems all rignt,
there is little left to wish for. “ Nancy ls > ln
some respects, a shade more impossible than tne
average comedy; in real life somebody must
needs sit on the head of old Ebenezer about the
second act, and tell him such an eminently pro-
bable lie that the whole matter would be cleared
up ; but there is a great amount of mirth in it,
and it goes at a flying pace, and is quite good
enough for any ordinary purpose.
*- * * *
The Brough season is to cover only six
weeks, and in that brief period it is proposed to
produce “A Woman of No Importance, A.
Woman’s Reason,” “Sowing the Wind,” and A
White Elephant.” The latter animal is abso-
lutely new and has just been unpacked.
** * *
The trouble about Sydney Palace has been
so far settled that manager Garner has been bound
over in a small sum of money not to bash manager
Goatcher for a comparatively brief period. The
Palace continues to be crowded every night, and
the most unsatisfactory person in the bill has re-
moved one cause of wrath by a judicious disap-
pearance. Knowles’s sketch of the way various
nations go home when they are drunk improves
with further acquaintance, and the balance of
opinion seems to be that the programme is a little
better than it was in some respects and no worse
in others. Two absolutely hair-raising trapezists,
Wilton and Stack, have been added to the list of
performers, and considering the chances they take
they will either be very popular or will shortly be
dead.
** * *
Chirgwin, the white-eyed Kaffir, is the
principal attraction this week at Sydney Tivoli,
where that versatile person is doing an extra-
ordinary number of things to the satisfaction of
most people concerned. As a vocalist, a performer
on the bagpipes and many other horrors, a, teller
of strange anecdotes ,and a wandering minstrel
and humorist generally, Chirgwin fills a pretty
large bill, and does it quite as well as any other
variety showman on earth.
** * *
The ancient, luckless Sydney Gaiety, which
has swallowed up many managements and many
one-night shows, and where a long succession of
spectres have either walked with difficulty, or not
at all, is open again, under the management of
another of the innumerable Cosgroves, with a loyal
drama of great violence, called “ Under the British
Flag. ” The Bulletin is suffering at present from
altogether too much undigested Xmas drama, and
further details about the “ Flag ” are held over.
** * *
M‘Mahon’s Cinematographe and the other
Cinematographe both remain above ground. Also,
some misguided person has been giving views of
the murder of Lee Weller and other horrors at an
address which it is better not to mention, and
quite an eruption of small shows has taken place
in various parts of Sydney.
*” * *
First impressions of “ Matsa,” the Firm’s
panto-extravaganza, at Melbourne Princess’s, are
very favorable in the bulk. There are points
where “ Matsa ” surpasses the glory of “ Djin-
Djin,” and in all respects the new show has been
calculated to meet the popular demand for expen-
siveness. The story, which begins better than it
continues, is full of open spaces where ballets,
processions, and Gordon’s excellent scenery can
come in. Every inch of opportunity for display
is turned to account. The splendor may be irrele-
vant, but it gets there all the same, and spreads a
sense of satisfaction that sticks to the mind long
after the mind has ceased to wonder what excuse
the spectacle.could have had for happening. For
the present, “ Matsa” is weak in the musical de-
partment. The melodies have been heard before,
or are considerably adapted from familiar sources,
or else they belong to the class of original compo-
sitions which one would prefer never to hear at
all. But catchy numbers are so easily dropped
into a pantomime that the defect is pretty sure to
get remedied very soon. Wi th a first-class orchestra
and plenty of singing ability in the company,
“ Matsa ” ought to please the ear as much as it
delights the eye. And, talking of defects, Miss
Florence Young may as well be reprimanded for
her hard, inflexible style of impersonating a
romantic hero. I lorence deals with sundry super-
natural agencies as though she were buying a
pound of tea. She kills a dragon, or arranges to
extinguish the Fire Queen with a flood in the
most uninteresting manner, and her alleged devo-
tion to Flora Graupner betrays no warmth what-
ever. It is sad to think that Prince will
probably be chewing gum whilst conducting
I rincess Graupner to the altar, and will start the
honeymoon by cracking nuts.

as striking an achievement as the “ Djin-Djin ”

and the spn-its must hold a magnifying glass over

acres in any other portion of the picture. Ancient
Memphis the Mountains of the Moon, the Grand

places of special interest in “ Matsa,” which has a
most elastic plot. Several characters are older
than the Garden of Eden, and some are so modern
that they ride into the proceedings on bicycles
previous to being put back 1000 years for reasons

brilliantly on Boxing Night. George Lanri
