*# * *
“ The Strange Adventures of Miss Brown,’
at Melb. Bijou, proves to be a light, speedy, well
ribbed-up farce, capable of carrying a 15st. title to
the fore. A military captain, who has an Irish
major to urge him along his lawless way, is the soul
and most of the body of “ T.S. A.0.M.8.” The
captain abducts award in Chancery from boarding-
school and marries her, but before they can digest
their wedding-breakfast the bride is captured by
her guardian and taken back to the place whence
she came. Then the captain, disguised as “ Miss
Brown,” in a wild Irish costume and an auburn
wig, is dumped down at the seminary by the
enterprising major, with instructions to carry off
his bride when the clock strikes 10. Sweet girlish
attentions from sundry unsuspecting boarders
keep the warrior occupied until it is time
for the elopement, which fails miserably. The
bona-fide damsel gets away in a carriage, but
Miss Brown falls into the handcuffs of a detective
after a long unladylike struggle, and next morn-
ing a tattered, unshaven caricature of woman-
hood, with its wig out of curl, is restored to the
bosom of the boarding-school. A quite moral and
satisfactory denouement is brought about by the
news that the captain’s uncle has died, leaving him
an earldom and wealth untold, whereupon “ Miss
Brown ” shakes her muddy petticoats at the lady-
principal and reveals the awful truth. The truth,
coming from the stubbly lips of a live earl, is joy.
fully received.
• * * *
Frank Thornton, as the masquerading
captain, repeats his previous success in skirts.
Miss Brown, dropping curtseys to the high
priestess, or nestling among the other boarders
whilst Angela (the bride) punches a pillow to
relieve her feelings, is an extravagantly droll im-
postor. Moreover, a real atmosphere of ladies’
school life pervades the joke, a particularly good
character sketch being an ardent Red Indian sort
of boarder, played by Miss Bessie Rignold. New-
comers in Fred Shepherd and Miss Flsie Carew
make successful appearances as the major and the
schoolmistress ; also Miss Seabrooke is a pleasing
bride. Another fresh arrival, Herbert Terry, fills
the part of a German music-teacher effectively,
although his accent is very mixed and variable.
The remainder of the cast is mostly an assortment
of virgin pupils, who giggle and fix up their hair-
pins with artless abandon. “Miss Brown”
should run far into the holidays.
** * *
“ For England,” produced in Sydney a
while ago, and just now raging at Melb. Royal,
is Bland Holt’s fourteenth libel on Dutch courage.
Indomitable Britons slaughter contemptible Boers
at all points in this ramshackle narrative without
the slightest regard for history. “ For England ”
makes only one concession to the enemy. The
villain is Hinglish by birth, instead of being a
Boer, but, considering that this iniquitous person
occupies an exalted position in the Boer camp,
and hurls “ curse you !” at every Afrikander who
questions his authority, the aforesaid concession
doesn’t amount to much.
* * * *
Probably it was a mistake on the author’s
part, anyhow. An elaborate representation of a
fox-hunt, a series of desperate rescues and glorious
victories in foreign parts, together with an amiable
specimen of stage Hebrew, explained the hearty
reception of “ For England ” on Boxing Night.
The scenery is irresistible, and the tender-hearted
Jew money-lender (Bland Holt) fills a long-felt
want. Having lent his substance to the British
army on philanthropic terms, the financier watches
over the army’s interests in the character of a
friendly Zulu, and fires off funny remarks at the
contending forces. Bland Holt has never re-
velled in a “fatter” part. Miss Frances Ross
and Miss Watson, likewise Cosgrove (who is a
harmless dude for a change), assist in relieving the
monotony of the eternal feud between Baker and
Albert Norman. A word of encouragement is
likewise due to the girl who went wrong. Her in-
discretion serves to divert her thoughts from the
British Flag.

The Wild-Cat Column,
A few days before the prorogation of N.S.W.
Parliament the “Additional Loan Estimates” were un-
obtrusively run through. They amounted to *210,718.
The original Loan Estimates authorised the expenditure
within the year of *2,020,000 on “ permanent and repro-
ductive works” (including *26,000 for arms and ammuni-
tion, *35,000 for cutting down scrub, &c., *3600 for
roads, £376,000 for bridges, dredging, &c., *147,000 for
gaols, lock-ups, repairs, patches, statues, and the like, and
*300,000, or thereabouts, for other unprofitable sundries).
The new estimates provide *150,000 of borrowed money
for dismantling the old Sydney steam trams, which were
built with borrowed money and haven’t yet been paid for,
and replacing them with an electric service; also
*40,000 for repairs to Government House (which was
built with borrowed money and is now being patched
with ditto), for building police-stations in the far west,
sundry offices, some alterations to a park, a drain or two,
and other like wreckage. Out of the £210,000 only
£20,000 is distinctly reproductive. And the above shreds
and patches are what the Reid Government calls “ per-
manent and reproductive works.”
¥ * * *
S.A. is spending .£414,000 of loan money this
year. Its “permanent and reproductive public works”
include £2772 as the province’s contribution to the
defence of Thursday Island (Q.) and King George’s Sound
(Westralia), a sum towards the cost of an expedition sent
out to look for a new stock route to W.A.—the Aus-
tralian nowadays can’t explore except with borrowed
money —*3500 for guns, £9OOO for school buildings, and
similar investments. The Ministry which puts down an
exploring expedition to “permanent and reproductive
works” has a fine gift of finance.
• * • *
The National Bank of Tasmania presents a
balance-sheet which calls for considerable execration.
The half-year’s profits are £3534 against £4299, *4136, and
£3989 for three previous half-years. Out of this the
board pays a 5 per cent, dividend which, with income-
tax, absorbs £3991, or £457 more than the scanty earn-
ings. This leaves £1575 to be carried forward. Two
years ago the balance was £1607, but in June, ’95, the
National paid a bigger dividend than it had earned, and
it does the same now, and at other times its dividend ran
the earnings very close indeed, so that the net result for
two years is a reduction of its funds by £32. The
National of Tasmania is 11 years old. It has a capital of
*152,040, and a reserve of £22,500. Its dividend has
dwindled from 8 to 5 per cent., and the necessity for
further reducing it to 4 per cent, sticks out conspicuously.
Four per cent, may not be much of adiv., but it is evi-
dently all that the institution can afford just now. The
business seems about the same as it was two or three
years ago, and the large cash reserve ( 1109,000) equals 30
per cent, of the liabilities to the public. If the National
put *20,000 ot this unwieldy mass of cash into State
securities the interest would improve its profits consider-
ably. Otherwise the report gives the usual scarcity of
information, and the decline is set down to the con-
venient depression, and there is nothing in the figures
to cast any light on the solvency or otherwise of the
National’s affairs.
* * * »
The Central Queensland Land Corporation is
announced in London, its object being “to acquire a
practical monopoly ” of the land around Gladstone,
Queensland. Gladstone has a magnificent deep-water
harbor, and would have been the biggest town north of
Brisbane—only the Fat Men of the day had ground at
Rockhampton, and the railway was started from there so
as to bring trade and boom the town and send up prices.
Consequently, to meet the railway, steamers plod 40 miles
up the dismal, muddy, shallow river, and thereby incur
great expense, and go aground three times on an average
each voyage, while the harbor at Gladstone is deserted.
If a powerful syndicate gets afloat it will probably secure
a western railway connection for Gladstone, and then the
forlorn town will begin to move, and J. Bull will annex the
profits of the boom. A great, large, gorgeous boom
swindle has been waiting to be done at Gladstone for
many years, but no institution had the pluck to do it with-
out the inevitable British capital.
* * * *
One “ labor-agent ” —this sort of pirate calls
himself a “registry office” —shamelessly circularises his
possible patrons to this effect: —Quarterly subscription,
ss.membership ” ends when the “member” has ob-
tained a billet through the “ office,” even though that
billet may not last a month; fees —“for commercial
situations, one week’s salary (!) ; for professional, educa-
tional, or other appointments, 5 per cent, on one year’s
salary (!!); minimum charge, 10s.” If Outowork gets a
billet at 15s. a week, and has to pay £1 railway and coach
fare, and 10s. to the “registry” cormorant, he having
already paid 5s as “subscription” for “membership,”
and if he gets “the run ” in (say) a fortnight —not an
uncommon practice, by collusion with the “ labor-agent ”
—and has to pay his return-fare to Sydney, he will be just
£1 ss. out of pocket in cash, to say nothing about
the cost of his living while dawdling as a “ member ”
around the “ registry ” office. The Bulletin not long
ago showed how at least one “labor-agent” was in a
business conspiracy with an employer to halve all the
fees from billetees, said employer, on his part, agreeing
to find some excuse to break even a six months’ agree-
ment within a few weeks by discovering faults in his
servant. Those who suck the blood of the miserable un-
employed deserve no consideration from law. Here is
exposed a direction in which it is the clear duty of a
democratic State to interfere with the “liberty of the
subject.”
* * * *
Outspoken opinion of Labor-member Stewart,
M.P. for Rockhampton North (Q.), on the Electric Power
and Light (shindykit) Bill : “It’s as big a swindle as the
land-grant scheme was.” To do it justice, it isn’t quite.
A municipality concerned has the right—the sole right
during 42 years—to compete with the “ existing com-
pany ” at any time. That is the Act’s only safeguard.
The provision that the “order in council may limit the
term, under which the “ existing eo.” is not bound to sell
to a municipal or local body, and that 42 years is only a
permissible maximum “ life,” is not worth considering—in
Queensland.
* * * *
Time that the Vie. Crown Law department
ceased to play the fool with public money. Thousands of
pounds have been squandered in make-believe bank pro-
secutions, and now the cable brings word that a hopeless
appeal by the V.C.L. department to the Privy Council
was dismissed last week. This represented a third unsuc-
cessful attempt to outrage common sense in the matter of
probate duty on the late William Pearson’s will. Pearson
(the Long Tunnel mine capitalist) died soon after the
Smellbourne financial crisis, and his executors valued his
deposit receipts in reconstructed banks at current market
rates. The Vic. Muster-in-Equity, who is the same thing
as the Crown Law department, claimed probate duty on
the face value of the documents in question. A fresh
struggle in the local courts resulted in a verdict for the
executors. Under the circumstances an appeal to the
Full Court was disgraceful. The appeal from the Full
Court to the Privy Council was a sin, and the men respon-
sible for it deserve to be dismissed likewise.

* * * *
Wyalong continues to show itself to the front as
a s mail, but steady, gold-produeinsr camp. The average
yield is quite up to, if not beyond, any other camp in Aus-
tralia, and the tonnage, though small, isgood enough, seeing
that it is produced without any of Bull’s gold to help it
along. Wyalong has had to grow out of itself, and had
the same thing been allowed in Westralia, it would not
now have Wyalong’s output, small as it is. A few recent
crushings Christmas Gift, 56 tons, 1320 z.; Neeld’s No. 1
tributors, 8 tons, 720 z. ; Bolte’s, firsts, 6 tons, 85oz. ;
seconds, 16 tons, 720 z.; Bantam, 16 tons, 33£0z.; Harry’s
Find, 119 tons, 201 oz. ; Welcome Stranger, 50 tons, 730 z. ;
Conway’s, 13 tons, 30oz.
* * • *
Great Western, Cobar, has crushed with the
new battery to date over 300 tons for 1200 z.— good enough
if the battery can keep up this average from a good big
face, and not by “ gouging ” out streaks in the ore-body. '

Fair average sample of N.S.W. mining news
“ lease has been issued to Crozier and sons for their
property at Mt. Maud, near Hockley, and final arrange-
ments for the purchase of it by a Sydney syndicate for
lt is expected that employment will be given
to fully 500 hands.” So a month’s wages would about eat
up an amount equal to the purchase money. “ From the
mine adjoining a small parcel of quartz was sent to the
Govt. Assayer. The return shows an average of 770 z. to
the ton.” An average of 770 z. ! And yet a man would
be hung for the murder of a local mining correspondent!
* * * *
Writes a Barrier correspondent There is going
to be an explosion on the Barrier some day that will go a
long way towards wiping it off the face of the globe.
Right on the borders of the town, on what is known as
the North Reserve, are a number of magazines, always
chock-full of dynamite, gelignite, gelatine, gunpowder, and
other fracteurs. Under the regulations but a certain
quantity can be stored in each magazine—the magazines
being small, galvanised-iron affairs—but the limit is a
liberal one, and there are lots of possibilities about the
business.
* * * *
It is the practice of a number of London finan-
cial and mining “ weeklies ” —born and fed by the W.A.
boom while it lasted, and now dying with it-i-to publish
big Westralian crushings, omit the poorones, and include
the tailings assays amongst the returns as if the gold had
been extracted and sent to the Mint. This idea is cer-
tainly very rich, considering that nine cases in ten the
statements made re gold in tailings are lies manufactured
to get around tight corners, and that in nine out of every
other ten cases whatever gold is left in tailings stays
there.
* * * •
From the Norseman, W.A. : “Things are not
too bright here, I am sorry to say. The impression is
that many of the mines will not re-open after the general
exemption. The open-call for prices of a large majority
of the shows has dwindled to almost nothing, a sign of
what the local people think of the field. Ignorant mining
managers have helped to damn it. The correct thing seems
to be to sink 50ft. and then start a fresh shaft until the
ground is fairly scratched, rooted and riddled. What do
you think of a dozen shafts on one lease ? Well, you have
it here. Money by thousands has been frittered away.
When men begin to talk of 15dwt. instead of 2*4oz. the
field may do something, but at present things look blue.”
—Qu AN DONG.

Eight tons of ore crushed at the Mt. Burgess
battery from Cashman’s Brilliant Reward mine for 14oz.
to the ton has caused that awful person, “ Smiler,” to
shriek. The mine is now called the Denver City. It was
abandoned as a duffer last year. Let us all hope that the
Denver City will continue to crush 14oz! rock, and will do
something bigger than an 8-ton lot next time.
* * * »
In his Nineteenth Century article Van Oss
calculates that the profit made by mining in Westralia
this year will be a miserable fraction of 1 per cent, on the
share capital. He describes the ways of the W.A. floater
in London, the lies of prospectuses, the paid—well-paid-
newspaper puffs, and the wiles of finance co’s. But the
warning comes too late. The promoter and boomster
have been and gone with the spoils. And there are too
many of them to gaol.
* * * *
So much has been said by newspapers concerning
the great richness of a W.A. mine called M‘Clay’s Welcome
that naturally the writer had a suspicion about it. The
W.A. newspaper-mine is usually cursed, any way. The
M‘C.W. is largely owned in Queensland, where contri-
buting shares are down to 9d. Boomed weekly by wire
and tipped religiously on the Sabbath.
* * * *
The booming of little Dr. Simon’s operations is
now largely confined to the Norseman mines, where, we
are told, crushing will commence in April, that there are
immense bodies of ore to work on, and success is assured.
Perhaps so, but alas ! we had the same story of his other
mines. The Australasian, a year ago, according to that
great authority, “ Smiler,” had any amount of lOoz. ore;
the Hill End, which cost some £3o,oooand has not crushed
for a year, also had wondrous prospects ; the New Vic-
toria was bought by the doctor for some £40,000 more
than a year ago, and nothing heard of it since Who
puts up all this money, and how do they feel about it up
to date ?
» • » *
Pups turning dog: “ The Londonderry North
and South gold-mine, for which the co. paid £lO,OOO and
upon which £5OOO has been spent, has been sold to Mr.
C. Kidman, Coolgardie, for £4OO cash.” Thus endeth the
fate of junipers. These blocks were jumped by Lyons
and Court from the findersof the Londonderry, who made
a technical mistake. The jumpers sold to a Perth crowd
of whom Alick —we forget his other name ! —was the
most prominent. Thj jumpers won all along the line.
The writer thought they would, but the line was a long
one. Tne Londonderry people, having dollied out £30,000
in gold and having a £lO,OOO deposit and their mine as
good as sold for £lBO,OOO, were in fighting trim, and they
soon found some lawyers to keep the game going. No
less than £50,000 was offered for the leases with the
proviso that the title be made unquestionable. The sud-
denness of the collapse of the Londondei ry golden hole
was only equalled by the celerity with which the £50,000
offer was withdrawn immediately afterwards. The
men who stood to the jumpers went down for £15,000,
and as they now sell for £4OO the loss by the famous jump
is just £14,600.

“ Moses,” of London Truth, again in the mud,
this time over the Hit or Miss mine (W.A.). He regarded
the good old tailings wheeze as satisfactory, and was glad
confidence had been restored and the shares again at par.
Since the two crushings of over 600 tons have averaged
4dwt., the mill has shut up and shares have dropped
50 per cent.

“Jupiter” writes, as a shareholder, wanting to
know how much of the £5OOO, received by A. J. Calvert
for the Mt. Prophecy mine in W.A., is available for dis-
tribution.
* * * *
The scapegoat of delay now in W.A. is “the
iniquity of the lahor conditions.” The representatives of
the English co.’s holding leases say that the excessive
stringency of the labor conditions is driving capital out
of the. country. Now, they have the same 3-acres-to-a-
man in W.A. as they have in Queensland and M.L.
Miners’ wages in most of the fields, say in Coolgardie,
Kalgoorlie, Menzies, Kanowna, and Black Flag and Broad
Arrow are £3 ss. a week. The English boomsters say
this is extortion. Yet they have appointed, and are still
appointing, mine managers at £l5OO a year. One of these
£l5OO a year men turned up lately, a week after
he was due, amongst the drunks at the police court, and
he would most likely have made an item in Lloyd’s
“ missing ” column had not a local director of the co.
which had engaged him happened on the Bench in his
J.P. capacity, and recognised the disreputable-looking
wanderer.
* * * »
Gladiators mine.—W.A. Prof. Nicholas’s official
report is not quite so glowing as this summary of it in the
Financial Times : “ Eleven shafts sunk depths varying
from 30 to 87ft. One reef proved for 1000 ft. to average
lift, in thickness. Ore in sight estimated to be worth not
less than £40,000, in fine gold shoots met with in the
proved 1000 ft. No ore stoped, can be cheaply mined and
milled. One of the richest mines in the district of Cool-
gardie.”
• * * *
“ Maorilander ” from Coolgardie to The Bul-
letin : —“ The failure to continue in richness, at a depth
considered ‘prospecting’ elsewhere, of such mines as
Bayley’s Londonderry, Mount Burgess, New Victoria,
Bendigo, Golden Bar, Tindalls, Empress of Coolgardie,
Big Blow, &c., which all ‘backed their opinion,’ anyway,
by erecting batteries, comparatively speaking, long ago,
does not strengthen any argument in favor of general
permanency of the Coolgardie lodes. A geological truth
is, that payable lodes do not ‘ live ’ to any great depth in
very hard country. Most of the ‘country’ enclosing
lodes on Coolgardie is diorite harder than the average
mining expert’s cheek.”

The Great Boulder now keeps all its glory to

others? Boulder Central Extended, Is. 2d. ; Boulder
Consols No. 1,2 s. s\d. ; Boulder North Extended, 2s. 3d.;
Imperial Boulder, 4s. sd. ; Boulder >4 Mile South, fid.
There are several more “Boulders” running from Is. a
dozen up to Is. each, and quite a number have dropped
out altogether.
* * * *
The awful collapse in London of W.A. mining-
stocks has received the attention of the big papers, while
the little weaklings born and nursed for no other purpose
than to shriek boom are again giving reasons. Water,
machinery, and the want of a railway, have been played
right out. Now it is all on account of the “ bears.” The
Daily Mail declares that W.A. market has always been
rotten and supported by trickery, and that there is only
one strong syndicate left in it. It sees no hope of the
slightest recovery, and says the only course for investors
drawn into it is to take what they can get and quit.
Statist says it was assumed that conditions existed
similar to the Rand, where calculations as to tonnage and
contents can be made on sound bases, but W.A. reefs
pinch and disappear so suddenly that no idea can be
gained as to what the life of a mine is. Daily Chronicle
trenchantly alludes to the fact that of mines representing
66 millions capital only one pays regular divs. It is satis-
fied that not one-tenth of the money sent to W.A. from
London will ever find its way back to those who sub-
scribed it. Altogether the howl of discontent has started
in dead earnest.
* * * *
Coolgardie, disappointed in the Menzies rail-
way, is now taking up with some vigor the long-proposed
scheme for the construction of an Esperance-Coolgardie
line, via Norseman. Vesper, of the Coolgardie Pioneer
—the man who wears his hair hanging down his back—
has framed some dozen formidable resolutions in connec-
tion with the matter, and is a shining light in the agita-
tion.
» • * *
Editor Bulletin. — I was down the General
Gordon mine lately, and, as the result of inspection, pro-
nounced her a wild cat of relentless ferocity. She may
pan out a couple of good crushings, and then, as an ex-
manager remarked to me in Melb. recently, “ Godelper !”
The rich shoot of opaline quartz cut out in the south
shaft on being sunk on, all the faces thereabouts being in
chloritic schist. Likewise various drives and cross-cuts
from the bottom of the north shafts as well as the engine
shaft, are all in the same unpromising material. The
lode formation is most erratic in its course, and is
traversed by small and uncertain leaders of opaline
quartz and ironstone from which the best prospects
are obtained. The winding plant is set down
close against the main shaft. When the engine starts
winding she will probably pull the entrails out of the
shaft and overturn the poppet-heads.—Yours, The Mug.
Kanowna, Nov. 22, 1896.
A truly wise provision in the new Victorian
Mines Hill is the one enacting that at least 2*4 per cent,
of Ihe profits shall, in every case, be reserved for the
development and opening up of the mine. The regular
procedure is to spend every available coin in dividends ;
then when the reef run's out to make calls for further
opening up, and when the shareholders grow tired of
call-paying to shut up the mine. There are dozens of
good mines in Victoria which paid glorious dividends,
and then when the “streak” gave out presently closed
up, for want of a little available cash with which to search
for a new formation.

The Mount Lyell Co., upon whose reserve the
lately-demolished Penghana (Tas.) township was built,
has posted a notice prohibiting the burn.t.-out residents
from re-building on the old site. As a result, the value
of building sites at Queenstown, the adjacent hamlet, has
gone up with a run, and the land-jobbers are having a
particulnrly merry Christmas.

* * * *
E. C. Lobb, the finder of one of the Queensland
goldfields, has just returned from New Guima, and while
returning was so impressed by the number of men he met
going Papua-wards in search of gold that he thinks some
warning is necessary. He admits good gold is being got
in New Guinea, that the island should be better explored
by expeditions with plenty of money, and that on several
of the small islands gold is being worked, some 1500 oz.
having been obtained last year by himself and others on
Marua. But the locality of the rush is a longwav inland
in the Mt. Soratchley district. To reach it, a swampy and
fever-stricken river has to be navigated, and numerous
high ranges have to be orossed, and, in the absence of a
track, this is next to impossible. The first prospectors
were assisted by about 100 natives, but so many aborigi-
nals died on the way back that they have retired from
this business. Of the few men who got there a big
percentage have died. The rush is not warranted by the
amount of alluvial gold got to date, and fully one-half of
the men who attempt it will never reach the field, or, at
least, will never come away alive.
* * * *
As soon as summer is on the wane, a rush to
New Guinea is a certainty. Many a poor devil will lose
the number of his mess in Papua, for the old Gulf remedies
of quinine and Worcestershire sauce are of little avail
against the Guinea fevers and malaria.
* * * *
The Macarthur-Forrest cyanide patent has col-
lapsed as far as South Africa is concerned. The High
Court of the Transvaal, from which there is no appeal,
has decided against it, and, as more gold is produced by
cyanidation in South Africa than in the rest of the world
(the local yield last year being over 600,OOOoz.), this deci-
sion may be regarded as the death-blow of the patent.
The fight against it will now be renewed throughout Aus-
tralasia.

Melbourne sweaters in the dressmaking and
millinery lines drive gaily through the Factories Act 2/6
minimum wage provision by sacking old hands and
exacting premiums from new ones. A board to fix the
wages and proportional numbers of skilled hands, im-
provers, beginners, and apprentices seems the only
possible cure.

CAMEO
Of late, the dark and gory horror hasn’t
been at all well distributed in Australia. Victoria
had Deeming and the decomposed wife under the
hearthstone. Then Maoriland had its Winton
THE SCORCHER’S CHILD.
FREDDY : “ Mother is going to buy me a bicycle, Mr. Wheeler.”
MR. W.: “ When you get bigger, Freddy—your legs are not big enough yet.”
FREDDY : “ But that will make them big. You ought to see ma’s leys since
she’s been riding.”
baby-farmer and the dead infant buried in the
flower-garden. Next N.S.W. had Mrs. Makin
and her gruesome cemetery in the back-yard, and
now it has the Glenbrook ghoul and his graveyard
in the Blue Mountains. Meanwhile Queensland,
S. A., Westralia and Tasmania haven’t raised one
really mysterious horror among them for quite a
long time. Also, N.S.W. has had two horrors to
Victoria’s one, which is doubtless owing to the
beneficent influences of Freetrade. A country
which languishes in the gloom and shackles of
Protection is bound to go short in its horror.
There is just a chance that that Union s.s. co
captain who boarded the Swanhilda and gave the
captain a pile of papers containing an account of
the Glenbrook mystery may have put his foot in
it badly. Supposing Butler, or whatever his name
may be, is on board : The captain may promptly
shove him down below in irons, and keep him
there until he is wanted. Or, again, he may do
nothing at all except let the news leak out, and
the wanted man may suicide, and thus spoil a
grand trial and a really beautiful hanging. Or, if
the vessel gets very close to land anywhere before
reaching California, Butler may take a desperate
chance and get ashore. Or, again, he may still
be aboard when the Swanhilda arrives at the
Golden Gate, but, being warned of the trouble to
come, he may start promiscuous shooting at the
local police, and, as a result, he may be scragged
in a strange land, far from the refining and
softening influences of home, and the moral lesson
may be wholly lost to this country. Altogether,
The Bulletin regards that Union captain as a
foolish, meddlesome person whose conduct in
touching the paint to see if it was dry may end by
spoiling the picture altogether.
« * * *
The much-wanted and discussed prospector
suspected of the “ mountain murders ” seems to
have been penurious as well as bloodthirsty. For
instance, he struck out two words in his decoy
“ advt.” at Newcastle to save 6d. His fatal mis-
take of personating Lee Weller was due to a desire
to save passage-money. “On the voyage one is
always making a bit,” was his excuse for not pay-
ing for a passage. Then, after “making a rise,”
he put up at 6d. restaurants and sailors’ homes.
People marvel at any man assassinating others
for the sake of <£s or so, but the case is not with-
out precedent: Nicholls and Lester, for instance,
killed their victims for even less. From at least
one they got only a very poor silver watch, sold
for 135., and a box of olothes on which they realised
18s. 6d. Like the mountain murderer, they car-
ried on a wholesale business and went for “small
profits and quick returns.” Even if the mountain
man killed and buried only three people a fort-
night and netted A 5 per head on them, his income
would exceed the official pay of a member of
Parliament.

Xmas and New Year’s Eve are times of
fairly universal joy and benevolence, but one
section of society never sleeps easily in its bed on
either of these occasions. The landlord fraternity
dreads the all-night crowds and übiquitous con-
veyances, the confusion of which furnishes the
finest opportunities of the year for moon-shooters.
7 * *
Xmas is the time when a good, kind gentle-
man who 'Wishes to donate one pound sterling to
a hospital, sends his cheque for the amount to a
newspaper and gets an advertisement to that
effect instead of dropping a sovereign in the
hospital-box as he passes the institution on his
way to business.
The somersault cure is the
very latest remedy for fatness.
When a stout lady of 50 or so learns
to turn somersaults it is alleged
that the exercise has a fascination
which makes her keep at it, and by
daily repetition she grows slender
and muscular. In the early stages
of the business she has naturally
to be helped over by an instructress
—that is to say, she lies down flat,
and an athletic lady seizes her by
the ankles, and stands her on her
head and then lets her drop. But
after a while she gains confidence
in herself and in her knicker-
bockers, and whirls her stout limbs
over her head “ on her own,” with-
out the slightest difficulty. When
she has learned to do this act
without assistance and without
coming down with a stunning
whack on her front part, the fas-
cination is said to take hold of her,
and after that she is liable to keep

Dear Bulletin,—The B.M.
Herald, in noticing the newly-
arrived pictures exchanged with
Melb. Public Gallery, descants
loftily on the refining influences of
pictorial art. There may be some
truth in the notion, but, as far as
I’m concerned, it doesn’t apply.
Emerging from the Public Gallery
the other day, in company with a
friend with whom I had been view-
ing the exhibits, we differed over
their merits so widely that the dis-
cussion ended in the most murder-
ous fight I have engaged in during
my lifetime. —X.Y.Z.
S.A. medical men have consistently boy-
cotted Dr. Cookburn because he refused to resign
from the Kingston Ministry when the Hospital
disputes were at their height.
* * * *
The Adelaide Hospital disputants are now
enjoying a forced rest. With the prorogation of
Parliament the select committee expired, and as
the Govt, shut down on the petition for a Royal
Commission, matters will be quiet until June at
least unless something extraordinary happens.
Further, the newly-imported doctors will arrive
from England in a few days, and the chances of
the striking doctors look less promising daily.
* * * *

pute has been to lay bare the flimsy qualifications
under which some medicos practise, and to induce

Mrs. Windeyer proposes to establish a
“ Queen’s Record ” maternity hospital in Sydney
next year. Surely, after this, a grateful sovereign
can do no less than elevate hubby to the coveted
Judicial Committeeship. Meanwhile, the mat is

* * *
In Westralia, the other
day, the engine of an alleged
express parted from the tram,
and the driver went on in bliss-
ful unconsciousness, leaving the
passengers stranded among the
scrub. When they got out to
investigate, they found th it the
train had been tied to the en-
gine with rope, and the rope
had parted through old age.
* * *
Chinese medicines on regu-
lar sale in Bendigo (Vic.) in-
clude such alleged remedies as
asbestos, stalactite, staghorns,
lizard skins, and dog’s flesh,
oyster-shells, loadstone, talc,
gold-leaf and silver-leaf, ver-
digris, calcspar, pearls, bear’s
gall, rhinoceros bones, and tur-
tle-shell. For poison-cases ele-
phant’s skin or ivory powder is
the favorite prescription. Dog’s-
flesh and stalactites are sold as
tonics.
The Australian detective
is about the most reliable dead-
head in the community. He
gets almost everything gratis
even his Epsom salts. The
other night, a Sydney chemist
was furiously rung out of his
bed at 2 a.m., and came reluct-
antly downstairs, to find his
customer was a D., who
wanted sixpenn’orth of salts
on the never.”

THE MEN ON THE FRINGE.
They sail to the out seas deep,
They tramp to the lands afar,
And some may find the land of sleep
And some the Morning Star.
The world is wide and the seas are deep,
And the life of the city is poor and cheap,
And though some reach marble and some the heap,
Yet the Wander-Star knows not rest or sleep,
For the Fringe must sow what the Waiters reap,
Led by the Wander-Star.
STEELE GREY.
A TOAST.
Death holds a cup
That holds the doom of Fate ;
Life holds a cup—
And Death comes soon or late.
Let’s drink of Life’s !
The toast ? A death to Fate !

From July 18, 1824, to December 30,
1826, 76 executions took place at Hobart, and 30
at Launceston. One hundred and six judicial
murders in 17 months. At the end of that period
Parson Horton wrote to Sydney for help, saying :
“ The wickedness of the people exceeds all descrip-
tion.” Rather!
Crowned heads of Europe have always
given their royal patronage to Ed. Pinaud, of
Paris, but never so much as lately ; his perfumes
are now recognised as incomparably the best in
Europe The biggest hits of Ed. Pinaud have
been ‘ Violette Reine” (quite different from
“ Rhine Violet ”), and “Preciosa,” the sweetest
and most delicate perfumes ever made ; in fact
they are known everywhere as the quintessences
of the richest flowers grown in France, and are
sure to be rushed by everybody.*

whereupon an employe of the Meat Co. knocked

to the chemist s assistance. Whereupon the case
got into court, and a magistrate who was much
too civilised for the great West fined the butcher-
man <£3 10s., and stated that it was very wrong*
indeed to carry a dead nigger about in a meat-
cart, however recent the deceased might be, and
that the practice of jumping on people who o'bject
to dead niggers on the meat required to be put
down. And Roebourne in general is now holding
its nose and regarding its breakfast chop with a
cold shudder.
A stable is used as a lock-up at Queens-
town (Mt. Lyell, Tas.), and refractory drunks are
chained up to the mangers. One night recently
the improvised “ chokey ” was occupied by a
drunk, a horse, a pig, and a corpse.

Northern Queensland
are exceeding partial to

is said to be that the

palatable, owing to rice
being their staple article
of diet. There is now a
nurnerousChinese popu-
lation in the north of
Queensland, and scores
of them who have ven-
tured beyond the con-
fines of civilisation have
been captured and de-
voured by the natives.
But our London con-
temporary misses the
sequel, which is now
ancient history in
Australia. Wily
John fed up on plas-
ter-of-Paris and
killed off the unsus-
pecting nigs, by the
thousand from indi-
gestion.
* * *
A Bulletin cor-
respondent :
Life in this Q. district
is very primitive and
idyllic. The place is
chiefly inhabited by Ger-
man. families, a very in-
dustrious, economical,
and self-reliant class,
and very prolific. With-
in a radius of 20 miles
from me there are nine
(unmarried) girls who
have had one youngster
apiece. They go back
to service in the city,
and leave the cherub
with a married sister or
grandmother. It is all
taken as a matter of
course. There is no
baby-farming here: they
are all strong and
healthy. Lately one
girl came home on a
visit to her mother ; left
twins; and went to
service again. There
never was but one main-
tenance case, and the
hank - clerk concerned
squared it by giving
the old man £5O. Nothing to boast of, perhaps, but it’s
better than Sheridanisation.
* * * •
The other day a deputation of Vic. police
officers waited upon Chief Sec. Peacock to protest
against the further promotion of one Sergeant
Fowler, who, it would seem, has profited unduly
through the influence of baronet Clarke, or because
he was an amateur sergeant-major in the Ruperts-
wood battery before he joined the police as a drill-
instructor. Peacock promised to consider the
possibility of checking this fortunate young man’s
flight over the heads of older, harder-working
sergeants, and The Bulletin herewith records
Peacock’s promise for the reason that the little
matter was not reported at all in the respectable
Sup-press.
LEARNING HIS TRADE.
TOT: “ Wotch yer doin’, man?"
MAN : “ Goin’ to cane a chair.”
TOT: “ S’pose you’re teamin’ to be a schoolmaster."
Apparently the days of the lunatics who
light their pipes with bank-notes are not quite
over. The other day a Brisbane bacchanalian
entered a pub. and ate a £5 note for the edification
of bystanders. Becoming uproarious, he was run
in by the police, and, when sobered up, proved to
be worth precisely 4Ad. Perhaps he fancied
himself a Queensland M.L.A. engaged (to use a
Ministerial phrase) “in digesting a new Bank
Bill.”
DENTISTRY IN THE BACKBLOCKS.
OLD FASTENON : “Once I git ’em set they can kick and be d — d. They
can’t kick me! ”
The other day a demented artist drove up
to the gates of Melb. Government House for the
purpose of dying, if possible, in Lord Brassey’s
arms. He had lost all desire for life, the stranger
explained, and his beau ideal for decease was to
fall into a long, last sleep upon his Excellency’s
bosom. The vice-regal flunkeys gave the crazy
artist into police custody, instead of conducting
him to Lord Brassey. One glance at that prosaic
nobleman would probably have cured the poor
fellow on the spot.
» » *
In Monte Video (South America), accord-
ing to the report of the policeman who brought
back Geake, the pious Gentleman One who
absconded from his bail, legal pioceedings are
conducted on the most courteous principles.
When the prisoner is brought into court, if he is
a reasonably clean and gentlemanly prisoner, the
judge shakes hands with him and possibly gives
him a light for his cigar, and inquires after his
health. The prisoner reciprocates and trusts that
the judge is well. Also the judge, the lawyers on
both sides, the principal officials and the witnesses
shake hands and exchange greetings. The prisoner
is courteously offered a chair, and then business
proceeds. In the course of the trial the judge
and the prisoner address each other in terms of
courteous equality. The Yic. policeman was filled
with scorn at these formalities, and the Melbourne
press talks of Uruguayan justice with contumely.
In Australia, also, the law professes to regard the
untried prisoner as an innocent man who is the
equal of any other citizen, and it acts up to its
theory by ordering him to stand up in a dirty
enclosure behind a row of spikes, while a huge
policeman stands alongside to suppress him
instantly if he attempts to speak to the judge in
any terms except those of abject grovel. And
even if the accused was the most perfect gentleman
in the land, any attempt to shake hands with his
Honor would probably lead to his immediate
execution in the backyard.
* * * *
Highway robbery is pretty well extinct in
the Australian provinces' but the churches and
benevolent societies still do a good deal of robbery
under alms.
In a N.S.W. country gaol, the other day,
a chaplain coming to deliver his customary warn-
ing to the wicked to flee from the wrath to come
was closely followed by the visiting gaol dentist.
Latter, being pressed for time, besought that any
who wanted his attentions might be excused from
attendance at the service. The request being
granted, every man-jack of the chaplain’s flock
discovered that he was the owner of a troublesome
molar.
TWELVE O’CLOCK.
A last farewell to Ninety-six,
The old year fading out of view;
Some incidental conscience-pricks
Remind us that it once was new.
What vows were made ! and, O, how few
Have left a lasting trace behind
To indicate the road to Heaven.
Salvation, though, we hope to find
In Ninety-seven.
Fain would each weary bardlet quote,
With well-considered sob or sigh,
Pathetic lines he dreamt he wrote
Whilst Ninety-six went rolling by.
Those verses, that would never die,-
Remain unborn : their music rings
As yet upon no waking ear ;
But we will do gigantic things
This glad New Year.
Here’s to the girl whom we loved best
A dozen vanished months ago !
Perchance the passion then professed
Still keeps an erring heart aglow
With thoughts of dearest So-and-so.
Well, here’s Her health, who e’en to-day
Imparts to life unworldly leaven !
Good wine—good girls--good luck—Hooray
For Ninety-sheven !
Melb.
E.F.
WE OFTEN FEEL LIKE THAT.
The sea, a silver hunting-case,
Shuts in God’s watch, the sky,
Jewelled in many holes, that Space
Move not through Time awry.
The headland, like a sofa couched,
Is resting on the Night,
And where its rocky nose is crouched
The waves snore calm delight.
Upon the beach are men who wait
On Providence and sharks :
The moon observes their anguished state,
But does not make remarks.
I dream of epics, odes sublime,
Lyrics without a flaw,
A sonnet-pearl whose stately chime
Shall trance the world in awe :
Exquisite true-love knots Of words
Tied tight in tenderness ;
A song of stars, a song of birds,
With each note a caress.
There is poetry in the air,
Poetry in the sea,
Poetry in the heavens fair—
But, alas ! there’s none in me.
AS QUAINT OLD HERRICK MIGHT
HAVE SUNG.
My Julia’s eye is sweet and shy
As ever was a summer sky ;
Her iris is the heavenly blue
That shines the fleece-white cloud-bank through,
And I the earth that looks up at
The summer heavens beneath her hat.

A WOMAN’S LETTER .
Sydney, Dec. 29, 189 G.
Mr Dear Myee—
Boxing Day races at Randwick were surprisingly
festive, and more like a Spring meeting than a
Summer one for crowds and dresses. Everybody
had on her best frock, which ran to most expensive
materials, silks and muslins with no signs of wear
and tear. The prettiest figure there was swathed
in a mysteriously-folded bodice of white silk with
no visible means of “ hooking-up,” and a magpie
skirt, and belonged to a tall, slender, city waitress.
A lovely shot silk with puffy chiffon sleeves was
carried about, to the envy of surrounding woman-
kind, by an oofy-looking person with touzled hair.
Fruit dangling from hats gave a “ cheer-up-
cherries-are-ripe ” appearance to the wearers.
Golden hair was largely worn ; more than one
woman looked as if her front name was Maudie
and her second name merely pro tern. The girls
seemed nice, but not altogether Nice, though one
in pink muslin and an artless fichu might come
under both categories. Also there was a military
magnate’s daughter in white, whose parent made
himself reminiscent of India’s torrid clime by
carrying around a huge green-and-white sunshade.
The gilded (and otherwise) youth were mostly
gorgeous in the very latest variety, dark-blue
flannel variously striped—some white, some red.
This draping is largely taking the place of blue
serge, because it is just as smart and is washable.
It was invented for the revolting Guards who
were sent to Bermuda a short time ago ; the lordly
officers could not be expected to conform to out-
landish fashions and give up their style, so an
enterprising tailor was inspired with a great idea
combining cut with coolth, and now the new
notion has caught on everywhere.
The wind on Saturday turned Randwick stand
and lawn into a shoe-show. Vast No. 6’s peeped
coyly out from beneath many a graceful gown and
spoiled the effect, and the woman with shabby
boots below a gorgeous top-dressing was given
away at once. The prize for pretty feet would
have easily fallen to a popular comedy actress,
who, with her sister and brother-in-law, defied the
wind in front of the stand, proudly conscious of
hoofy perfection, which high heels and pointed
toes only served to accentuate.
On Xmas Eve the pantomime at Her Majesty’s
was in rather a chaotic condition of pink tights,
beaded tunics recalling memories of Jimmy
M‘Mahon’s celebrated “bead ballet,” red-legged
imps, green Nautch girls, and general gauziness.
The audience softly folded itself up like the Arab
and silently melted away by large instalments
before the end, for the show didn’t look as if it
were coming to any conclusion at very little to
midnight. The d.e. was occupied largely by
friends and relatives of the performers. An
Amurkan girl showed triumphantly how to look
cool in a scarlet blouse—a very useful wrinkle, as
red is the coming color. Eva Clements, as second
boy, wore a purple suit with huge sleeves, which
made her loom larger than Stella Esdaile, as Boy
No. 1, who wore no sleeves and very little tunic,
and therefore looked comparatively important.
The only stage woman who could successfully put
all her four limbs on exhibition at one and the
same time was Addie Conyers.
Minna Phillips, tied up in gauze bags,
looked Turkish, and sang with Sinbad the prettiest
and most catchy duet in the piece. There is very
little haunting music in this year’s panto. You
don’t dream about its warble, and hear it on the
midnight breeze. “ Good old Santa Claus ” and
“ The Greatest Show on Earth ” are the only two
tunes that stick after one hearing. W. Crackles
has piany new hats, but nary a new song. Mrs.
Lucifer, the new importation, possesses some
pretty skirts, a “ vurry Amurrican ” accent, an
unoriginal dance-step, and a husband who holds
the world’s record for high-kicking. In the
variety scene the girls all wear assorted colors in
tights and look like a box of Xmas crackers.
An Australian girl tells that when staying in
England she visited an unfashionable watering-
place with some friends, and at first felt rather
shy about using the only out-of-season bathing-
machine in the place, said implement being run
by an aged longshoreman. A half-century of
gazing at dipping womankind had made him
blase, and when he saw the new-comer’s hesita-
tion he wearily remarked : “ Lor’, Miss, don’t
mind me. I’ve been at this business 40 year and
legs is as common to me as breakfus’.” A single
visit to the Panto, is calculated to produce the
same feeling, there is so much nether limb in it.
The Centennial Hall was crowded at the usual
“Messiah” performance. A heaviness, as if
hecatombs of roast-goose and mountains of plum-
pudding were piled on its chest, always hangs over
that regulation Xmas-night production. There is
only one thing in music more weighty, and that
is Bach’s Xmas Oratorio, and fortunately nobody
can live through that, and so it is never per-
formed. The man who takes in the “ Messiah ”
as he takes in his dinner on the great annual fes-
tival of over-feeding, is usually the stodgv person
who gloats over the wailing goose” in his
back-yard for weeks before he performs the
Lee Weller act on it at Christmas Eve
and then feasts with iron nerve and jaw upon the
murdered fowl. He is of the soulless sort who
would keep a parrot and train it to cry like a
child to the joy of his neighbors, and has framed
texts hanging in his sitting-room, and a favorite
deceased dog stuffed on a stand, and glaring down
at him with a twisted smile and cold, glassy eye-
balls. Next century, when cremation is “ in,” his
descendant will arrange all his dead relatives in
pots along his dining-room mantelpiece, and point
out to his guests the cinerary urns of dear Aunt
Jane and the ash of Cousin William.
A Xmas treat to sailors was given last week by
some well-meaning but unworldly missioners who
regard Jack in the foc’s’le as a gentle babe, a
cross between a Sunday scholar and an uncon-
verted Hindoo. So they gave 300 of him a tea-
meeting in the George-street rooms : and after
swallowing enough weak fluid to float an ironclad,
the patients were allowed to run away and play
till the subsequent concert began. It was of the
mildly religious type. The nautical feeling was
intensified by a hornpipe benignly smiled upon by
some kind ladies who also patted poor Jack upon
the back, so to speak, and told him to be a good
little boy. The pious command was emphasised
by the present of a Xmas card, a cracker, and a
buttonhole bouquet. Why seamen should be thus
specially favored with infantile gifts is hard to
say. _ Nobody would think of organising a
mission to other trades and decorating a
long procession of, say, fish hawkers or
auctioneers or bookmakers with floral tributes.
But the merchant seaman is expected to
treasure up the little mementoes of his joyfully
riotous Xmas treat, and as he folds his hands
meekly and says “ Now I lay me down to sleep ”
before turning into his bunk every night at sea,
to press reverently to his lips the faded flowers
given him by the kind ladies at the Sydney Mis-
sion Hall.
Miss Emily Hughes, as the deserted wife in
“Tommy Atkins,” begins her career gaudily in a
white gown topped off with bright blue, but dis-
cards color with convention, and takes her father's
curse and her subsequent way through the cold
and snowy world without a hat. Instead, she
wears a stiff, wooden baby wrapped in a shawl.
In the course of the play she discards baby,
too, but the infant subsequently turns up
and is re-united to the harrowed mother, who,
however, never gets back to her pretty frocks,
which is a pity, for Miss Hughes has good taste,
and her stage-gowns give many a hint to observant
women in front of the house. The rest of the
drama is mostly soldier and brass-band playing
“ Tommy Atkins ” on the slightest provocation,
and in 15 different keys at once.
An article in a recent S.M. Herald pulls
down the long-idealised “ Melican gal” from her
pedestal, and tells us poor benighted “ colonial ”
women we mustn’t admire her any more. She is
too selfish and exacting, and makes hubby work
too hard for the diamonds which she wears. It is
all the American man’s fault in domesticating his
angel. Too practical to worship the sex in the
abstract as Venus Victrix or Madonna, he piles
all his idealism on the women nearest him, with
the result, so croakers say, that the type is de-
teriorating through over-much indulgence. That
is because the American woman can do as she
pleases. There are many grumblers about who
honestly think that all the sex should be im-
prisoned in harems and face-veils still. Liberty
can never hurt anybody. “ A woman to-day may
do whatever her body and soul will let her.” But
those two limitations are so substantial that in
3000 A.D. her boundary-line will be very probably
what it is to-day and has been for centuries.
At the Cri. on Saturday evening—first night of
“Nancy and Co.” —there was a smart-looking
house. Stage frocks in the play are unremarkable.
Mrs. Brough, as Nancy Brasher, wears a noughts-
and-crosses pattern silk, pretty pink and black
bodice and flowery head-piece; Emma Temple
has an unbecoming fawn canvas skirt and a rather
elaborate long bodice, while Kitty Hardy fascin-
ates in girly muslins. G. Titheradge makes the
jealous husband Brasher put much expression into
a white top hat, and Percy Ward is a cute little
Willieboy with a street-sweeping coat.
Sydney holiday-makers are a joyously Bohemian
crowd ; they spend the day in harbor picnics and
tumble hilariously into theatres at night with their
hands full of droopy greenery and the marks of
jam-tarts still on their frocks. Even in the dressy
parts of the house they consider a blouse and skirt
and wind-blown hair and a hat hastily unpinned
in the cloak-room as full dress. Only here and
there do bare shoulders and arms dot the d.c. like
trussed Xmas turkeys in a poulterer’s window.
The Melb. Blind Asylum boom worked up by
Her Ex. of Vic. has panned out badly. When it
became known that she was in the movement, the
floodgates of correspondence were opened, and it
rained letters for 40 days and 40 nights. Now
Her Lx. has fled away to sea to escape from the
charitable monster she has created, and which was
fast qualifying her for the insane asylum. The
task of reading the letters is now deputed to the
aides or the boy in buttons, and the loyal sub-
scriber who sends along his or her guinea in hopes
of receiving a vice-regal signature at the bottom
of the receipt is falling in badly. It was the
Herculean task of writing thousands of begging
k-tters for St. Mary’s building fund which helped
to kill Archbishop Vaughan. A former Governor’s
wife was lazier or wiser about her pet charity
She had an autograph letter lithographed on Govt

mg families used to leave the epistle lying* care-
lessly about their drawing-rooms to delude visitors
into the belief that they were in direct and per-
sonal communication with “Yours sincerely
Hersee Hopetoun.” J ’

Miss Beatrice Griffiths is a prodigy who has
grown up. She began her career as a musician at
seven, and still continues to love her art and ta
of it with a capital A. Most infant phenomena
either languish and fade beautifully out of exist-
ence before they have reached their teens, or take
a violent reaction and become prominent foot-
ballers in adult years, or turn into tea-shop girls.
But, under Kowalski’s training, Miss G. has
still continued her devotion to music and will take
a trip to Europe to learn still more. A benefit
concert is to be given her about the middle of Jan.
Nicholson and Co. send me “ May ” polka, by
Sydney Turnbull, a tripping, jiggy tune with
plenty of swing and go and well-marked rythm.
The library of the late Mrs. Carl Fischer, auc-
tioned a few days ago, covered several large tables
in the sale-room, and was a fairly average collec-
tion, which brought no fancy prices. Two
or three really valuable books, which should not
have been sacrificed, were the “Breeches Bible,
“Old Ballads,” the Art Journal, Hogarth, and
Gilroy. Books of this sort are usually slaughtered
at auction. If a careful selection had been made
by an expert, and sent to London for sale, probably
half-a-dozen rare volumes would have realised
thrice as much as the whole collection brought
here.
Many Xmas-pudding mishaps are told me, but
the saddest yarn of all is about the man who
dropped a golden sov. into the unboiled mixture
as a pleasant surprise for the finder. After the
pudding had all been eaten and no coin was re-
vealed he observed that there was “something
funny about the stuff,” and asked who had got the
coin. His missis suddenly remembered having
exchanged samples with the next-door woman
over the fence. So sadness darkened that Xmas.
“Do men admire small-waisted women?” is a
reiterated silly-season puzzle. Why not reverse
it—“ Do small-waisted women admire men ? ” If
so, who and how many? It is like “Do bats eat
cats ? ” in “ Alice in Wonderland ” ; or “ What is
the cause of the lightning ? ” or “ Which would
you rather do, or go fishing? ” or “ Why is a bit
of string ? ” or any other hoary-headed query.
Tight-lacing does as much service as the pin which
saved lives by not being swallowed, because it
kills off girls silly enough to do it, and so works
for the survival of the fittest. Besides, nobody
does tight-lace .now, because bicycling has killed
the practice.
Remote suburbanites have been victimised lately
by a schemer who trades upon feminine bargain-
hunger. His stock-in-trade is a smart buggy con-
taining a convincingly show lady, some samples
of dry goods and a large quantity of shoddy.
When the house-mother eyes him suspiciously,
he offers alarming sacrifices (as per sample) from a
firm in town, obliged to clear out at any cost, and
invariably receives an order for silk stockings at
2s. 6d. a pair, or some other equally tempting
“ line.” Then a thought seems suddenly to strike
the trader, and he brings forth from his trap
some “ not quite such good quality, but still
remarkably cheap” tweed or serge. The victim
usually tumbles in and buys vast quantities of
useless stuff ; the shoddy-merchant drives off,
and, of course, never returns with his wonderful
bargains, and the shoddy serge is angrily scissored
by the woman of the house, and has about a fort-
night’s brief career as school-knickers for the boys.
An up-country town is just now the scene of a
heartless deception practised on its guileless and
unsuspecting Nicest, who hailed the advent of
another young man in the place with joy. He
was a stranger, and the first families took him in
because he is a handsome, clean-shaven, Johnnyish
youth, expert at tennis, flirtation, dancing, and
billiards. When he hints at his probably early
departure, lamentations arise on all sides. But
when he does finally go out into the infinite, and
Nicest discover that they have been honoring
the boots of the chief hotel of a distant town
more wailing will be heard in that district.
It is a tiring thing to have to live up to a label,
but most women have to do so in this weary
world, or get counted out in the great game of
life. In every large family of girls each one “does
something now-a-days, and is branded accord-
ingfly- One is 1 artistic ” —defies every botanical
law in her waratahs and flannel-flowers painted
on wood panels ; another is “ literary,” and sends
shrieks in three chapters, peppered with dashes
and asterisks, to an editor who is craving onlv for
solid facts; while “dear Maud” is “so philan-
thropic, and gets up, with the assistance of her
friends like-minded, entertainments for benighted
factory-girls. 6

by an almost entirely new cast. The new people
that Frank Thornton has brought out, though
taken singly they would not, perhaps, be able to
hold a candle to some old favorites, seem about as
refreshing as a rainstorm could be to this parched
and sun-struck city. ‘ Miss Brown ’ is obviously
a daughter of Charley’s aunt, but she is by no
means as amusing as her aged relative, ana has
not nearly so convincing a personality. Capt.
Courtenay’s make-up, however, is perhaps cleverer
than Lord Babberley’s was, and Miss Brown’s
blue serge with scarlet sash, in the second
act, might deceive anyone. Noney Seabrooke,
a dainty brunette with an insignificant vocal
organ, has an insinuating little manner, besides
which she looked radiantly pretty in the second
and third acts. Her gowns were all simple prints
and muslins with smart satin sashes tied (as all
up-to-date ceintures should be) at one side. Bessie
Rignold, as the girl from Demerara, made up her
face coffee-colored and her eyes deep purple, but
she wore artistic and appropriate gowns. Her
first frock of orange and brown was smart, and she
struck a splendid color note in the crimson damask
with gold sequin trimming of Act 3. Herbert
Terry, as the music-master, spoke like a- faded
Gecko, and looked like a shaven and bleached
Svengali. An incident in a story of Kipling’s is
cheekily ‘lifted’ bodily into the play, with no
kind of acknowledgement. Robert Buchanan,
who once spoke of ‘ little Kip ’ with abhorrence as
the ‘awful Mr. Kipling,’evidently considers the
Anglo-Indian good enough to steal from.
“ Miss Nellie Veitch, Melb.’s most popular and
successful elocutionist, leaves for a trip to Europe
early this month. The Austral Salon, of which
the silver-voiced Nellie is a prominent member,
farewells her somewhat elaborately next Thurs-
day. Miss Veitch may be said to have set the
fashion of “speaking pieces” in Vic.—a fashion
which has brought in its wake much suffering to a
comparatively innocent public, although it may
help a little to cure Australia junior of his native
habit of mumbling and his tired drawl. Lucky
Westralian specs have enabled Miss Veitch to
throw up her teaching for a time, and treat
herself to a Continental tour.
“AMelb. furrier lately received a large order
from the Spanish Government for opossum rugs
for the troops in Cuba. Fortunately for a
languishing fur-trade, a Cuban insect, with an
unspellable name, finds the soldiers’ blanket
so palatable that he consumes most of it
in one night, and, as he has not yet learned
to digest fur, and as Australian opossum is
the cheapest fur that an insolvent Government
can buy, poor old ’poss gets a boom on his own.
Black opossum is considered a superb fur by con-
noisseurs, and if there were not such quantities
of it available, it would take its place with the
sable and seal and other furry aristocrats. Fur-
yielding seals are becoming so rare that sealers
are bewildered at the numbers of alleged sealskin
jackets produced each season. Much - abused
bunny holds part of the key to this enigma.
“ Modern curates are, as a rule, meek, neglected
men, who buy their slippers and comforters ready-
made or go without. One of the Pres. Assembly
orators, the bachelor incumbent of a metropolitan
kirk, always commands attention, if only as one
of the few survivors of an almost obsolete type—
the clerical lady-killer. He once got into hot
water for complaining piteously at a suburban
conversazione of his perils at the hands of his un-
married lady-parishioners. At the Assembly, the
other night, he announced to a hard-headed hall-
full that he had a proposal of marriage to make
which the ladies would value all the more when he
told them it was his first proposition of the kind.
(Dissent). In the end, the conceited creature ex-
plained that he wished to coalesce the blanket-
guild with the medical-comfort league—or some
such twaddle.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.

Sydney’s wild-rabbit and bottle-merchants
now take their “ donahs ” out driving on Sundays
in a “four-bob robbo,” i.e., sulky. Now, what is
the derivation of “ robbo ” ?
The Eye-sight measure sent per post by
the inventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough,
the well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney,
is a wonderful little contrivance which will enable
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection
such as long sight, short sight, old sight. Full
particulars from Messrs. Greenfield and Barra,
dough,*

Tasmania owns a blind axeman who can
put a “scarf ” into a standing block as truly and
dexterously as most men. He falls a tree —his son
tells him how it leans—butts the logs, crosscuts,
sharpens his saw, makes his mall and uses his
paling-knife well enough t<p support a wife, and
six children. He lost his sight twenty years ago
from a gunshot accidentally received while wattle-
bird shooting. “The last objects I saw were a
gum-tree and a wattle-bird,’ pathetically remarks
the old timber-slasher.
Alfred Deakin, M.P., now does his thirty-
five miles and more on a bicycle with the gusto
he evinces in all pursuits, except politics. Barris-
tenally he has been almost continuously employed
in the biggest cases. When the Federation ques-
tion gets a little bit hotter a recrudescence of
political energy may be expected in one who is
somewhat blase, having exhausted the possibilities
of the big intellectual game at a very_earlv age.
It is not given to everyone to be Chief Secretary
of his native province at 24.

Gov. Lamington is earning popularity in
Brisbane (Q.) by painful decrees and slow. “ Old ”
Norman was a popular idol—-though compara-
tively a poor man. Women wept in numbers on
the occasion of his departure, while men beered
each other maudlinly. Atop of this, never had
there been in Brisbane such a hysterical hooray of
greeting as welcomed Lamington (all on account
of Norman), who was reputed to be enormously
wealthy. His refusal to donate to the Eight-
hours’ movement set the first recoil; when, on a
furthercomprehension of matters, H.E. did forward
a cheque, the Eight-hours people stiffly returned
it. But gradually, very gradually, hi's name is
being uttered without sneer or contempt, and the
present verdict is that “ though not so good as he
looks, he isn’t as bad as he first seemed to be.”
George Fisher, described as the ablest and
most useless politician inMaoriland, who has just
got back to Parliament as a Government supporter,
is a man of many parties. Nine years ago he was
Minister for Education in the Continuous Minis-
try, but no one could work with the Wellington
Ishmaelite, and he left after 18 months with a fine
impression of Atkinson’s hob-nailed boot on his
coat-tails. This is only metaphor, by the way.
Atkinson was a hard kicker-out, and a confirmed
nigger-driver, and no man who wouldn’t drive
easily ever stayed in his Cabinet. After that
Fisher was a Liberal. Then in ’9l he was a Labor-
member, but he speedily cooled off, possibly
because he wasn’t offered a portfolio in the Ballance
Government. By ’92 he was a modified Tory
again. In ’93 he stood as an independent
and was defeated, and everybody said his political
career was ended. And now in ’96 Fisher is a
Liberal M.H.R. once more. What he will be next
year no one knows, but his amazing vitality always
keeps him more or less on top. A kedge-anchor
wouldn’t sink George Fisher permanently.
In the Melbourne Review of July, 1876,
appeared an admirable article, entitled “ An Epi-
sode in Californian Banking.” A graphic account
was given of the career of the founder of the
Bank of California, William Chapman Ralston,
who seemed to have been a popular idol because
he disobeyed the rules of old-fashioned banking.
Ralston lent money to brokers to gamble in scrip,
to “ land-sharks ” to cut up estates and build ter-
races, to building societies for loan on long mort-
gages, and to the daring spirits of San Francisco
generally for speculative purposes. A ’Frisco
paper attacked Ralston and his bank, laid bare his
and its affairs, and thereby induced a run which
ended in a smash, and a re-construction in the
absenoe of Ralston, who suicided. The “ episode ”
bristled with trenchant criticisms of heterodox
banking methods, and sneered at the people of
’Frisco for remaining true to the errant Ralston’s
memory. Read in 1896, one would imagine the
great Balzac’s spook had sent the “ copy ” to the
editor of the Mklb. Review in 1876. The writer
was Henry Gyles Turner, then, as now, general
manager of the Commercial Bank of Australia !

Rev. Robert
Knopwood(on whose
diary “His Natural
Life ” was in part
founded) arrived at
Hobart Town in
1804, and was a
magistrate as well as
a parson. “ A bad
magistrate and a
worse parson, ” said some. At an exhibition of old
rlobart pictures a while back, the catalogue in-
cluded one of old Knopwood on his grey pony.
Out of his tail-pocket peeped the neck of a bottle,
and the 4 artist-poet remarks :
The bottle s neck is stealing out
You may readily divine
His mission’s one of mercy,
For he carries with him wine.
The picture, though a speaking likeness, is, from
an art point of view, on a par. with the poetry.

The Bulletin is now doing its best to fit in the
grun face of the. remorseless judicial slayer of
poor Mary Brownlow with the conventional idea

One of the Chaffey Bros., when he first
came to convert Victoria into another Garden of
Bden, happened to be proceeding to Echuca in a
first - class railway - carriage upon a blazing
summer day. Whilst the train was passing through
one of the dry and treeless plains, Chaffey, looking
out of the window, remarked upon the lean and
haggard condition of the stock that nibbled the
post-and-rail fences for sustenance. Looking
towards the opposite window, he sprang up excited-
ly. My God !” he exclaimed, “ this is the
slowest country on God’s fair earth. There you
have millions of gallons of water and not the
gumption to run some of it into your dried-up
ground. You want Yankee enterprise here, my
friend—Yankee push, Yankee brains.” Some of
the farmers sharing the compartment slowly took
in the situation and smiled aloud. Chaffey had
been admiring a mirage ! The Bulletin regards
this narrative with suspicion, all the same.

Of the 59 Acts of Tas. Parliament passed
during the session just closed, no less than eight
are syndicate measures. The future Tas. peerage
will probably have gridirons for coats-of-arms.
The Country, S.A. National Ass organ,
which was recently described in the Assembly as
“a manure-wrapper,” did not long survive the
name. It announced last week that its publica-
tion will cease with the present year.
Q. delegates to the Federal Council, who
would not have delegates to Reid’s Federation
Convention elected by the people, propose to
move, at the next meeting of the Federal Council,
that its delegates shall in future be so elected !
Already the small, far-off voice of Premier
Forrest is heard from W.A. suggesting that
perhaps it would be more convenient to have the
Federal Convention about the end of 1897. Evi-
dently there are still a lot of possible troubles in
store for the movement.
Last days of Q. Parliamentary session were
worked on the “ caw me an’ I’ll caw thee ” prin-
ciple. “You vote for the Brisbane Municipal
Loan Bill, and I’ll vote for the Electric Power
Bill.” Or, “You vote for (or absent yourself from)
the Companies Bill, and I’ll vote for the Factories
Bill.”
Coincidence. At Vic. general election,
1892 Carter, the retiring member, beat Styles for
Will’iamstown by 330 ; in ’94 Styles beat Carter by
exactly the same number. Reason : In ’92 Carter
was the Labor candidate, although they were both
in favor of one man one vote. In ’94 Carter stood
as a Revenue Tariffist and a supporter of Patterson.
Styles was the Labor candidate and, of course,
High Tariff.
Duncan Gillies, in the usual course of
things, is reckoned a fit and proper person to suc-
ceed to the Vic. Speakership. Duncan was a
calamitous Premier, at present he is a deplorable
Agent-General. Therefore, as a reward for having
served his country very badly in two capacities,
he ought to be supplied with a comfortable arm-
chair and a soothing salary for the remaining term
of his natural life,
Westralia has retained, as
regards some part of the machi-
nery of the law, the forms that
were in vogue in the “ dark ages
of Hingland. For example, it
costs to issue a.summons in the
local court —similar to the county
courts of the other provinces—
two pounds, this extortionate
charge being made up of fees to
magistrate, clerk and bailiff. In
Fielding’s days the magistrate
was paid from the fines he in-
flicted upon the unfortunates
who came before him, and the
author of “Tom .Jones,” who
was stipendiary magistrate in
London, characterised the in-
come of .£3OO a year he received
from this source as the “ dirti-
est money on earth.” Again, in
Forrestralia, wheu costs are
given against a magistrate or a
police constable in his official
position, the Government does
not “ stump up,” but makes the
Jay Pay or the policeman per-
sonally “pay the piper.”
Why John Chinaman wins :
“The Chinaman can write all
day, he can work all day, he can
stand for a whole day in one
position, weaving, hammering
gold, or cutting ivory, without
being once attacked by nervous-
ness. This peculiarity makes it-
self apparent in early youth. The
Chinaman can bear any kind of
bodily exercise. Sport and play
are to him unnecessary labor.
He can sleep anywhere and in
any position —amid thundering
machines, deafening noises, the
ory of children, or the wrangle
of grown people, on the ground,
in bed, or on a chair. ’ ’ — Deutsche
Meoizinal-Zeitung.
To replace the obnoxious
Income-tax in Tasmania a Par-
liamentary candidate proposes an
“ Ability ’’-tax on persons according to the rent
they pav for their houses. It is held that rent
paid is a pretty safe guide as to income, and a
graduated tax on the rent basis would obviate the
present necessity for declaring sources of income.
As the man with 14 children and other relations
to keep him poor needs the biggest house he
generally pays the highest rent. Also, the rich
man with a house of his own doesn’t pay rent.
Yic. Public Service Board has issued a
long catechism to every State employe. Some
sample questions :
1. (a) Are you a correspondent for any newspaper or
periodical, or (6) do you write articles or paragraphs or
(c) communicate news to the “ Press ” verbally or other-
s' Do you receive remuneration for same of any kind
whatever, either by salary, fee, honorarium, or other-
wise ?
At a Wellington (M.L.) polling-booth, a
parson met Premier Dick Seddon, and the two
“ passed the time o’day.” (The ennobbled Dob
Stout, who, despite his treble-X surname, is a tee-
totaller, and who is also an agnostic, was an Oppo-
sition candidate.) “I’m goingto vote Opposition,
said the parson. “ Just like you, replied Dick,
“preach Christianity on Sunday, and vote tor
atheists on Friday.”
Sidelight on the aims of reigning Q. poli-
ticians. to be considered from a federal view-point.
By 29 to 13, on last day of sitting, Q. Assembly
affirmed the desirableness of reviving free and
assisted immigration, while it shelved Labor-
member Browne’s motion re the restriction ot
colored alien labor, and Labor-member (wharf -
laborer) Turley’s motion for the insertion of a
minimum-wage clause in contracts.
Y. : A simple solution of the colored-alien
difficulty. In England every employer of a male
servant pays a tax of 15s. per annum per head.
He becomes liable, if the menial is employed
but for a single day. Apply this system here to
the employer of aliens, and the tribe will soon
disappear. The cheap races are formidable only
because they are cheap. Make their employment
costly, and their virtues in the boss’s view will
vanish.
National Ass Abbott, refused publication
in his favorite Sydney daily, has turned his well-
worn cyclostyle on Tasmania. His particular
trouble at present is to warn the smaller provinces
that unless they insist on equal voting rights in
the Federal senate they will be gobbled up by the
bigger states. He advocates one state one vote
for the senate, and, ye gods ! One Man One Vote
for the Assembly. Abbott advocating One Man
One Vote !
It is now proposed that the Federal Con-
vention shall meet in April. When it has drafted
a constitution it will adjourn for not less than bO
or more than 120 days to allow the various Parlia-
ments to discuss the scheme. They will suggest
amendments —five different sets of amendments
and, when the Convention meets again, it will try
to strike an average among all the suggestions and
please everybody as far as possible. When the
amended constitution is finished it will be sub-
mitted to a popular vote in each of the five pro*
vinces for acceptance or rejection. And when it
has steered through all these difficulties, and the
British Parliament has assented to the new consti-
tution, then things will begin to be really started.
WALLACE BROWNLOW, on his favorite trotter
(Menzies, W.A.).
From photo, by Albert Russell.
In N.S.W. a civil servant who has put in
fifteen years’ service can claim a pension if re-
trenched ; should he be fired out before his fifteen
years expire he only gets “ compensation,” which
is a much smaller matter. ~ The other day half-a-
dozen men, or thereabouts, got a few weeks’ notice
to quit, and one man was thrown out instanter.
He was so near the end of his fifteen years that if
he had got the same notice as the rest he would
have been over the line, and have struck the
Superannuation Fund for his pension. Which was
seemingly the reason why he was so suddenly and
furiously biffed—at least, that is his view of the
case.
When the Mildura Bill—by which the Vic.
Government proposes to advance ,£30,000 to
Chaffey’s settlers so as to put the muddled irriga-
tion works in order, and thus give the settlers
the efficient water-supply which they paid the
Chaffey co. for and didn’t get—was before Parlia-
ment, its chief opponent was Levien, M.L.A.
Levien, who was a director of the Chaffey co. and
was largely responsible for the awful failure, com-
plained that the measure was “ unfair ” and “im-
practicable.” The spectacle of the man who drew
many thousands in fees and commissions out of
Mildura, and left it a wreck, severely admonishing
the people who were putting money in to repair
