THE GLENBROOK MYSTERY.
Premier Reid dreams he sate an advertisement.
In Gisborne (M.L.) recently three “ pro-
hibited persons,” not being allowed to buy drink
on the ordinary lines, burgled a house in search of
liquor, and one of them took a long swallow of
what he thought was stolen beer, only to find that
it was carbolic acid. At Napier, another pro-
hibited individual, not being able to ob ain
whisky, took to chlorodyne, and when chlorodyne
led to insanity he sawed his head nearly off with a
razor. Teetotalism has its death-roll as well as
alcohol.
Yic. Leg. Council, being wise in its genera-
tion, passed the State Land Bank measure, practi-
cally without alteration. The Council represents
the property-holding community. The object of
the Land Bank is to supply property-holders with
cheap loans on mortgage. Therefore, this was the
one democratic measure on the Government’s list
the rejection of which would probably have made
the Council’s constituents rise up and tear it in 96
separate pieces, and the 48 Rich Landlords recog-
nised the fact and knocked under.
The “ revelations ” as to the condition of
Vic. Postal Department go to prove that P.M.G.
Duffy is the hopeless incapable of the Turner
Ministry. Somehow it is a recognised thing in
ail the Australias that the hopeless incapable of
the Ministry should be P.M.G. Which is one of
the things which make The Bulletin suggest
that the Postal Department should be run by a
permanent Commissioner, like the railways.
Continuous management by the fool of the Cabinet
doesn’t give any department a fair chance.
Canterbury (M.L.) Times, a strong sup-
porter of the Seddon Government, remarks : “We
should prefer to have a popular referendum-vote
before further borrowing is authorised.” It has
already been proved in Australasia that the
referendum is workable, and the question “to
borrow or not to borrow ” is a clear-cut issue
eminently suitable for decision by a popular vote.
And as nobody really knows what is the popular
opinion on the borrowing curse, the referendum,
which is the one way of putting a single issue
straight before the nation, would come in ad-
mirablv ; "“t here.
Melb. Age barracks hard and barracks
always for Alfred Deakin. The windy orator
with the stubborn black hair is evidently David
Syme’s nominee for the next Premiership vacancy,
and from this fact the decadence of D. Syme’s in-
tellect may be safely assumed. Alf. Deakin,
word-spinning lawyer and dreamy self-deceiver,
will be a bigger failure than even Shiels if his
newspaper champion should succeed in carrying
its point. And hasn’t Victoria already had enough
of the smug prattler who sat smiling at the right
hand of Gillies all through the stinkful boom-
time? A decent, honorable, “clever” fellow in
private life. Nobody doubts the assertion. But,
as a politician, Deakin deserves to be burned at
the stake, or taken up to Heaven without dying.

Cullen, and O Sullivan.

Sargood, the Yic. boss M.L.C., wails
greatly about alleged infringements by the Assem-
bly of the rights of the House of 48 Rich Land-
lords. Yet he has the gall to move in the Council
a needless and useless resolution that the plural

Clutha (M.L.) is in a curious position. In
1894 it voted for Prohibition and got it. At the
election of a few weeks ago it went back on the
Cold Tea gospel with disappointing results. Only
three issues could be voted on : whether licenses
should remain as they are, or be reducjed in num-
ber, or be altogether abolished. The drink party
voted that licenses should remain as they are, that
being the only way they could express their views.
And they carried their proposition with the result
that licenses do remain as they are—in other
words, they don’t remain at all. Unless it is
made possible to vote for an increase of licenses it
seems that when once the Cold Tea party arrives
no majority can shift it.
It now transpires that Premier Reid, after
the collapse of his Queensland mission, tele-
graphed to Braddon (of Tasmania) and Turner (of
Victoria) asking if they didn’t think Federation
should be dropped. Both returned an emphatic
“ no,” and pointed out that the Enabling Act
specially provided that the movement might go
on if three provinces adhered to it, and asked
why G. Reid agreed to the insertion of that
proviso if he wasn’t willing to proceed when five
provinces were in the movement. Then the half
or less than half-hearted N.S.W. Premier decided
to shove the matter feebly along a little further.
Apparently G. Reid will want a lot of watching if
the Federal question isn’t to drop accidentally
dead somewhere by the political wayside.
“ The Westralian Govt.,” writes a Bulle-
tin correspondent, “is a backstairs concern by
habit and instinct. Suppose you apply preposter-
ously for anything and the local officer rightly re-
fuses it, do you accept that? Not a bit. You
simply go to Perth, see the understrapper in
charge and give him a ‘cut.’ Then you see the
Minister and he—being perhaps worried with the
fair demands of the officer who refused your appli-
cation in the first instance—is delighted to snub
him instead of upholding his action and — grants
your application. It is pretty nearly all ‘ cuts ’
from start to finish. The clerk gets a ‘ cut,’ the
registrar a ‘ cut,’ the surveyor a ‘ cut ’ to put your
lot through and recommend it, and you, if you are
cunning enough and have got money enough, get
anything you want.

“ One of the most amusing election inci-
dents on record,” writes “ Maxillus ” to The Bul-
letin, “occurred at a political meeting in Wel-
lington, M.L., some four years ago at the time of
a hotly-contested bye-election. Reeves was speak-
ing one night at the Opera House when a conser-
vative wit threw a cabbage on the stage. Some
college boys—with as much appreciation of horse-
play and as little originality as the majority of
college boys—determined to improve on the joke.
Accordingly, on the night of a similar meeting a
few days later, they repaired to the family circle
with an artistic bundle of turnips and carrots.
All might have gone well had they not, in antici-
pation of the joke to come, handed round the
bouquet among themselves for inspection. The
family-circle at election times was the chosen
resort of the wharf-lumpers. Some of them spied
the floral tribute, and the youths were politely in-
formed that if they threw it over they would
follow it. The young humorists placed it under
the seat, making desperate efforts not to appear
uncomfortable. The news was soon passed round
the circle,-and at every interval in the speech-
making pressing appeals were made to ‘ throw
them blanky greens over ! ’ These invitations
were not responded to. At the end of the meeting
the youths rose to slink away. No such luck,
however. They were immediately stopped, and
told to ‘blanky well sit down.’ They did so,
meekly. ‘Now, hawk out them vegetables.’
It was dfibe. ‘ Now, set to work and d d well
eat them.” Sorrowfully the leader distributed
them among his comrades ; sickly smiling, they
fell to ‘ with what appetite they might.’ ’Twas a
solemn meal. When nothing was left but a few
green stalks, they were suffered to steal away,
amid a storm of hisses, catcalls and ironical
cheers.”

Give us this day our daily bread. A Fitz-
roy (Melb.) baker was lately charged with having
his premises in an uncleanly state. Hits of evi-
dence: Dough-trough dirty, not cleaned for
months ; on shelf above trough a bundle of dirty
rags; flour-shoot resting on floor; at bakehouse
door a puddle of liquid refuse and slops from three
houses at rear ; floor covered with dirt and manure
brought in by baker on his boots ; over bakery a
loft containing stocks of horse-feed, firewood, and
flour, also two pigeons, which roosted on the bags
of flour and deposited various things thereon ; loft
also used as a bedroom and store for old tins ; loft
stairs caked with same tilth as floors ; heaps of
dirt in middle of both loft and bakehouse; no
manure-pit, and open sewer in yard. This baker
had three shops where he sold meat-pies, tarts,
and other cookies fabricated in this filthy Tophet.

<£l, with £2 2s. costs, met the case.
L. J. Nugent, of the Hermitage, Barron
River (Q.), a capable naturalist, tells the Queens-
lander a good yarn about his happening upon a
number of intoxicated beetles. “Two of the six
were on their backs, apparently dead; another
pair were, by all appearances, wrestling—they
were standing on their hind legs, and had each
other tightly clasped. . . The other two . .
were fluttering and toppling, but, once they fell
on their backs all power seemed lost to them.”
Nugent watched, and found others getting drunk
on the sap of a tree —“ a thick liquid matter
fizzling out of a blister or wound from a knock the
tree may have got.” The sap was like yeast, and
contained a deal of alcohol. Nugent captured the
beetles, which recovered next day, and not one of
them seemed to “ have a head ”on it. What says
the Cold Tea Brigade to this story ?
Dear Bulletin, —In Hyde Park, Sydney,
t’other day, saw threeold-fashioned slum youngsters
working off a joke,on the poor blind wretch grind-
ing an organ at the end of the path. Walking
by they would smartly tap with a pebble the little
open-topped tin box, placed to receive the browns
of the charitable. The old man’s hand would fly out,
accompanied with a muttered “ Lawd bless you!”
to scoop in the copper which was never there.
This was continued about half-a-dozen times, till a
benevolent-looking old lady hove in sight. She
waddled quietly up to the musician, dropped a opin
into the box, and “ Damn you, you young wretch!
—— !” said the blind man. The old
lady fainted.—J.F.D. P.S.—The old lady didn’t
faint, but I put that in to round the yarn off, as it
were.—J.F.D.
Actuary Alsop, of Melbourne Savings
Bank, has his own particularly painful manner of
calling attention to “Thrift.” Every Xmas he
pelts the prodigal and the pauper alike with
maxims, or, rather, he gets the newspapers to hurl
these unnecessary reminders at the Thriftless
whilst he sits in his office waiting for the result.
Mr. Alsop may be doing good, but The Bulletin
has its doubts. His maxims are dangerous when
not irritatingly puerile. “No matter what comes
in, if more goes out, you will always be poor,” is
hardly the text to turn a hardened sinner to re-
pentance, nor does it make a deep impression upon
the struggling householder who saves five shillings
per annum with splendid heroism, and remains
always poor notwithstanding. As for Mr.
Alsop’s statement that “a savings bank-account
has made many a man happy,” it might be more
forcibly applied to Beer. Swallowed in modera-
tion Beer has been, and still is, a much more cer-
tain producer of bliss than the price of an
occasional pint invested at two per cent. Yet
Thirst and Thrift are eternal foes.

The Girl who Says "No Trust."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Dame Nature with her fickle ways
Makes mortals mostly weak :
E’en brainless rogues can govern “jayi ”
By force of vulgar cheek.
How oft we love the thing we hate,
And can’t discover why ;
We seek the cause in mood sedate—
The answer is a sigh.
I ofttimes ponder o’er the “ tart ”
Who greets me with a leer,
And wonder why within my heart
I hold her rather dear;
Tis not because she has more charm*
Than other girls I know:
It is, perhaps, because at times
Her little word is “ No.”
God Bacchus mocks the fools devout
Who worship at his shrine :
The proverb tells that Wit is out
When one is full is Wine ;
And yet if cronies two or more
Induce me to a “ bust,”
I still retain my liking for
The girl who says “No Trust! ”
The thirst that I would madly slake
She bids me bear away :
“Dry up!” she says, “for Tottie’s sake,”
And meekly I obey.
My throat is parch’d, my soul I’d sell
To feed the liquor-lust,
But home I stagger ’neath the spell
Of her who cries “ No Trust ! ”
Next morning with a frown severe
I seek that pub. in town,
And Tottie leers the killing leer
Which brings my anger down.
I growl as I have growled before,
Her sweet reply is “ Stuff !
When customers can’t pay for more
They’ve always had enough.”
H.V. and E.F.

PINK PILLS SAVED MY
THE EMPHATIC STATEMENT OP A YOONG LADY WHO
BESIDES IN THE HOME OF BEV. J. E. HDNTEB,
THE WELL-KNOWN EVANGELIST.
The Rev. J. E. Hunter, whose marvellous work
in connection with Rev. Mr. Crossley, in the
Evangelistic field, has given him a Continental
reputation, resides in the city of St. Thomas, Ont.
The particulars of the following remarkable cure
upon the person of a young lady living with the
family are therefore of more than passing interest.
To a reporter of the St. Thomas Journal she told
the following story
“I believe Dr. Williams’Pink Pills saved my
life, and I am quite willing that everyone should
know it,” said Miss Harris, when asked if she
would make public her story. Continuing, she
said, “When I was 12 or 13 years of age I was
taken sick. The doctors said my blood was all
turned to water. For five years I suffered terribly,
and was so weak that I could barely keep alive.
If I tried to stand for a short time, or if I got the
least bit warm, I would fall over in a faint. My
eyes were white and glassy, and I was so thin and
pallid that everyone beiieved I was dying of
consumption. During the five years I was ill I
was attended by five physicians in St. Thomas,
two in Detroit, one in London, and one in
Aylmer, and none of them could do anything for
me. I was so far gone that they had no hope of
my recovery. Towards the last my feet and limbs
swelled, so they had to be bandaged to keep them
from bursting. They were bandaged for three
months, and my whole body was swollen and
bloated, and the doctors said there was not a
pint of blood in my body, and they held out
no hopes whatever. I saw in the Journal
about a man in Hamilton being cured by
taking Pink Pills. I thought if they could cure
him they would help me, and I decided to try
them. Before I had finished three boxes I felt
relieved; the swelling went down and the
bandages were removed. I continued taking Pink
Pills until I had taken seven boxes, then irregu-
larly I took three more, one of which Mr. Hunter
brought back from Brockville. I am perfectly
cured. I have not been ill a single day since I
finished the seventh box of pills. I came to Mrs.
Hunter’s a year ago, and she will tell you I have
never been ill a day since coming here, and I
always feel strong, and able to do the work. I
can and do strongly recommend Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills,’ said Miss Harris in conclusion. Mrs.
J. E. Hunter, wife of the Evangelist, told the
reporter that Miss Harris was a good, reliable,
and truthful girl, and that perfect reliance could
be placed on her statements. “She looks like a
different girl from what she was when she came
here a year ago,” said Mrs. Hunter.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are a certain cure for
rheumatism, sciatica, lumbago, loss of physical
strength, weakness, indigestion, and sick headache,
and are obtainable from all chemists and dealers,
or from Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney, who
will forward six boxes for 14s. 9d. (or one box for
2s. 9d.) post free. They restore pale and sallow
complexions to the glow of health, and are a
specific for all the troubles peculiar to the female
sex, while in the case of men they effect a radical
cure in all cases arising from mental worry, over-
work, or excesses of whatever nature.

“Papa’s Hair.”
A gentleman has sent us the following :—“ A few
weeks ago I bought a bottle of your ‘ Papa’s Hair,’
and it has acted with the most satisfactory results.
Previous to doing so, my hair had a mousey color
streaked with grey. The dear children would try
to count papa’s grey hairs, but, because of the
number, could not succeed. Being only 44 years
of age, I determined to improve my hair, and tried
your ‘Papa’s Hair,’ and am pleased to write that,
though at first there seemed little difference, gradu-
ally the hair regained its original color (there was
no dyeing whatever) and it is now strong and glossy.
I asked my little daughter this morning to count
my grey hairs, but when looking for them she cried
out— 1 Why, papa, there are no white hairs; mamma
must have pulled them all out.’ ”
JSf" This Preparation is not a Dye, but acts gradu-
ally on the Roots of the Hair, and restores Grey
Hair to its natural color and lustre.
No preparation will make hair grow better than
“ Pap a ’.s II air. ”
It effectually prevents the formation of Dandriff,
as it is called, the presence of which always denotes
diseased action in the cuticle of the scalp, causing
premature baldness, or a falling off of the hair.
DIRECTIONS.—WeII shake the bottle, and with
a small piece of sponge rub into the roots of the
hair, night and morning, till the hair is restored to
its natural color ; afterwards apply twice a week.

JIMMIE PANNIKIN.
Brisbane excise officers, the other day,
surprised some alleged “colonial ale” that con-
tained nearly three-eighths of an ounce of sul-
phuric acid (oil of vitriol) and a lot of nicotine
per gallon. The average Queenslander likes his
soft stuff” that way ; nothing but boiling nitric
acid with corrosive sublimate and chloride of lime
will do to flavor up his “ hard tack.”
“The Patentees —Macniven & Cameron—deserve
A NATIONAL MEMORIAL
for their excellent inventions.”— Dover Chronicle.
The Waverley Pen. The Pickwick Pen.
The Owl Pen. The Hindoo Pen.
Sold at all Stationers. Waverley Works, Edinburgh.
Premier Kingston's Law Reform pro-
posals are not the only reason for his unpopu-
larity with his legal brethren. Recently he was
the means of a big land compensation case being
settled by Judge Bundey, acting as arbitrator,
without the intervention of lawyers on either side.
The Judge made a personal inspection and deli-
vered judgment in court, the whole proceedings
occupying just half a day. Legal expenses next to
nothing !
“ I heard a fragment of a conversation in
a railway train, lately,” writes a Bulletin cor-
respondent, “describing a piece of seashore on
which for hundreds of yards dead penguins were
piled up as high as Oft. Here and there among
them were gigantic crows, so gorged that they
were unable to fly. The penguins had been swept
there by a storm from a line of rocks a little dis-
tance out, somewhere in the neighborhood of Aus-
tralia. Where, please ? ”
The gentleman in “the horrors” who
sees “ snakes ” and thinks them realities is really
wiser than the average doctor, who believes the
“snakes” to be hallucinations. A New York
ophthalmic surgeon has just pointed out that
in front of the actual seeinti parts of the eye
lies a retinal layer carrying the blood-vessels.
In a state of “D.T’s’’ the tortuous blood-
vessels are dilated, filled with dark blood,
and rapidly pulsating. On the sensitive layer
these produce the effect of lots of waggly, squirm-
ing things, which waggly squirmers the befuddled
brain, partly from inexperience, partly from news-
paper allegation, promptly classifies, and not
without some justification, as “ snakes ” —the only
waggly, squirming things it knows.

The same old column of reading matter has appeared
ftn the Sydney Lyceum programmes for many months
past. And the Goatcher management hr.s apparently
taken it over from its predecessor, Williamson, for the
same six alleged jokes turned up on Saturday night as
usual.
Considering that the experienced Arthur Garner is
understood to have shouted rich wines and smoked big,
!at cigars all over two continents in quest of the new
Palace Co., and considering how very ordinary half
;he co. is, it would seem that variety talent is
i little scarce just now. Still, as the preachers say, “ all
things work together for good.” Garner is thrown out
now, and Goatcher has the co. Therefore, Garner is
probably very glad that he didn’t get a better one.
What’s bred in the bone, &c. Veteran actor-manager
George Coppin’s father was a medico by education, but
abandoned surgery to become an actor-manager. Of
George’s children, his son George and three of his
laughters have appeared on the boards, and the fourth is
secretary to Rignold. Son Fred, the sole non-pro. among
the veteran’s progeny, is gold-mining in N.E. Victoria.
Jakoff Hambourg, brother to pianist Mark, of the
furious hair and merciless fingers, is coming into notice as
i youthful phenomenon on the violin. If little Jakoff
resembles Mark Hambourg in face and figure it was
Nature’s duty to give him, some .musical talent to go
dong with the dreadful mane. They used to hang round
the piano plugger as though they could eat him. Musio
Oath charms to move the padded breast.
Now that Australia boasts of Melba, Crossley, and
Frances Saville, to say nothing of minor stars, one wonders
to find this pre-eminence of talent on the side of the
women, while the men are wholly unrepresented.
It would be interesting to hear Madame Marchesi, or Sant-
y, lecture upon the classical method of music-hall voice
roduction. There must be some virtue in the nasal style of
garbling practised by such hard-working professors as
Charles Godfrey and Chirgwin. A comic-opera singer
with a good voice, but an uneducated, has a short life at
the game, yet a Rickards goes on for ever. A certain class
of twang seems to preserve the voice against breakage,
let them knock it about as they may. It lends expression
alike to maudlin sentiment or rough humor, and has con-
siderable magnetic charm for the multitude when pro-
perly worked. Superior singing-masters sneer at the
twang’s barbarity, but they don’t attempt to explain
why a veteran twanger retains his good old top-note in
defiance of all accepted rules.
“Madge Kendal, after a scathing attack on Clement
Scott, of London D.T., has sprayed her hysterical bitter-
ness over the Referee for daring to comment on the
shamefully-neglected state of her brother, Tom Robert-
son’s, grave,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent. “ Here is the letter, and he who reads must
needs run afterwards :— 4 Central Station Hotel, Glasgow,
Nov. 11, 1896. Sir, —As you have been manly enough to
publish my letter !—and what an honor for your paper it
has been !—I will tell you this. ... I offered years ago to
attend to my brother’s grave, but the paper proving the
ownership of same cannot be found, and among so many
has been lost! and (as even you in your ignorance may
know) that unless one can prove the ownership of the
grave, to clean or to alter anything in connection with it
is impossible ! When you write my brother's name again
—do it on your knees ! with your hat off ! I never knew
your paper existed—till I saw it one day on my kitchen
table ! when I told my Housekeeper to burn it. She rang
for the Groom—who sent for the Stable Boy—who did so !
—Yours faithfully, Madoe Kendal.’ Madge is a ‘near’
woman, though, and with all her wealth, gives little
thought to her own sisters striving hard to make that w ay
in life so difficult for educated women in crowded Lon-
don. Perhaps it was the cemetery trustees prevented
her lending a helping' hand or even a protective wing to
her dead brother’s parentless daughter? A tragic queen
sunk in shoddy burlesque.”
Rickards has been wondering whether Melb. Royal
would suit him.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Whene’er I take a walk around,
How many pros. I see ;
An 1 tho’ I sometimes shout for them,
T ley never shout for me.
Miss Frederica Mitchell, the Vic. contralto (it is always
fictoria !), is winning her way into favor in London. Her
mother, a finished vocalist, who sang as an amateur, was
the daughter of a former British Resident in Persia,
where he married a native lady; hence the attractive
Orientalism of Miss Mitchell’s appearance.
The late Frank Cates’s boys are now- in the care of Mrs.
Thos. Kennedy and her husband, the secretary of the
Musical and Dramatic Association, who took them in on
the death of their aunt.
Apropos to variety-artist Priscilla Verne horsewhipping
a Perth man-about-town. The horsewhipping woman is
a coward in that she depends on male chivalry preventing
a hit back, but it doesn’t always work. A few years ago
a weird woman-suffrage gasometer sailed into a Castle-
maine newspaper office, armed with a buggy-whip, and,
after much incoherent oratory, proceeded to use it.
About one minute after there was a wild row like theatre
thunder, and a mass of humanity rolled downstairs into
the main street gutter. Presently editor Mac. was seen
sitting astride a wildly dishevelled female, picking large
hunks of mud out of his eyes and ears, while the office-
boy sat on said female’s head to keep her down. That
female doesn’t tackle country editors now.
Song-writer Tito Mattei often conceives a melody in the
street, and rushes into the nearest music-shop, or even
pub., plays it over, writes it down, and departs suddenly
as he came. His great song, “Hear the Wild Winds
Blow,” he composed in a Margate oyster-shop, in the in-
tervals of a big bivalve supper.
John F. Sheridan’s account of the origin of “ Fun on
the Bristol ” “ I was playing for Jarrett and Palmer in
New York a part in a piece that was not a success ; there
was in it a court-scene wherein a young girl was brought
before the magistrate and fined. I said to Jarrett, ‘ I
wish 1 had that gill’s part instead of this one.’ Jarrett
said, ‘ All right—you can please yourself.’ So next night
I took the girl’s part in addition to my own, arranging
with the man who played the Magistrate to cross-examine
me and take me up sharp. I dressed the part of an old
Irish woman—witty, and a habitual drunkard. This
took, and people dropped in to see the court-scene alone.
George Fawcett Rowe, in New' York, saw me, and Jarrett
suggested his writing me a piece on the lines of the Irish
woman. He wrote ‘ Fun on the Bristol.’ I went out
with it. It was a failure from the start, as it was very
broad—the Count stayed too long in the Widow’s Cabin
and made saucy remarks when he returned to the stage.
Jarrett allowed me to re-write it, leaving out the over-
vulgarity which I saw- was its weak point, and from the
first night ‘ Fun on the Bristol ’ took as played and re-
written by me. In England Arthur Garner saw me, and
engaged me for a six months’ Australian tour. I made
£4OOO, the firm just cleared themselves.”
From a private letter : “ Sardou is now 60 years old.
wrinkled, and half-bald, but in his elastic step and
brilliant eye as youthful as a boy. He is said to have
earned nearly a quarter-million from his plays.”
A New York publication gives some interesting con-
fessions of Thomas Minnock, a star hypnotic subject.
Minnock says the whole business is a swindle, and alleges
that before reaching the top rung he made a complete
physical pain conquest. One of Kennedy’s wretched‘sub-
jects made a similar “ confession ” in Melb. last year.
These fellows have their share of human vanity, of
course, and it pleases them to try and persuade them-
selves that they are free agents in the mesmerist’s show.
All the same, they are under the showman’s influence.
They rehearse their hideous tomfoolery, and know what
they are about to do when they go upon the platform,
but when the fat man puts them through their perform-
ances they are the irresponsible puppets of his will. Pro-
fessional hypnotists have tried to work one another’s pro-
fessional subjects, and failed generally. The subject
might be willing enough, but the new influence was too
weak.
It is a remarkable thing, as theatrical customs go,
that nearly every member of the Brough co. plays under
his or her own name. Mr. and Mrs. 8., Miss Hardy,
Titheradge pfere et fille, Carey, &c., none of them wear
stage names. In most companies the exception is to find
a mummer who appears with his or her lawful name.
A journeying band of brilliants lately struck a country
town. The brightest gem immediately borrowed a pound
from a resident, and hired a cottage at 7s. a week, paying
the first week’s rent in advance. Agent, in due time,
calls for the next week’s rent. Doth he get it? He doth
not. The interview ends by the agent himself lending
the B.G. 6s. ! A day or two later the landlord himself
calls for the rent. Doth he get it ? He doth not. B.G. :
“Oh, I refer you to your agent; I’ve settled with him.’
Position : Won’t shift for a month, anyhow ; nothing to
levy on, anyhow; won’t shift then if don’t choose ; eject-
ment process slow and costly and nothing to be gained
by it 1

When Macready produced “ Henry V.” for the last time
in London, John Forster was present at the rehearsals.
An illiterate actress named Mrs. Bunby, cast for Mrs.
Quickly, was much annoyed by Forster criticising her
pronunciation and saying to Macready, “ Put her through
it again, Mac. ; put her through it again.” But she was
comforted by a young actor saying, “ Never mind what
the gentleman says, he is only the author.” By the way,
who did the condensing of the “Henry V.” which Rignold
plays ?

It is suggested that George Rignold ought to try Fal-

An evening’s observation of a newly-reorganised co., in
which the “ young and fresh ” element is very dewy,
developes the idea that the inginue may always be
detected at a glance by the promptitude with which she
pulls her dress down every time she “ flops” into a chair.
The debutante is harried with pale apprehension that the
audience may catch sight of her ankles. Old stagers don’t
trouble to “hitch down.” Nellie Farren used to say she
liked to wear jewelled garters because —well, never mind.
A theatrical agent, just returned from the North,
relates how he once called at the office of a small Q. up-
country newspaper, and finding no one in left the Usual
“ press ticket,” endorsed “ Editor, with comps.” He goes
on to say that they took it literally at that office, and
that the “ed.” and his five “comps.” turned up that
night “on the nod.”
A travelling pro. : “ Holy South Australia ! One town
I was in lately (population, 600 souls) had no less than ten
denominational chapels and churches. I spent a Sunday
there ; I shiver when I think of my experience. Sunday
means, in inland towns, three heavy courses of Salvation
Army, the perpetual clanging of church bells, and half-
cold meals. At one hotel I sojourned in, the people were
so saintly that they covered the canary-cages with cloths
on the Sabbath, so that the birds shouldn’t sing. Fact 1
South Australia is a fine country to keep out of.”
The artful actor told the simple people of Oroagingolong
that he loved them and the place so well that he hoped
to live and die there. Next week he was telling the same
gag at Boulahmine, when a voice roared out, “Why, man,
you said the same thing at Oroagingolong last week.”
“ That’s quite true,” said the blushless actor; “but re-
member I hadn’t seen Bowlahmine then.”
Dear Bulletin, —I had been told it was the second best
Agricultural Show to be seen in Maoriland, and I found
myself wondering what the worst must be like. There
were engines going, and reapers and binders clawing at
space looking like huge crabs ; there were separators and
turnips and other things I’ve forgotten the name of, and
a female who fell off a box, where she had perched her-
self, into the mud. I was glad she splashed some of the
crowd, because she seemed a respectable old lady. I’d
have gone away, for my feet were wet and I didn’t know
anyone, and I didn’t know a merino from a Berkshire, and
the awful Australasian holiday cigar was in the air; but
I’ve read much Lawson and much “Banjo” and much
Bulletin generally, and I wanted to see a real “Jim ” and
a real “ Bill.” There was a chopping contest to come off.
Eventually it started, and I tried to be regardless of a
drip down my back from a taller man’s hat, in an en-
deavor to work up all my old Bill and Jim enthusiasm. I
was getting along nicely when a voice beside me re-
marked, “ Let me see the cows chop.” I did my best to
let the owner of the voice do as he wished. He was a
large man with an unpleasant breath—suggestive of a
buzzard’s. Presently, squeezing all the sarcasm possible
into the remark he ejaculated : “ Chop! . . . I’ll
chop any one of ’em 1 . . . I’ll chop any man in the
country. I’ll chop any man in the world ! ” After a
pause: “And me mate’ll chop me!” Just then some-
thing seemed to annoy him, and I couldn’t get up
till two others got off me, and as I was walking away
wringing the mud from my ear I could just make out his
voice remarking to the choppers : “ Why don’t yer chain
yer dogs to it! ” —X.
Ada Crossley, judging from latest samples of the jam
awarded to her, is within easy distance of earning a
steady £5OOO a year. At present, she must be making
quite half as much. Among sundry notices of her per-
formance at the Sheffield Musical Festival, where she
ranked as principal contralto, London D.T. said “ Miss
Crossley’s voice, if she had no other merit, would plead
for her with golden-mouthed eloquence,” whilst the local
papers were unanimous in praise of the Australian lady’s
artistic method. Birmingham Daily Gazette calls her
“a superb artist, who, to a magnificent voice, adds the
much rarer gift of refined and correct artistic feeling,”
and the Glasgow Daily Mail talks about “the great con-
tralto, Miss Ada Crossley.” In another two years’ time
the Hinglish press everywhere will have accepted Crossley
as a power on the platform. Then she will command a
special fee for her name in addition to the prices now paid
her for singing, and Australia’s chance of hearing its
graceful daughter again before her hair turns gray will be
1 in 50 or thereabouts.
This has been a ’Murkan year in Australian theatrical
productions, and one memorable chiefly for its stage
twang. The “ Trilby” co., the Nat. Goodwin crowd, the
“ Chinatowners,” all from the land of monotone. And
now, in the last month of the year, Brough lets loose a
typical ’Murkan farce, “ Nancy and Co.”

Allan and Co. (Melb.), having obtained the Australian
copyright of “ Love’s Serenade,” are publishing that
tuneful souvenir of “The Milk White Flag” at 2s.
“Love’s Serenade” is a hitherto unobtainable ballad
which plump tenor Pacie warbled to Widow M‘Cann
from behind a curtain. The tenor’s kindness in
remaining hidden meanwhile, so as not to spoil the effect
will never be forgotten by The Bulletin.
Glen and Co. (Melb.) issue a Xmas Federal Music Book
and a “ Colonial Musical Cabinet.” The latter, decorated
outside with a picture of the expansive Libbey, contains
“Molly’s the Girl for Me” and a selection' of similar
taffy. In the much more desirable “ Federal ” there are
two new marches, some bright dance music, and at least
one classic song—“ When the Heart is Young.”
According to Charles Dilke, M.P., there
are 54,000 sailors in the British mercantile marine
of whom 34,000 are British. If a great war broke
out it would require fully 17,000 sailors, in addi-
tion to those now enrolled, to take the British
navy to sea, and this demand would sweep off half
the merchant sailors in one act. It would take
11,000 more to man the vessels now in course of
construction, which would absorb most of the other
half, and practically abolish British shipping and
commerce for the time being. Also, in a severe war
it would probably take 50,000 more sailors to re-
place the killed, wounded, and invalided, and
Britain hasn’t got them.

“A HIGHLAND GATHERINGS
Hawkshaw : “ ’Bout that crow. What’s
his price as a bird of ill omen ? Some eight years
ago I was returning from looking at some land I
afterwards selected, when I saw a crow on the
ground. He could not rise, yet his wings were
sound, and I could find no reason why he should
not fly. I brought the beggar home— quawking
vigorously—and in the afternoon I got a telegram
from Broken Hill saying my two brothers were
blown up by a missed hole. One lost one eye, and
the other had both badly damaged. While I
read the wire that crow croaked in a sepulchral
tone of voice—his last croak.”

[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
He was an oldish man, and he got up beside me
in the empty ’bus. Presently he sidled up and
said, “ Would you mind feeling my pulse, sir, and
telling me whether it goes too fast ? ”
I felt it. “It seems thin and wiry to me, but I
don’t think it is going too fast.”
“ Thank you, sir.”
In a minute or two he began once more : You
know, sir, I feel terribly out of sorts. I have been
a great drunkard, and am just getting over it. I
feel it, I can tell you.”
“ I suppose you do.”
“Do you believe in prayer, sir? ”
I am not greatly addicted to prayer, but I re-
plied “Yes.” It seemed more respectable.
“ Would you mind praying for me when you
say your prayers ? ”
“ Certainly, if you wish it.”
Just then we passed the Brown Jug Hotel.
“Stop,” he called to the driver.
He got out and —walked into the hotel.
I prayed.
MAXILLUS.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The cricket-match N.S.W. v. S.A., in Adelaide,
was perhaps the most extraordinary yet. played. When
NSW made 162 on a perfect wicket Capt. Garrett,
although admitting magnificent bowling was very
distrusted. No one save S.A. less than 300, but tne Jast
wicket fell for 187. Then N.S.W. lost her best men cheap,
but a junior saved a big defeat, and Garrett and Turnei
made victory certain. . >
Nine Australian men played, and only two of
them—Clem. Hill and Kelly— showed any batting form.
Lyons’7B for S.A., in the first innings, was a
grand display of sound forcing cricket. “ Carbine Jack
never played sounder cricket, and one hard hit, which
Coningham should have caught, would have been a safe
fiver in Sydney and Melb. Clem. Hill made 100 in the
two innings by the best batting of the match. Clem, is
the leading artist in Australia on the “ on ” side.
The three juniors in the match — Noble (N.S.W.),
and Evans and Bailey (S.A.)-were complete successes.
Noble’s innings was a fine sample of pretty and plucky
cricket. He went in to stop a rot, and he succeeded.
Bailey went in when three wickets were down for 34, and
stayed there until the century was posted and victory
looked likely for S.A. But S.A. has a tail.
M‘Kibbin, who upset the S.A. apple-cart, comes
back from England a much-improved bowler. Although
pig-fat, he bowled with great consistency. A word for
Garrett, the “stormy petrel’’ of S.A. cricketers. Last
year Garrett made a fine stand with Howell, and secured
victory for N.S.W., and this year he and Turner did the
same. The Sydney University captain, like others of the
old school, knoweth not the meaning of the word “funk.
A bad decision possibly lost the match for S.A.
Darling played a ball hard on to his pad, and without any
appeal being made the N.S.W. umpire called “out.”
Then Garrett again appealed, which was necessary on the
rules, and Darling had to retire. As neither bowler nor
batsmen appealed for the 1.b.w., or anything else, Gar-
rett’s action was, under the circumstances, rather unfor-
tunate, to put it mildly. “ Darling on Umpires ” would
be unprintable just now.
The nine Aus. Eleven men who played in Adelaide
last week have nearly all become stale from a surfeit of
cricket. Iredale and Gregory are obviously out of form,
Darling is only in fair nick, and Jones, although his
bowling seems improved, cannot at present keep up his
great pace as long as usual. The one man who seems
really fresh and lively is M'Kibbin. George Giffen is at
present much hampered by a knock on the right knee,
received in America, which nearly forced him to stand
out of the S.A.-N.S.W. contest.
* » * *
Coincidences : In the first innings of the match
between N.S.W. and S.A., M'Kibbin made 7 not out and
took 7 wickets. In the second innings, he made 8 not out
and took 8 wickets.
* * » •
Premier Keid is also president of the N.S.W.
Cricket Association. Years ago he accompanied a N.S.W.
team down to Melb., in the days of Charley Bannerman,
Ted Evans, Spofforth, and “Ned ” Sheridan (the “ Scud,”
as he was called). The result was a little while ago
described by veteran “Ned ” Sheridan (now a prosperous
tobacconist in Brisbane) in these words : “ Well, Georgie
was the comicalest figure I ever struck, with his legs
doubled up and toes turned in. His pants was skin-
tight, he had a double chin and a single eyeglass.
We got him to take a strike at the nets. Lord!
we laughed till the tears rolled down our cheeks.
We was all pokin’ it at him, and pitchin’ soft stuff, with a
break from the off to go between his legs. He always
lifted the bat after the ball was past, and fixed his eye-
glass after each ball. We thought him the biggest duffer
we ever saw—until the banquet, when he got up and
made a speech ! Holy Moses, didn’t he talk ! My boy,
you couldn’t bowl him. He nearly talked us silly ; one
minute we was laughing, next crying, and all the time
cheering. I tell you, we never looked on him as a mug
after that speech ; and always after that we used to pitch
’em up to him, at the nets, so that he couldn’t possibly
miss ’em, if he tried.”
* * * *
Cricketer Ernest Jones’ first question on arriving
at Adelaide was about that engagement offered him by
S.A.C.A. when his capture by Sussex looked certain. “ I
virtually broke off the negotiation,” said Jonah, “when
they cabled me, because I want to stay in Adelaide.”
Anyway Jones says if they don’t make a definite proposal
he will play in Melb. or Sydney directly something valu-
able offers.
* * * *
A clergyman at an anti-gambling meeting the
other night told a heart-rending tale of a young friend oi
his who had been ruined by “backing a horse in Tatter-
sail’s sweep.” Another said he was constantly annoyed
by the bookmakers on an adjacent racecourse “ voci-
ferously calling the odds.” At the racecourse in question
the bookmakers never call the odds vociferously or other-
wise. The seductive “Who’ll take the last two for six-
pence” is absolutely the only “odds” that are called.
Ditto at a church bazaar.
* * * *
A Westralian correspondent writes : “ This is a
great province for sport of all kinds. I have been on many
racecourses around Sydney and Melbourne, and have
witnessed many awful things thereat, but it was not until
I came West that I saw daylight robbery practised by the
powers that be. At our meetings it is usual for one of
the stewards to act as banker for the others, and back one
‘ fancy ’on behalf of the syndicate. The stewards’ fancy
wins for certain if it finishes within half a length of the
horse in front. The judge attends to that."
* * * »
Fleet Admiral’s owner made no mistake in
bringing the colt from Adelaide. In the December Stakes
at Randwick, Patriot was backed down to 6 to 4, and led
to the distance, where Fleet Admiral tackled and passed
him, winning by a length.

Despite Fort’s ignoble display in Summer Cup,
he will be very near winning Tatt.’s Cup to-day, his most
dangerous opponent being Response, who, at present time,
is very fit and well.
* * * *
The A. J.C. authorities should really study their
Leger and Flat patrons. A wretched array of numerals,
most irregularly kept as regards scratchings, is all that is
vouchsafed them re starters, &c. ’Tis imperative that a
board, on which is displayed not only starters’ numbers
but jockey’s names, be erected for the benefit of the two-
shilling and shilling racegoers.

The gods drop plums in the mouths of some
folk and then they are too lazy to munch them. Why
Pharamond’s owner did not start his horse in Christmas
Handicap is one of those things no fellow can understand
Nice handicapping for that race, too. Pharamond gave

Why was Hopscotch left in the Xmas Han-
dicap at Randwick till the last minute? Perhaps the

will explain. The poor punter has a lot to put up with

* * * *
Fleet Admiral so readily downed Patriot in
December stakes that he may fairly lay claim to being
considered the best two-year-old of his year. The Tola’s
performance was very creditable, and this youngster will
be returned a winner very shortly. As to Patriot, he is
Buch an in-and-out performer that ’tis charity to dub him
‘** * *
The public keep racing alive, and vet they are
ever being put in the hole. The very latest and most
flagrant take-down was with Cartridge, whose sportin'*- Cl
owner preferred trying to win the Summer Cup to giving
the public a run for Villiers Stakes, a race almost a cer
tainty for Muskets son. Of course, Cartridge failed in
Summer Cup, to the huge delight of the public, who had
planked their money on for the Villiers Stakes. This llth
hour scratching is reprehensible, and the sooner a rule is
passed by A.J.C. to the effect that, “if a horse be not
scratched before 12 o’clock the day before the date for
running, that horse be considered a starter, unless a valid
excuse for his not running be submitted to the stewards ”
the better it will be for racing generally.
* * * *
No tablet in a Melb. church is more elaborate
than one in a local livery stable. It is in memory of Old
Coppin, the most famous roadster ever known in that
City. Over 20 years since he died, at the age of 35,
memory is kept green by three verses on the
tablet.
* * * *
The secretary of a country racing-club badly
wanted to eject an objectionable person from the “pad-
dock,” but it was pointed out that, having a ticket the
man was legally there, and if violence were used an action
for assault might result. The secretary, however, was a
man of resource. Going up to the bugbear, he asked for
his ticket, and, on its production, deliberately tore it up
and said “ Now, you get out of this paddock ! Sergeant! ”
, calling to the local trap) “ this man has no ticket ; see
that he leaves at once. If he doesn’t, put him out 1 ”
and put out he was.
* * * ♦
Tasmania has been enjoying a novel variety of
turf scandal. A trainer sued another local “sport’’for
a balance of £lO, due upon a verbal agreement to run a
certain horse stiff. Plaintiff swore that defendant under-
took to pay “ materially increased” fees in the event of
the aforesaid dead bird lositui a particular race. The race
was lost as per contract, but all the stiffening money was
not paid ; hence the action. Mr. Commissioner Shaw ex-
pressed amazement at the effrontery of the trainer in
suing upon an agreement to defraud, and gave judgment
for the defending conspirator.
* * » *
Mr. J. Nixon is the champion brumby-shooter
in South Queensland. He and his mates have despatched
3492 head of wild horses in the Burnett district in 18
months. The wild horse is a tearful curse. He eats as
much as any bullock, and gives practically nothing in
return.
• * * .
At a pony-course, last week, an owner hrought
down all his hats to back his prad—a certainty. The
dollars were planked, but the pony, palpably pulled, ran
last. At a subsequent angry interview between owner
and jockey, the latter only cursed the former for “not
asking if he was on the joD before putting down his
bleeding beans.”
* * * *
The biggest horse ever known was exhibited in
London just 50 years ago. He was bred in U.S.A., and
named General Washington. From the ground to the top
of the withers he measured 20 hands lin. (6ft. 9in.).
* • . *
Grey horses are said to live longest, with roans
a good second. Blacks seldom pass 20 years, and a cream
over 15 is a phenomenon.
* * » *
The skeleton of Traducer—best of all Maori-
land stallions with the exception of Musket —stands in
Christchurch Museum.
» * * *
In a steeplechase at Deloraine, Tas., the other
day, six of the dozen starters fell, and three of them
broke their jockeys’ collar-bones thereby.
» * * *
A Collingwood (Melb.) tote-shop shows this
Elacard : —“ A man is specially engaged and kept in the
ack-yard to do all the shouting and swearing that is re-
quired in this establishment. A dog is also kept to do all
the barking. Our fighting-man, or chucker-out, has won
75 prize-fights. An undertaker calls here every day for
orders.”
* * * *
An examination of the photos, of the mile
finish of the Martin-Parsons match casts considerable
light on the reason why the Australian so frequently' loses
a closely-fought sprint. The top of Martin’s head is the
only part of him observable —just a round patch of hair
with the muscles of his jaw projecting on each side—but
Parsons—the handsome Jack—though his wheel is still
a yard from the line, is sitting almost bolt upright with a
saintly' smile at the nearest camera. Parsons might win
a heap more races if he had been born more* ugly, or if
the camera men were warned off the track, or if he broke
his pretty nose in a big smash.
* * * *
A truly impressi ve sight was Bilmartin’s theatre-
party' at Melb. last week, when the plugger invited his
pacing-teams and other prominent cyclists 40 strong to
the Melb. Opera House. Bill himself occupied a seat in
the centre of the theatre, and, with Parsons on his right
hand, formed a subject for a great historical picture. At
the inter.'al all hands adjourned to a neighboring squash-
shop, where Bill shouted ad lib. Coming into Bourke-
street, the “Rats’ push,” the local aggregation of bare-
footed urchins, mobbed the cyclist and clamored loudly
for a share of his ’winnings. Martin’s iron jaw relaxed,
an 1 he had evidently' come prepared, for he reached to
the pocket of his tail-coat, and scattered sundry handfuls
of coppers on the pavement. To the average Australian,
who has never had any nearer acquaintance with royalty
than an occasional glimpse of King Billy' or some equally
potent monarch, Bill Martin seemed distinctly suggestive
of Henry V. or any other mythical personage distribut-
ing largesse to the multitude.
One of the most pathetic sights of a recent big
cy'cling match down South was the way the prospective
ma-in-law of the defeated competitor burst into tears
when the other man came in. The object of his affection
was more philosophical, and contented herseif with sending
in condolatory messages to his dressing room about once
every minute and a-half. When a man is fairly knocked
out, and his naked body' is being pawed vigorously with
eucalyptus oil by horny-handed trainers, billets-doux
appear a trifle incongruous.

The Vio. Wheelmen’s League, in refusing to
sanction the match between Martin and Ken. Lewis unless
the League could manage the spec., has thoroughly ex-
posed the cheap and nasty character of its bike-racing
authority.

* * * »
-Tack Molloy, one of the most scientific middle-
weights who ever donned a glove, is dead. The writer
recollects one afternoon at Foley’s White Horse Hotel,
when old Sand y Ross happened to call in, and, fighting
his old fights with Foley o’er again, remarked that the
youngsters could not hit as in their days. Old Sandy put
the gloves on with Molloy, who crossed Sandy with the
light almost as soon as their hands were lip, and the sub-
sequent proceedings interested the old-timer no more.
Ex-pug Mick Nathan is the only Melbournite
who has ever made boxing-shows a lasting pay-game,
the reason is that all Michael's shows are run on the strict
straight, and, if any of Mick’s showmen attempt crook
business, Mick violently pounds them a bit “on his own,”
and thereafter the Victoria Hall ring knows them no
more.
* * * »
Bruiser John L. Sullivan’s day is done. His
hair is quite grey, his new little moustache is silverv, his
weight has climbed to 20st., his corporation is like a* bow
window', his face is bloated and sodden, and his chief oc-
cupation is beer-sinking. Bruiser to boozer, as ever.
• * * •
Jack Hall, perhaps the best light-weight Aus-
tralia ever saw', is back in Sydney, fat as a grampus. He had
a set-to with Tw’eedie the other night, but, lacking condi-
tion, did not show all his old-time cleverness. The fio-ht
of Hall’s life was with the Yankee, young Mitchell, at the
old White Horse Hotel, Sydney, when a draw was de-
clared well into the small hours.
* * * »
That most popular of pugs, Peter Jackson, is
not quite such a wreck as the par. inventors would make
out. Fitzsimmons says that when he last saw the big
fellow he was excellently groomed, and looked little
different to the Peter Jackson of eight years ago.
* * * *
Weiss practically admits his inferiority to Mem-
mott by barring the push stroke. As Coleridge might
have sung—“ He playeth best who pusheth best.” One
of these days a champion billiard artist will become in-
vincible at making losing hazards into the middle pockets.
It is possible for a man to score a 10,000 break that
way. Then the other fellows will bar “middle-pocket
losers ” as they now bar the comparatively easy “ spot.”
At present the highest development of billiard skill is in
the push stroke, and if Weiss could achieve excellence at
it he would.
* * * *
The Zeehan (Tas.) football ground during the
season is a sea of mud. The umpire is perfectly safe, as
after the first quarter he cannot be distinguished from the
players ; and it is usual for the friends of the combatants
to scrape them down during the intervals in order to find
out who is which.
* » * «
There is still some money in Victoria. At a
“ shilling-in, the winner shouts ” pool in a Gippsiand hotel
the other evening, after sundry ties and buying-in, there
was £62 in hard cash on the table. The winner, after
“shouting” for all and sundry, bought himself a bicycle
with the proceeds. There were only' 16 throwing.

From S.F. Call: —“Editor Call, —Will you
kindly state through the Call that I will back John
Hurey, the champion egg-eater of the San Francisco Dis-
trict Telegraph Co., against Ernest Miller, the so-called
champion of the Pacific Coast, in an egg-eating contest
for 2odol. a-side, or I will wager the above sum that he
can devour six dozen at one sitting. I can be found at
912 Market-street, also the money.—John M'Donald, for
John Hurey.

Nearly a thousand tickets taken in the Govt,
raffle for two allotments of land at Boomahnoomana, on
the Murray, each ticket representing at least £5 spent in
expenses over the job. And yet the law prohibits a plain
and simple flutter over a horse race.
**» * *
Re “cronk” dice. At Christchurch, M.L., the
police raided an oyster-saloon. Twenty men were ar-
rested in an upstairs room, and on the floor were found a
dice-box and 40 dice. With an average of two per man,
it looks as if dog eats dog in Maoriland.
* * * *
Eggs and Bacon were horse-dealers. Eggs was
honest and had money without the experience; Bacon
was a rogue (unlike the average horse-dealer) and had a
lot of experience along with a few “quid.” They went
into partnership. Bacon went out on the quiet and
bought twenty head of horses cheap ; put them in a pad-
dock in charge of a pal. Then Bacon tells Eggs that he
knows where there are some good horses to he bought.
Both go out to inspect. The pal sells the horses (Bacon’s)
to Eggs and Bacon for a big price. Eggs pays for them ;
then Bacon has a row with him. Eggs takes the horses
himself, and is unable to sell them at a profit. Bacon
makes “millions” out of the “deal.”
X.Z. : “ Old John Batman, when he first
himsdf on the site of Melbourne, 29th
Hay, 1830, at the junction of what is now North-
cote and the sandy waste which later became Col-
mgwood Hat, ceremonially re-christened the
Mern Creek, after his pet daughter, ‘Lucy’
wn •' , nalne > however, never caught on.
alking along its banks the other evening, I
bethought me that probably Lucy’s spirit, as it
floats on warm summer nights round that place of
many stinks, and beholds the sinuous string of
green, stagnant, smellful ponds adorned with
multitudinous phosphorescent upturned decaying
dogs, thanks Heaven fervently that her father’s
amiable effort to perpetuate her memory has failed
utter y, and that the Merri Creek is Merri Creek
still.
Algernon was new to the droving, and
ion the boss at last, after many misgivings, gave
bun a watch “on his own,” he was cautioned to
sing or whistle all the time to keep the cattle
quiet. He did sing for half-an-hour or so, till he
got sleepy ; then he half-slid, half-tumbled off his
borse, and snored where he lay. After a bit he
woke, with a confused sense of responsibility, and
ooked round for the cattle. Then he scratched
his head and went to rouse the boss. “Hey!
boss ! “ Yes,” said the drover sleepily. “ You
know that bullock with his tail half-mast ? ” The
diover nodded. “ Well, I can’t see him any-
where. That’s all right,” said the drover, “it’s
a dark night; he is in the mob somewhere ;we can
look m the morning.” Then the drover turned
over and went to sleep again ; Algernon con-
tinuing his round. He had not been away long,
when round he came again. “ What’s the matter
nc ?'T \. sa id the drover. “ You know that bullock
with his ear hung down ? ” “ What about him ? ”
growled the drover. “ I can’t see “ Damn
y°u . interrupted the drover, “ perhaps you
have lost the blooming lot ? ” “So I have,” said
Algernon, but I wanted to break it to you
gently. (The Bulletin gets this from a young
correspondent who hasn’t had much practice in
lying yet.)

-Item from list of tenders called for, in
S.A. Government Gazette: —
The contractor for funerals shall carry out same with a
hearse in a decent and respectable manner, and provide a
coffin with nameplate on same, to the satisfaction of the
board. The hearse, while engaged in conveying a body
to the grave, shall be driven at a walking pace.
Tenders to be enclosed in a sealed envelope, addressed
to the secretary of the Adelaide Hospital, “ On Public
Service,” and marked on the left-hand corner “ Tender for
groceries or provisions ” as the case may be.
Evidently the drought is rather bad in S.A. when
they are driven to regard the late lamented as
“groceries or provisions.”
Some derivations : “ Skillion,” Australian
term for a lean-to added to a building: from
“scalene,” obtuse-angled (mathematical term).
Vide Cbllins’ “ Accountof New South Wales,” vol.
ih, p. 312—“Enlarged by a scalene building run-
ning the whole length, the dwellings of the prin-
cipal surgeons,” &c. “Barracker,” a clamorous
parti zan of football team, &c. From baragouineur,,
a jabberer, one to talk gibberish. “Scorcher,”
recent slang for a fast cyclist. From ecorcheur,
one who takes the skin off, an excorihtor, a
skinner.
“The simplest hero I ever knew,” said an
old ex-sergeant of the 58th the other day, “ was in
our first Maori war. He and I were carrying grog
to the men in the trenches (Kawiti Pass). It was
dangerous work, and we had got about half way
when I heard him say ‘ Sergeant, I’m cooked.
Take the grog on, don’t mind me.’ On I had to
go. When I got back he was unconscious. We
found by the heavy, blood-trail that he had walked
about 100 yards before he spoke. Later on he sent
for me. He was dying and, as I bent over him,
he whispered : ‘ Say, did the boys get the grog
all right ? ’ I nodded. ‘ Did you tell ’em I didn’t
spill a drop ? ’ Again I nodded, and, smiling as if
he had won the V.'C., he died.”
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
The secrecy of the ballot in remote country
districts doesn’t amount to much. If, in every
electorate, all the ballot boxes were brought, un-
opened, to one common centre, and the papers
mixed together and then counted, the system
would answer well enough. When the dozen or
the few dozen votes given at some outlying polling
' place are counted in the presence of the two local
I squatters as scrutineers, it doesn’t answer at all.
The writer recalls one case where at a very small
polling place three votes were given to a demo-
cratic candidate —the local boss and scrutineer
being a Tory. The three voters were promptly
identified and sacked. One gave himself away by
using his own indelible pencil instead of the one
provided, and the fact that he habitually carried
an indelible pencil bowled him out. The other
two had been regarded with suspicion all along,
and were watched as they put their papers in the
box. The peculiar method of folding gave one
away, and a grimy thumb-mark on the paper
bowled out the other. A lot can be done by
judiciously changing the official pencil; if it is
first blue, then black, then red, and careful note is
made of the voters who use each kind, the field of
research is considerably narrowed down. In a
very small polling place a really smart scrutineer
who knows everybody around can identify almost
every individual vote and act accordingly.

MELBOURNE-
The G-ulf ant-hills would really surprise a
Southerner. They are so thick in certain dis-
tricts that the country almost resembles a large
graveyard. Some of them are of great height—
nine and ten feet being nothing uncommon, and
they are all built in the one direction —due North
and South. This is attributed by many to the
desire of the ants to secure from the sun’s rays the
maximum of dessication. The hills are extremely
hard, being cemented by a glutinous secretion
exuded by the insects themselves. Building and
repairing are only carried on in the wet season.
The model informer—Bismarck.
Quoth quaintly an old Latin medical poem
found in MS. the other day in the National
Library, Paris “ To the patient you promise
cure, but immediately on leaving the room you
say to the relatives that the disease is grave. J lie
event will be that, if you cure him, your merit is
greater and you will receive the greater praise
and fee; while, if he dies, they will say that you
had no hope from the first! ” How keenly modern
in their ways were those belittled ancients!

AN INTERESTING NOTICE OF A MOST

MARVELLOUS TESTIMONIALS.
FLATTERING PRESS OPINIONS.
- - - MIRACULOS CURES.
A few words as to the progress and growth in
Sublic favour of the Registered Alfaline Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W. A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my place on the Rose River, one was taken

I gave him a few of your Pills. I am pleased to inform
you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found
him up and pegging-out his skins and looking well. This
speaks volumes for your valuable Remedies.”

perature sometimes rises to 173deg. Altogether
100 miles of bore are down, at a cost of about £900
a mile. One bore out of every four is a failure.
On Boatman station there are three bores, pro-
ducing ten million gals, daily. Think of it!
Enough to supply a town of 200,000 persons with
60 gallons apiece daily.

Despite the “ borak ” poked by Bulletin
scribes, there is a grain or two of truth in the
frog reservoir legend. Thus the Larapintan ex-
plorers : “ As soon as the rain comes the frog
imbibes freely. The water permeates to all parts
of the body until it is swollen up like a cricket-
ball ; then it buries itself deep in the mud ; is
baked in; and lies moist and cool until another
wet season softens its environment.” This on the
most eminent scientific authority in Australia.
It was in a galvanised-iron bush church,
Temperature, 90deg. in the water-bag; ratio of
flies to seekers of the truth, 1000 to 1. The ser-
mon, “ Do unto others as you would have others
do unto you,” had just run dry. Whereupon up
rose six feet of bone and sweat, which wanted to
know why the &c. the roof at the parson’s end of
the show was painted with refrigerating paint,
and the rest wasn’t. And the parson looked
above, pulled a fly from his eye, and, with a sorry
smile, replied: “Ah ! my dear brother, that I
might be the better preserved to preaoh unto my
flook the glorious Terewth.”
Dear B.—-Up West the warm weather is
setting in, and the snakes are beginning to show
out. Last week my girl and two others went for
a walk and met one. Now, the Western creed
is—kill your snake or corner him till someone
comes along to do it. There were no sticks about,
and, not wanting him to continue a live snake,
they chased him ahead for about three miles, till
a lame swaggy came up and dropped heavy on
him. We’ve got the skin hanging up in the bar
of the local hotel, next the whisky bottle. If I
were at all given to exaggeration, I would say they
chased him seven miles, but I scorn deception.
—Brand Er
“Cancer houses” are now a recognised
actuality. Dr. D’Arcy Power tells how a house-
keeper whp.had lived in a certain room of a par-
ticular house died of cancer of the stomach. A
year later the succeeding housekeeper who also
slept in that room died of cancer of the liver.
Eight years later her successor in the room died
of cancer of the breast and womb. These three
persons were unrelated by blood, and were appar-
ently in perfect health till they inhabited that
room. Dr. Blyth tells that three successive
tenants of one London house died of cancer, that
a frequent visitor of the last of these three also
died of cancer of the breast and lung, and that
this visitor’s niece, who slept with her, was also
operated on for cancer at 14 years of age. A
similar case to Power’s occurred in a Glasgow
house, where three successive night watchmen,
previously unrelated, and with no can-
cerous family history, died of cancer one after the
other. i

“In the case of men and emancipated
hair-cropped women we contravene Nature’s law,
and Nature’s revenge is baldness. If the eman-
cipated woman can be made to understand that
wearing the hair short brings with it the danger
of baldness, she will have to be very thoroughly
emancipated, or she will give up this method of
emphasising her masculinity.” — Birmingham
Medical Review. :

Freedom: A Fable for Democrats.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Came a folk to proud Olympus, in the peer-
less days of old,
Crying, “Zeus, Great Zeus—O hear us!
From thy banquet-hall of gold
L aid thy peasants ear a moment, for their
plaint is loud and long
Of the grievous slights they suffer from the
iron hand of Wrong,
Of the hard hearts of the Mighty and the
grinding of the Strong.”
Then Zeus answered : “O, my people—came
your asses late to me
Crying, ‘ Zeus, Great Zeus—O hear us !
From the peasants set us free ;
For the loads they give are grievous and our
plaint is loud and long
Of our Freedom reft and rended by the iron
hand of Wrong,
Of the hard hearts of the Mighty, and the
grinding of the Strong. ’
“ Therefore spake I gently : ‘ Asses—Free-
dom suits not such as ye,
For ye love at heart a master, though a
despot oft he be ;
Yet in mercy this I grant you—Race of asses
evil-starred : .
From this day your hides, my creatures,
dented deep and cudgel-scarred,
Shall grow callous and unfeeling when the
blows are falling hard.’
“And your asses went rejoicing. Be ye
wise, my folk, as they ;
If ye will not fight to free you—see ye
toughen day by day ;
Teach your hides to thicken. Know ye—
from your cradles to your graves,
If ye did not crepp as cravens there would
stalk no despot knaves,
If ye were but Jit for Freedom never Man
shoidd make you slaves ! ”

P. LUFTIG.
At Wellington (M.L.), the other day, 14
ladies arrived in a group at the Registrar’s office
to look up the records and find out if the woman
next door was really married as she claimed to be,
that alleged bride being somehow viewed with
suspicion. A careful search showed that there
was no such marriage on the records, and the 14
went away looking happy, to cut the suspected
lady dead and otherwise make things hot for her.
And now the Registrar is waiting to hear of a
horrid butchery with an axe when the woman
next door hears of the kindly interest taken in her
character.
“And wretches hang that jurymen may
dine! ” will soon be once more the truth if the
gto<m-coercion of juries by Judges isn’t more
energetically resented by the juries themselves.
At Orange, the other dav, that little tin Win-
deyer, Judge Docker, couldn’t understand how a
jury “failed to agree in such a clear case.” But
no such expression of private opinion should have
had any influence upon men whose sworn duty it
was to judge with their own judgments, and not
with Mr. Docker’s—even in face of the calamitous
circumstance that Mr. D. “had intended to go
away that night, and now could not do so.” The
jury would, therefore, be locked-up till 7 next
morning (the newspaper report continues) and—-
“he could not order them any breakfast.”' N.S.
Wales has got rid of Judge Windeyer. It would
be well for it now to get rid of Judge Docker.
A fine point of law. A Melbourne man
left his wife in Victoria, and went to N.S.W.,
allegedly in search of work. He didn’t come back,
nor send for his wife, and after a while he van-
ished altogether. The lady applied for a divorce
on the ground of desertion, and Judge Hood
argued the matter out thus : If the old man
intended desertion when he left his wife in Vic-
toria, then the desertion occurred there and the
Court had jurisdiction. If he meant to come
back, but afterwards, while in New South Wales,
decided that he wouldn’t, then the desertion
occurred in New South Wales, and the Melbourne
Court couldn’t interfere. If he was half-inclined
to desert his wife while in Victoria, and arrived
at the other half of the resolution in New South
Wales, then the situation was mixed. There was
no evidence either way, but Judge Hood decided
that he would naturally intend at first to come
back, but drifted gradually into the habit of stay-
ing away, and refused the decree on the ground
that he had no jurisdiction. This may be good
law, but if it is meant as a regular thing it is very
poor common sense. Quite probably the old man
never came to any resolution, but simply post-
poned his return because his pants couldn’t bear
inspection, and kept on postponing it owing to
the continued deterioration of his pants, and
didn’t write because the ink-bottle was dried up.
Most people simply meander, and never quite
resolve to do anything.
Dodging the Inspector .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Saunders and Harrison were teachers in
charge of backblocks schools some 10 miles apart.
JNaturaHy they fraternised, sympathised with each
o hers grievances against “the Department,” and
ipped the mutual wink when the Inspector was
about. Sometimes he visited one school first;
sometimes the other.
One day, however, Saunders was caught nap-
ping. the Inspector wasn’t due for three months
when he dropped suddenly from the clouds. A
new one, too—worse luck ; with fresh peculiarities
to study, and fresh prejudices to wheedle.
He opened fire at once. 1 ‘ Good morning, Mr.
feaunders. Don’t you think it is time your school
was assembled at 10.15 ? ”
Saunders looked uneasy; but led the wav into
the school-house and the examination started.
In vain Saunders looked for some traveller to
convey a message to Harrison. The day wore on,
and the examination was completed. The in-
spector departed, and made for Harrison’s school
by a road which also led to the township of Mary-
burg, wliither he informed Saunders he was going.
Still no message was sent to Harrison. But
next morning a bullock-team passed by Saunders’s,
and bullocky” was entrusted with “ the
message.” An hour later, a drayman passed ;
and, as he was likely to overtake the “ bullocky,”
he was given the message also. Then a horseman,
coming along a short time after, was told to ride
briskly and inform Mr. Harrison that the In-
spector was about.
* * « *
The scene changes to Harrison’s school,
with the examination going on nicely. Suddenly
the noise of a great shouting is heard outside.
a arr i son » the Inspector has been up at
old Saunders’s, and he says he will be down here
to-day.”
“What’s that, Mr. Harrison ?” asked the in-
spector. Harrison hadn’t heard anything, and
the examination proceeded.
An hour later there was a frightful yell outside
the school-house :
Harrison ! Harrison ! The Inspector is up at
Saunders’s school, and he says you’re to look out
for the old devil. ”
Harrison went to the door and returned looking
uncomfortable; and again the work began, and
was proceeding quietly when a third voice, louder
than either of the preceding, interrupted the
inspector. ;
“Stand over, Bloomer, you ——■ ! Gee,
ftoaney ! Boh, Dandy ! Stand oyer, Bloomer,
you ——— ; I’ll kill you, you cowl We-e-ee ! ”
The inspector looked at Harrison curiously, and
there was a short silence.
Then : “Mr. Harrison, the blanky old inspector
has been at Saunders’s yesterday, and the old
blank may be down here soon. ”
“ All right, my man; all right; lam here,”
said the inspector, coming to the verandah.
Then; “Mr. Harrison, are you troubled like
this every day? It is disgraceful,” said the
inspector.
Harrison reigned only three weeks longer in the
service.
On the whole, Saunders “ preferred to resign.”

H.O.G.
The efforts of Australian Parliaments to
define the hours of labor and construct scales of
wages are, so far from being a new legislative
departure, a very old, almost ancient one. Such
attempts are commonly denounced by politicians
as un-British, un-English, Socialistic, and so forth,
but in reality they are more English than any
other of the prominent movements of the time.
The “ Statute of Laborers,” passed by the British
Parliament, dates from the year 1496, and is in
tenor a factory, workshops and field-workers’ Act.
In it the hours of work are strictly defined and the
wages to be paid determined. That the hours
were longer than now and the wages less does not
affect the point. The historical fact is that the
legislation now called novel in spirit is actually
almost as old as our civilisation.
“Gates closed” is the brief inscription
facing would-be visitors to Sydney Hospital on
visiting-days, the authorities not taking the trouble
to add any intimation that the visitors’ entrance
has been shifted from the front in Macquarie-st.
to the rear in the Domain. This, of itself, means
a long detour, but many persons wait on in
the belief that the gates will be opened directly.
“Why was the visitors’ entrance shifted?”
“Well, sir, you see, it’s the quality opposite.”
“The what ? ” “The quality. The bigwigs that
live in those houses across the road. They made
complaints that they didn’t ought to be sickened
with the sight of all sorts of persons of all classes
flocking to the hospital on visiting-days to see
their sick friends. It offended their eyes.” “Per-
haps, too, that’s the reason why the building of
this new wing was blocked for so many years ? ”

A proposition drafted for the forthcoming
Lambeth Conference is to the effect that all
bishops of colonial provinces shall take an oath of
allegiance to the Archbishop of Canterbury,
Several Australian bishops are stoutly opposed to
the innovation, believing that it tends to a Papacy
of Canterbury, and are determined to make their
opposition heard. . .
Tobacco-smoke largely prevents infectious
disease. For one man-smoker who contracts
diphtheria three non-smokers are attacked. For
practical comparison, women are non-smokers and
men are smokers. Compared by sexes, we again
find three non-smoking (women) diphtheritics for
one smoker (man). Prof. Hajek, of Vienna,
deduces this from the health records of three sue-
cessive years of the Austrian capital.
Habitat: “A rule, stuck up all over
Melb. Public Library, is that uo volume, under
any circumstances, is to be removed. Going, only
yesterday, for the new vols. of a periodica), I find
them all wolfed away by some privileged leader-
writer.”
j n countries where opium-smoking is cus-
tomary, it is not uncommon (says Annals of
Veterinary Medicine, France) to see cats, dogs
and monkeys that are much in the presence of
men acquire a taste for opium, in consequence of
inhaling the fumes given out by smokers. Which
statement can be confirmed by numerous Australian
cas,es, where pet native-bears have acquired the
habit of inhaling tobacco-smoke from their masters,
chewing up tobacco left lying around, and even
eating clay or wooden pipes left about the house.
