Branna Satburg.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Branna Satburg was scraping gum in a sod
whare, by Auckland harbor—a big woman, with
pale yellow hair : a Swede, from the shores of
Bothnia.
She sat on an old soap-box, and held a long
manuka stick against her left shoulder. With her
left. hand she held lumps of earth'-encrusted gum
against the stick, and scraped off rust and dirt with
swift, drawing cuts of a sharp knife. The brittle
amber-like substance powdered under her strokes,
and this powder filled the air with the scent of
pines—the ghostly perfume of kauris that had
perished a thousand years ago, hidden in the fossil
resin her husband had dug from the ground.
The candle that lit the windowless whare smelt
like a pineknot. The burning logs on the hearth
were the hearts of dead kauris, dragged from a
neighboring swamp. The wind driving against
the raupo thatch sang the song of pines. The
semi-darkness was the gloom of a pine forest.
Scent of pines, sound of pines, aye, and sight of
pines, for the woman, in fancy, was walking in the
pine forests that slope to the Kalix, and she saw'
the golden moss and the green on the serried
tracks. She trod on a springy carpet of pine-
needles. She saw Carl with the light of the mid-
night sun on his face coming to meet her.
A wave of passion and desire swept into the
woman’s mind. She sprang to her feet; she must
tread on the path of pine needles, feel the spirit
of the forest. She flung open the door and saw
long smooth slopes of dark manuka scrub, stretches
of tidal mud and salt water; and there was her
lord and master toiling up the path with a pikau
of gum on his shoulders, and his polished spade
gleaming red with the rays of a stormy sunset.
A hard smile crossed the woman’s face as she saw
the man sway in his tracks. She clutched the
great knife ; looked beyond him, above him, to the
dark hill-ranges sloping up to the roof of massive
clouds. There were forests boundless and
measureless on those ranges. Pines in countless
millions breathing the spirit of youth.
The man staggered to the door, threw his pikau
on the ground, hung his spade beneath the pro-
jecting thatch and stood before her—a sullen,
burly, ill-tempered fellow, sodden with drink.
“You hag,” he said, “why do you stare with
your green eye at me ? Out of my way ! ”
He strode into the hut and looked around.
“Devil,” he shouted, “you have not worked:
there is no food for me. Bah ! fool! Woman,
was it for this that I took you from Karl Abbstein?
You were not worth a lie. I told him you were
false and he believed, the fool! the fool! ”
“ Fool yourself! ” screamed the woman in
sudden fury, and she slashed the knife she held
across the man’s face and left a red, gaping, hor-
rible wound. He sank to the floor with the blood
pouring on to his chest, and she fled out of the
whare : out with the wind towards the forests on
the ranges. The wind drove lines of rain against
Jter; whipped her forward. She rose up the bare
spur; turned once, and saw spread far below her
the smooth, green undulations of the manuka
country ; she saw, or fancied she saw, that whare
by the tidal creek ; the man with a red wound ;
then she entered the forest, and darkness.
There was no springy needle carpet to walk on.
Wet moss, sticky mud. She pushed upward
through the dripping undergrowth. Upward, for
it was on the high ridges that the pine-trees grew.
She lost her slippers in the mire; lost some of
her dress against the bushes ; her great ..pile of
yellow hair tumbled over her shoulders, and there
was only blackness before her. She walked on
and on, striving: blindly to reach the pine forests,
to breathe their perfume, feel their sheltering
arms.
All was darkness, the rain fell in sheets, and
now before and around her rose barriers of vegeta-
tion, rata vines, thorny lawyers, wharangi and
supplejack. She was imprisoned, but she fought
with Northern fury and won freedom. There
before her was a light—a quaint, high-roofed
Bothnia house. She pressed the wooden latch,
opened the door, and stood in the full light of a
great log fire. A man rose slowly, grasped his
bright felling axe : saw the woman : saw the
yellow hair falling loose, the white face, the big
blue eyes. The axe slid to the floor. “Branna,”
he muttered, softly, “a dream ! ”
“ Karl! ” she shrieked ; “ Karl! Touch me—
clasp me—l am real—l am not false ! ”
F. ROLLIT.
When the Shed Cut Out.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Burhunga cut out the first week in Sep-
tember, Wild, wiry-woolled brutes most of the
sheep were, plentifully daubed with dags, and
burry. The classer smoked and swore, the boss
shook out the sand and grunted, and we who
were on the shearing board never earned our
money harder.
Friday was pay-day. As we hung about “Go-
vernment House,” awaiting our cheques, the
hawkers got to work. First there came an Afghan
—or something colored, anyway. Then “ The
Chump, ” who had bottomed the tally most days,
had his “pitch.” It was his avowed intention to
give that sanguinary nigger “ partikler hell ” ; and
he did it. We had at last learned “ The Chump’s”
strong point: it wasn’t cooking, for we’d run him
at that, and it wasn’t shearing, and it wasn’t
yarning. It was intense patriotism. “ Australia
for the Australians ” was his motto.
From Afghans to Chinamen was a short jump.
“ Don’t ‘ The Banjo ’ say he saw the Chows shear-
ing—yes, not cooking, shearing ? Listen :
I looked along the shearin’ floor before I turned to go—
There were eight or ten dashed Chinamen a-shearin’ in
a row.
And it’ll be those other yellow whelps there next
—eh ? Yes, encourage them to hang round shear-
ing sheds, do.”
And so on. Abdul Khan was given ten minutes
to “get.” He sadly packed his wares, shook up
his reins, and, cursing, “got.”
Abdul Khan, who had camped just outside the
home paddock over-night and so had got in early,
was succeeded by the man from the township
stores who came with his hawking cart. He, too,
spread out his goods around him, and, squatting
down, began to bargain. Again “ The Chump ”
harangued the group. But this time he varied
the argument. He pictured the sweating dens of
the cities.
“ That coat in his hand now—yes, buy it. A
dozen of ’em were finished for a blanky Jew by a
starving Christian girl (the sister of one of us
blokes, for all I know) for 15d.— yes, 15d. a dozen.
Fourteen hours work for 15d. Garn, buy it—and
encourage that sort of thing, too, and bring
another poor devil of a girl to the gutter that
feeds hell. . . . Them boots—Oh, yes, I know
>em, and some of the poor starved wretches that
made ’em. One’s a brother of mine, if he isn t
dead by now. I’d crawl on my splintered bones to
a decent shop where a man gets what he earns
rather than tramp out a pair of them and a man s
soul at the same time.”
There was fire in the little man. The knot
around the huckster got smaller, but the social
and moral reformer stuck to his post; and there
wasn’t a man who mortgaged his cheque.
Then a hawking-boat pulled in and moored to a
tree. But the owner was a Jew, and the super-
cargo had a Jewish name, which he had done his
little best to corrupt —he was Cohen ai d had called
himself Colquhoun —and so “The Chi mp’s ” work
was easy.
* * * *
The office was open, and the cheques were
ready.
“ Thompson,” said the boss, “ I don’t, of course,
approve of agitators; yet I think the men ought
to be grateful to you for urging them to keep their
cheques unbroken until they get to their homes.
I am sorry your own is not larger.”
• * * *
There are two routes from Burhunga to the
railway centre. One is by way of the township,
which is a God-forsaken splotch on a box-fringed
wilderness of sand, consisting of two pubs., a
butcher’s shop, a saddler’s, and half-a-dozen
private residences built mostly of rough-sawn
timber covered with thatch. The other route is
over a billabong, and available only in this droughty
weather.
Three days before the cut-out we had discussed
how we should go. The regular coach people
would take us in for £2 ss. a head. The repre-
sentative of the hawking-boat would take us
down to the township for nothing, and from there
we could get in for thirty shillings. The night
before the cut-out the regular’s agent, having
meantime been offered a subsidy by the two pub-
keepers, said he would carry us right through for
thirty shillings.
“ Oh, well, if it comes to cutting,” chipped in
Rawlins, who ran the Victoria on the Billabong,
and had dropped in quite promiscuously on the
way to the township—“ if they can do it at thirty
bob, so can I; and if you do miss the township
you’ll swallow a pint the less sand and get to town
the sooner. ”
From thirty, the offer went down on the other’s
part to twenty-five; and Rawlins topped it with
“a quid.”
At that Rawlins got the job. No “Chump”
was conveniently on hand to vehemently protest
against the immorality of the competition.
♦ * * *
Rawlins and his pub. are not troubled by
neighbors. The nearest, a boundary rider, is at
the Sixteen Mile, three miles back. At the pub.
Rawlins collected the fares and “shouted.”
Then they had some feed.
The change of horses was made smartly. There
were no suspicious circumstances about Rawlins,
“ The Chump ” observed, pointing to the team : he
didn’t want to take them down. So they drank
again. And again. A casual or two dropped in.
“ The Chump ” suspected one of being “ a scab.”
Triumphantly producing his union ticket, there
was, of necessity, another drink, “ The Chump ”
shouting.
“Yes, why not? A little scratch sports meet-
ing—quoits, and a flat and a hurdle-race, and a
jump.”
“ Sweepstakes, of course,” said “the Chump,”
who had reluctantly taken a hand—“with
the ‘Victoria Handicap’ to follow—yours, eh,
boss ? A couple of quid, shall we say ? ’
Rawlins was agreeable. .
Soon Rawlins’ “ wife’s cousin ” and the girl
from the bar ” had to go out and hold the tape—
“ just for the big go, the Handicap. ”
By this time the running was not absolutely
straight, and there were a couple of falls. There
wag a dispute, of course; but the demure “missus’s
cousin ” was the fair peacemaker. They all went
and had a drink over the fact that there hadn t
been any unpleasantness.
The horses had been taken out again.
“A sing-song? Oh, yes; if you like. And
perhaps Nellie—that’s thewnissus’s cousin, y’know
—will play the piano, and sing. She can, take
my word for it; but she’s a bit shy, and may take
some coaxing.”
Nellie was coaxed accordingly; and then be-
witched us. Three separate fights ensued ; round
six separate pairs of shoulders did Nellie throw
her arms and beg the dear fellow not to quarrel;
and six separate men pledged themselves not to
do it, just for her sake —and then were turned
over to Maggie, “ the girl in the bar,” who mean-
time made such conquests as she could.
“ I wish they’d get dead-drunk, if they must,”
she whispered to each—“all, I mean, but you;
and then—and then I wouldn’t be so busy, and—
and —”
She spoke the rest with her eyes, and each one
thought he understood. Whereupon in ecstacy
he drank, and she sipped, and so he held the
illicit contract sealed.
“I’m afraid they’re going to keep it up all
night,” she said. “Now, go in, do, with the
rest, and don’t hang around here, or there’ll be
suspicion, and then ”
He thought he understood —and went.
* * * *
We took a vote the next day, somewhere
after noon, as to whether we should move on.
But Nellie and Maggie looked out, and—well, we
didn’t go.
* * * *
Two of us threw our swags over our
shoulders that night and sneaked away. I met
one of the most-smitten of Nellie’s victims a
fortnight afterwards in town.
“ Hullo ! Is Rawlins’s coach in ? ”
“ Damn Rawlins ! ”
“ But when d’ye get here?”
“ Tramped in yesterday. Rawlins never drove
an inch past his shanty. There was always a
majority against him starting. Those women
‘ had ’ us. And the little devil Thompson,
‘The Chump,’ why, the missus’s cousin—Nellie,
you know —she’s ‘ The Chump’s ’ wife. And ‘ The
Chump ’ is Rawlins’s buttoner, and his wife’s.
And, by thunder, she is fly.”
